	Then, similar to \cref{cha:origin}, we have that $D-Î”<Î¸$ and $R=1-ğ˜—\{T(ğ˜_n)<Î¸\}$.
	To rephrase it, the trade-off between $ï¼°$ and $R$ for lossy compression is
	\[ğ˜—\{T(ğ˜_n)<D-Î”\}â‰ˆ1-R.\]
	Let $\{ğ˜›_n\}$ be the total variation process defined by $ğ˜›_nâ‰”T(ğ˜_n)$ .
	
	The last paragraph is not the only driving force
	for learning the total variation process $\{ğ˜›_n\}$;
	channel coding over asymmetric channels enjoys a similar reduction.
	
	Recall that $Q(x)$, presumably non-uniform,
	is the capacity-achieving input distribution w.r.t.\ an asymmetric $W$.
	Pretend that $Q(x,â™£)$ models a channel with a constant output $â™£$.
	Then we can polarize $Q$ and talk about its descendants.
	Details omitted, the block error probability of polar coding over $W$ is \cite{HY13}
	\[ï¼°â‰¤âˆ‘_{jâ‚^nâˆˆğ’¥}Z\(\(\dotsb((Wï¼·{jâ‚})ï¼·{jâ‚‚})\dotsb\)ï¼·{j_n}\)
		+T\(\(\dotsb((Qï¼·{jâ‚})ï¼·{jâ‚‚})\dotsb\)ï¼·{j_n}\).\label{ine:P<sumsum}\]
	We know how to bound the sum of $Z$'s, so it remains to bound the sum of $T$'s.
	
	All in all, we now want to show
	\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<e^{-2^{Ï€n}}ï½>H(W)-2^{-Ïn}\label{ine:T-e2pin}\]
	for $(Ï€,Ï)$ lying in the same region as in \cref{cha:origin}.
	The following section argues that we can reuse the same the proof.

\section{Stochastic Process Nonsense}

	In this section, I will show that since the total variation process
	$\{ğ˜›_n\}$ satisfies almost all properties satisfied by $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$,
	the majority of the proof of \cref{ine:Z-e2pin} applies to \cref{ine:T-e2pin}.
	
	Let us start with an counterpart to \cref{lem:squares}.
	
	\begin{lem}[Evolution of $T$]
		\cite[Lemma~3]{Muramatsu21}
		The following hold for all SBDMCs $W$:
		\begin{gather*}
			T(Wï¼·1)=T(W)Â²,			\label{ine:T^2}\\
			T(Wï¼·2)â‰¤2T(W)-T(W).	\label{ine:2T-T^2}
		\end{gather*}
	\end{lem}
	
	This implies a nice consequence---that there is another supermartingale
	that will dominate the behavior of $ğ˜_n$ at the noisy end.
	
	\begin{lem}
		The process of total variation norms, $\{ğ˜›_n\}$, is a supermartingale.
	\end{lem}
	
	Note that we do not have a counterpart to \cref{ine:2-Z^2}.
	This will affect how we deal with the en23 behavior of $\{ğ˜›_n\}$.
	The counterpart of \cref{lem:ZvsH} follows.
	
	\begin{lem}[$T$ vs $H$]\label{lem:TvsH}
		\cite[Lemma~4]{Muramatsu21}
		The following holds for all SBDMCs $W$:
		\[1-T(W)â‰¤H(W)â‰¤hâ‚‚ï¼ˆÃ·{1-T(W)}2ï¼‰\]
		where $hâ‚‚$ is the binary entropy function.
	\end{lem}
	
	See \cref{fig:TvsH} for a visualization.
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=6]{
			\draw
				(0,1)pic{y-tick=$1$}(0,0)pic{y-tick=$0$}
				(0,0)pic{x-tick=$0$}(1,0)pic{x-tick=$1$};
			\draw[fill=gray]
				plot[domain=0:45,samples=180]({h2o(\x)},{cos(2*\x)})--cycle;
			\draw[->](0,0)--(0,1.1)node[left]{$T$};
			\draw[->](0,0)--(1.1,0)node[right]{$H$};
		}
		\caption{
			The possible region where $(T(W),Z(W))$ could lie in.
			The curve part is half of $hâ‚‚$ after rotating and rescaling.
		}\label{fig:TvsH}
	\end{figure}
	
	The counterpart to \cref{lem:Z-en23} follows.
	Note that we skipped \cref{thm:bdmc-mu}, but the following proof
	shows that we need not duplicate \cref{thm:bdmc-mu}.
	
	\begin{lem}[From eigen to en23]\label{lem:T-en23}
		Fix $Ï±â‰”1/4.714$.
		Assume \cref{thm:bdmc-mu}.
		Then
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<e^{-n^{2/3}}ï½>H(W)-2^{-Ï±n+o(n)}.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		By \cref{lem:ZvsH,lem:TvsH}, $T(W)â†’0$ iff $H(W)â†’1$ iff $Z(W)â†’1$ on the noisy end
		and $T(W)â†’1$ iff $H(W)â†’0$ iff $Z(W)â†’0$ on the reliable end.
		More strongly, each â€œiff\kern.1emâ€ can be stated as
		two HÃ¶lder conditions in two directions.
		That is to say, there exist constants $c,d>0$ such that
		$T(W)â‰¤c(1-H(W))^d$ and $1-H(W)â‰¤cT(W)^d$, as well as
		$1-H(W)â‰¤c(1-Z(W))^d$ and $1-Z(W)â‰¤c(1-H(W))^d$,
		as well as the other four inequalities on the reliable end.
		
		This common-fate property implies that, if $n$ is sufficiently large,
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			ğ˜—ï½›e^{-n^{2/3}}â‰¤ğ˜›_nâ‰¤1-e^{-n^{2/3}}ï½<ğ˜—ï½›e^{-n^{3/4}}â‰¤ğ˜¡_nâ‰¤1-e^{-n^{3/4}}ï½	\\
			&	â‰¤Ã·{ğ˜Œ[h(ğ˜¡_n)]}{h(\exp(-n^{3/4}))}
				â‰¤Ã·{h(ğ˜¡â‚€)2^{-Ï±}}{\exp(-n^{3/4})}<2^{-Ï±n-o(n)}.
		\end{align*}
		This is the counterpart to \cref{ine:bdmc-expel}.
		
		It remains to show that $ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_nâ†’0\}=H(W)$ and that
		the bad event $ğ˜‰_nâ‰”\{ğ˜›_nâ†’0$ but $ğ˜›_nâ‰¥\exp(-n^{2/3})\}$
		is exponentially rare, i.e., $2^{-Ï±(n)+o(n)}$-rare.
		The former is again by the common-fate property
		$ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_nâ†’0\}=ğ˜—\{ğ˜_nâ†’1\}=ğ˜Œ[ğ˜_âˆ]=ğ˜Œ[ğ˜â‚€]=H(W)$.
		The latter is by that $\{ğ˜›_n\}$, a supermartingale, cannot jump back and forth
		between the neighborhood of $0$ and the neighborhood of $1$,
		so $ğ˜—(ğ˜‰_n)$ is bounded from above by $âˆ‘_{mâ‰¥n}2^{-Ï±m+o(m)}<2^{-Ï±n+o(n)}$.
		
		We can finally conclude that
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<e^{-n^{2/3}}ï½>ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_nâ†’0\}-ğ˜—(ğ˜‰_n)>H(W)-2^{-Ï±n+o(n)}.\]
		This calls the end of the proof.
	\end{proof}
	
	The last lemma is the most technical one in this chapter.
	It uses the common-fate property to show that not only do
	$\{ğ˜¡_n\}$, $\{ğ˜_n\}$, and $\{ğ˜›_n\}$ control each other's limit,
	but they also control each other's pace of convergence.
	In particular, we can also show that $ğ˜—\{ğ˜_n<\exp(-n^{2/3})\}>I(W)-2^{-Ï±n+o(n)}$ and
	that $ğ˜—\{1-ğ˜_n<\exp(-n^{2/3})\}>H(W)-2^{-Ï±n+o(n)}$ although it is not useful here.
	
	The een13 behavior follows.
	
	\begin{lem}[From en23 to een13]\label{lem:T-een13}
		Given \cref{lem:T-en23}, that is, given
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<e^{-n^{2/3}}ï½>H(W)-2^{-Ï±n+o(n)},\]
		we have
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)ï½>H(W)-2^{-Ï±n+o(n)}.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		The proof is just a copy of that of \cref{lem:Z-een13}.
		All we did there was by that $ğ˜¡_n$ is squared or doubled with equal probability.
		Since $ğ˜›_n$ is squared or doubled with equal chance, the conclusion follows.
	\end{proof}
	
	The ultimate behavior follows.
	
	\begin{thm}[From een13 to e2pin]\label{thm:T-e2pin}
		Fix a pair $(Ï€,Ï)âˆˆğ’ª$.
		Given the conclusion of \cref{lem:T-een13}, that is, given
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)ï½>H(W)-2^{-Ï±n+o(n)},\]
		then
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<e^{-2^{Ï€n}}ï½>H(W)-2^{-Ïn+o(n)}.\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		The proof follows the same type of argument as in \cref{thm:Z-e2pin}.
		All we need is based on the fact/axiom that
		the process is squared or doubled with equal chance.
	\end{proof}

\section{Chapter Wrap up}

	In this chapter, we first see two coding problems---source coding for
	lossy compression and noisy channel coding over asymmetric channels.
	I then argued that polar coding applies to those scenarios
	with an extension to asymmetric (test) channels.
	To deal with asymmetric channels, we prove a series of lemmas whose takeaway is that
	we only have to consider the SBDMC $ËœW$ that lies in the same equivalence class.
	To characterize the performance of polar coding,
	it remains to understand the behavior of $\{ğ˜›_n\}$.
	And then we move on to proving the behavior of $\{ğ˜›_n\}$
	using the same techniques we used to handle $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$.
	
	Now that we have proved
	$ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_n<\exp(-2^{Ï€n})\}>I(W)-2^{-Ïn+o(n)}$ (from the previous chapter)
	and $ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_n<\exp(-2^{Ï€n})\}>H(W)-2^{-Ïn+o(n)}$ (in this chapter),
	plug them into \cref{ine:D<sum,ine:P<sumsum}.
	For lossy compression, my result implies that lossy compression via polar coding
	enjoys code rate $I(W)+2^{-Ïn+o(n)}$ and distortion $Î”+\exp(-2^{Ï€n})$.
	Eliminate the $o(n)$ term with a topological argument.
	
	\begin{cor}[good code for lossy compression]
		For any lossy compression problem whose test channel $W$ is a BDMC,
		using polar coding yields excess of distortion $D-Î”<\exp(-N^Ï€)$
		and gap to capacity $R-I(W)<N^{-Ï}$ for any $(Ï€,Ï)âˆˆğ’ª$ and big $N$.
	\end{cor}
	
	The asymmetric channel case is more involved.
	We actually have, and need, four inequalities
	\begin{gather*}
		ğ˜—\{Z(ğ˜_n)<Î¸\}>1-H(W)+Î³,	\\
		ğ˜—\{T(ğ˜_n)<Î¸\}>H(W)+Î³,		\\
		ğ˜—\{Z(ğ˜˜_n)<Î¸\}>1-H(Q)+Î³,	\\
		ğ˜—\{T(ğ˜˜_n)<Î¸\}>H(Q)+Î³,		
	\end{gather*}
	and basic facts
	\begin{gather*}
		\{Z(ğ˜_n)<Î¸\}âˆ©\{T(ğ˜_n)<Î¸\}=0,	\\
		\{Z(ğ˜˜_n)<Î¸\}âˆ©\{T(ğ˜˜_n)<Î¸\}=0,	\\
		\{Z(ğ˜˜_n)<Î¸\}âˆ©\{T(ğ˜_n)<Î¸\}=0.
	\end{gather*}
	Here, $\{ğ˜˜_n\}$ is the channel process grown from $Q$, and
	$Î¸$ (threshold) and $Î³$ (gap) are the shorthands of the complicated functions.
	The basic facts are consequences of the common-fate property
	and the monotonicity property $H(W)=H(Xï½œY)â‰¤H(X)=H(Xï½œâ™£)=H(Q)$.
	
	Applying the inclusion--exclusion principle, we conclude that
	$ğ˜—\{T(ğ˜˜_n)<Î¸$ and $Z(ğ˜_n)<Î¸\}>H(Q)-H(W)-6Î³=I(W)-6Î³$.
	This implies the existence of codes with $ï¼°<\exp(-2^{Ï€n})$ and $R>I(W)-2^{-Ïn}$.
	
	\begin{cor}[Good code for BDMC]
		For any BDMC, polar coding yields block error probability $ï¼°<\exp(-N^Ï€)$
		and gap to capacity $I(W)-R<N^{-Ï}$ for any $(Ï€,Ï)âˆˆğ’ª$ and big $N$.
	\end{cor}
	
	The next chapter contains yet another application of \cref{thm:Z-e2pin,thm:T-e2pin}.
	In brief, the idea is to prune the channel process
	when $ğ˜_n$ becomes too reliable or too noisy.
	And it is only after learning how reliable/noisy
	$ğ˜_n$ is that we know where to prune.

\section{A Side Note on Lossless Compression}

	Lossless compression with side information at the decoder side can be solved
	by polar coding as well but assumes a different format \cite{Arikan10,CK10}.
	In this scenario, the random variable to be compressed is denoted by $X$,
	and will be treated as the input of an abstract channel $W$;
	the side information accessible by the decoder
	is denoted by $Y$ and treated as the output of $W$.
	
	$W$ is then polarized.
	For if a descendant of $W$ is reliable, it means that
	its input is mostly determined by its output, and hence needs no extra action.
	If, otherwise, a descendant of $W$ is noisy, its input is
	largely independent from its output, and we should record this input.
	The code rate is thus the density of the noisy descendants.
	There will be a block error if the decoder fails to
	recover the input of aÂ descendant that was not recorded;
	the block error probability is thus the sum of
	the $Z$'s of the reliable descendants.
	
	This come down to the following corollary.
	
	\begin{cor}[Good code for lossless compression]
		For any binary source $X$ to be compressed losslessly and side information $Y$,
		polar coding yields block error probability $ï¼°<\exp(-N^Ï€)$
		and gap to entropy $R-H(Xï½œY)<N^{-Ï}$, for any $(Ï€,Ï)âˆˆğ’ª$ and big $N$.
	\end{cor}



\chapter{Pruning Channel Tree}\label{cha:prune}

	\dropcap
	Complexity of encoder and decoder influences,
	sometimes dominates, practicality of a code.
	Throughout the history of coding---Hamming, Reed--Muller, turbo, LDPC, polar,
	et seq---real world codes are always easy to implement in the first place
	regardless of achieving capacity or not (usually not).
	This is why, apart from $N$, $P$, and $R$,
	we should care about the complexities of codes.
	
	Polar code, an outlier in said list, is the first code
	to achieve capacity in combination with a very low complexity.
	Considering that the decoder should at least read in
	all $N$ symbols in a code block, which costs $O(N)$ resources,
	polar coding's complexity of $O(Nã’N)$ is impressive, if not surprising.
	
	In this chapter, a modification is made to polar coding.
	It aims to eliminate the inefficient components
	in the encoder/decoder to reduce the complexity even further.
	By doing so carefully, the new complexity is $O(Nã’(ã’N))$,
	the block error probability decays quasi-polynomially fast to $0$,
	and the gap to capacity decays as fast as before.
	For comparison, existing works like \cite{AK11,EMFLK17,MHCG20}
	did not break the $O(Nã’N)$ barrier, and some latest work \cite{HMFVCG20}
	achieves $Nã’(ã’N)$ \emph{latency} only in the fully-parallel mode.
	
	Being called pruning, this technique is inspired by a trinitarian correspondence
	among the encoder/decoder, the channel tree, and the channel process.
	The argument start from the channel process side:
	Since $ğ˜_nâ†’ğ˜_âˆâˆˆ\{0,1\}$, the increments $ğ˜_{n+1}-ğ˜_n$ converge to $0$.
	By the correspondence, that means that on the channel tree side,
	branches that are deep enough are purposeless---%
	applying channel transformation barely changes anything.
	Now turn our focus to the encoder/decoder side;
	the said observation implies that some components in the encoder/decoder
	are consuming resources without helping the code become better,
	and we should have removed them.
	
	The goal of the current chapter is to make precise the last paragraph.

\section{Encoder/Decoder vs Tree vs Process}

	The design of the encoder and decoder of polar coding is best described by figures.
	There is a device called EU (encoding unit)
	and another device called DU (decoding unit);
	their actual implementations are not of interest here.
	But the devices are such that, when you wrap two copies of $W$ with one EU--DU pair,
	like \cref{fig:transform} does, the top pair of pins (A and B) behaves like $Wï¼·1$,
	and the bottom pair of pins (C and D) behaves like $Wï¼·2$.
	We say that \cref{fig:transform} corresponds to a tree with three vertices:
	$W$ the root and $Wï¼·1$ and $Wï¼·2$ its children.
	
	\tikzset{
		E/.pic={
			\pgftransformscale{1/6}\def~{coordinate}
			\draw[very thick](-6,3)~(=o0)--(6,3)~(=i0)(-6,-3)~(=o1)--(6,-3)~(=i1);
			\draw[fill=white](-5,-5)rectangle(5,5)(0,0)node{EU};
		},
		D/.pic={
			\pgftransformscale{1/6}\def~{coordinate}
			\draw[very thick](-6,3)~(=i0)--(6,3)~(=o0)(-6,-3)~(=i1)--(6,-3)~(=o1);
			\draw[fill=white](-5,-5)rectangle(5,5)(0,0)node{DU};
		},
		C/.pic={
			\pgftransformscale{1/6}\def\c{coordinate}
			\draw[very thick](-4,0)\c(=e)--(4,0)\c(=d);
			\draw[fill=white](-3,-2)rectangle(3,2)(0,0)node{CH};
		}
	}
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=1/6]{
			\draw
									(0,12)pic(c0){C}
				(-12,9)pic(e){E}						(12,9)pic(d){D}
									(0,6)pic(c1){C}
			;
			\draw
				\foreach\f in{e,d}{
					\foreach\j in{0,1}{
						(c\j=\f)--(\f=i\j)
					}
				}
				(e=o0)node[left]{pin A}(d=o0)node[right]{pin B}
				(e=o1)node[left]{pin C}(d=o1)node[right]{pin D}
			;
		}
		\caption{
			The design of encoder and decoder---level $1$.
		}\label{fig:transform}
	\end{figure}
	
	To construct the grandchildren of $W$, wrap more
	EU--DU pairs around two copies of \cref{fig:transform}.
	For instance, in \cref{fig:transform(1)}, the two copies of pin A
	are connected to another EU, the two copies of pin B to another DU.
	Recall that pin A--pin B behaves like the input--output of $Wï¼·1$;
	so wrapping one more layer of EU--DU transforms it further into
	$(Wï¼·1)ï¼·1$ (from pin E to pin F) and $(Wï¼·1)ï¼·2$ (from pin G to pin H).
	Note that the two copies of pin C and pin D are naked (not connecting to anything);
	this represents the fact that there are two copies of $Wï¼·2$
	that are not (yet) transformed into $(Wï¼·2)ï¼·1$ and $(Wï¼·2)ï¼·2$.
	Now \cref{fig:transform(1)} corresponds to a channel tree with five vertices:
	$W$ the root, $Wï¼·1$ and $Wï¼·2$ its children, plus
	$(Wï¼·1)ï¼·1$ and $(Wï¼·1)ï¼·2$ the children of the elder sibling.
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=1/6]{
			\draw
											(0,24)pic(c00){C}
			(-27,21)pic(2e0){E}(-11,21)pic(1e0){E}	(11,21)pic(1d0){D}(27,21)pic(2d0){D}
											(0,18)pic(c01){C}
											
											(0,12)pic(c10){C}
								(-11,9)pic(1e1){E}		(11,9)pic(1d1){D}
											(0,6)pic(c11){C}
			;
			\draw
				\foreach\f in{e,d}{
					\foreach\k in{0,1}{
						\foreach\j in{0,1}{
							(c\k\j=\f)--(1\f\k=i\j)
						}
						(1\f\k=o0)--(2\f0=i\k)
					}
				}
				(2e0=o0)node[left]{E}(2d0=o0)node[right]{F}
				(2e0=o1)node[left]{G}(2d0=o1)node[right]{H}
			;
		}
		\caption{
			The design of encoder and decoder---%
			transforming $Wï¼·1$ further at level $2$.
		}\label{fig:transform(1)}
	\end{figure}
	
	Duplicate \cref{fig:transform(1)}.
	This time, wrap around the two copies of pin G and H
	as shown in \cref{fig:transform(1)(2)}.
	Then we are effectively transforming $(Wï¼·1)ï¼·2$
	into $((Wï¼·1)ï¼·2)ï¼·1$ and $((Wï¼·1)ï¼·2)ï¼·2$.
	Now \cref{fig:transform(1)(2)} corresponds to a channel tree with seven vertices:
	$W$, $Wï¼·1$, $Wï¼·2$, $(Wï¼·1)ï¼·1$, and $(Wï¼·1)ï¼·2$ and its children.
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=1/6,scale=3/4,every pic/.style={scale=3/4}]{
			\def\pi{pic}
			\draw
														(0,48)Ï€(c000){C}
						(-24,45)Ï€(2e00){E}(-11,45)Ï€(1e00){E}	(11,45)Ï€(1d00){D}(24,45)Ï€(2d00){D}

		(-42,21)Ï€(3e00){E}(-24,21)Ï€(2e10){E}(-11,21)Ï€(1e10){E}	(11,21)Ï€(1d10){D}(24,21)Ï€(2d10){D}(42,21)Ï€(3d00){D}
														(0,18)Ï€(c101){C}
														
														(0,12)Ï€(c110){C}
											(-11,9)Ï€(1e11){E}	(11,9)Ï€(1d11){D}
														(0,6)Ï€(c111){C}
			;
			\draw
				\foreach\f in{e,d}{
					\foreach\l in{0,1}{
						\foreach\k in{0,1}{
							\foreach\j in{0,1}{
								(c\l\k\j=\f)--(1\f\l\k=i\j)
							}
							(1\f\l\k=o0)--(2\f\l0=i\k)
						}
						(2\f\l0=o1)--(3\f00=i\l)
					}
				}
			;
		}
		\caption{
			The design of encoder and decoder---%
			transforming $(Wï¼·1)ï¼·2$ further at level $3$.
		}\label{fig:transform(1)(2)}
	\end{figure}
	
	Lesson:
	Each pair of pins corresponds to a (synthetic) channel;
	one channel may have multiple copies that each corresponds to a pair.
	For any synthetic channel, we wrap another layer of EU--DU pair
	around the corresponding pins if we want to transform it further.
	We leave the pins corresponding to a channel naked
	if we do not want to transform it anymore.
	
	Rephrase in terms of channel process:
	The channel process $\{ğ˜_n\}$ is defined prior to
	whether we want to transform channels or not.
	But we can choose â€œnot to look at itâ€.
	More rigorously, we look at the endless sequence $ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚,\dotsc$
	and determine the least $n$ such that $(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚â€¦ğ˜‘_n)$
	points to a channel we do not want to transform anymore.
	Let $ğ˜´$ be that $n$ in this case.
	That is, $ğ˜´$ is a random variable depending on $\{ğ˜‘_n\}$;
	moreover, whether or not $ğ˜´=n$ is determined by the first $n$ terms of $\{ğ˜‘_n\}$.
	This makes $ğ˜´$ a stopping time adapted to $\{ğ˜‘_n\}$.
	Consequently, $\{ğ˜_{nâˆ§ğ˜´}\}$ is a stopped process that evolves like $\{ğ˜_n\}$
	at the beginning but halts at $ğ˜_ğ˜´$, a channel we are satisfied with.
	
	It remains to do the following three things:
	(a)	Show how $ğ˜´$ is related to the complexity,
	(b)	show how $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ is related to the code metrics $ï¼°$ and $R$, and
	(c)	define a good stopping time $ğ˜´$ to optimize (a) and (b).

\section{Stopping Time vs Complexity}

	Fix an SBDMC $W$.
	Fix an $n$;
	we are to construct a low-complexity code of block length $N=2^n$.
	Pretend that the complexity is the number of EU and DU devices used.
	Doing so is backed up by the fact that, in reality,
	EU and DU cost bounded amount of arithmetic and memory.
	
	Let $ğ˜´$ be any stopping time adapted to $\{ğ˜‘_n\}$, and assume $ğ˜´â‰¤n$.
	Having $ğ˜´â‰¤n$ is to make sure that we do not transform any depth-$n$ channel further.
	We have the following lemma concerning $ğ˜´$ and the complexity.
	
	\begin{lem}[Complexity in terms of $ğ˜´$]\label{lem:prune-C}
		Use $ğ˜´$ to generate a code.
		Then the encoding and decoding complexity is
		$O(Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜´])$ per code block, or $O(ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´])$ per channel usage.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Let us begin with $N=2^n$ copies of the channel $W$.
		If $ğ˜´>0$, then there are $2^{n-1}$ EU--DU pairs that
		wrap around $W$'s to synthesize $2^{n-1}$ copies of $Wï¼·1$ and $Wï¼·2$.
		
		Next, let us consider the $ğ˜‘â‚=1$ case.
		If $ğ˜´>1$ in this case, it means that we want to transform $Wï¼·1$.
		We need $2^{n-2}$ EU--DU pairs to wrap around the $2^{n-1}$ copies of $Wï¼·1$.
		Similarly, if $ğ˜´>1$ in the $ğ˜‘â‚=2$ case, then we are transforming
		$2^{n-1}$ copies of $Wï¼·2$ using another $2^{n-2}$ EU--DU pairs.
		
		Next, consider the depth-$2$ branching of $(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚)$.
		For each of the four possible realizations of the pair $(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚)=(jâ‚,jâ‚‚)$,
		if $ğ˜´>2$, then we are to transform the $2^{n-2}$ copies
		of $(Wï¼·{jâ‚})ï¼·{jâ‚‚}$ with $2^{n-3}$ EU--DU pairs.
		
		Finally, consider the general case at arbitrary depth:
		If $ğ˜´>m$ for a certain tuple $(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚â€¦ğ˜‘_m)=(jâ‚,jâ‚‚â€¦j_m)$,
		then we will transform $2^{n-m}$ copies of
		$ğ˜_m=\(\dotsb((Wï¼·{jâ‚})ï¼·{jâ‚‚})\dotsb\)ï¼·{j_m}$
		further with $2^{n-m-1}$ EU--DU pairs.
		The total number of EU--DU pairs is thus
		\[âˆ‘_{m=0}^nâˆ‘_{jâ‚^m}2^{n-m-1}Â·ğ˜\{ğ˜‘â‚^m=jâ‚^mâ€  and â€ ğ˜´>m\}
			=âˆ‘_{m=0}^n2^{n-1}ğ˜—\{ğ˜´>m\}=Ã·N2ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´].\]
		Here $ğ˜$ is the indicator of an event.
		
		Recall that I claimed without a proof that
		EU and DU devices cost constant resources.
		Thus, the complexity of a code is proportional
		to the number of such devices used.
		So a code generated by $ğ˜´$ has complexity
		$O(Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜´])$ per code block or $O(ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´])$ per channel usage.
		The end.
	\end{proof}
	
	The last lemma connects a stopping time $ğ˜´$ to the cost of its code.
	Intuitively speaking, it attributes to the fact that
	preparing a synthetic channel at depth $m$ costs $m$ layers of EU--DU.
	Hence preparing $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ costs $ğ˜´$ layers,
	and preparing all instances of $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ costs $Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]$.
	
	In the next section, we will see how $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ connects to the code performance
	in a very similar way, i.e., $ï¼°â‰¤Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡_ğ˜´Â·ğ˜\{ğ˜¡_ğ˜´<Î¸\}]$ and $R=ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_ğ˜´<Î¸\}$,
	due to very similar reasons.

\section{Stopped Process vs Performance}

	Thanks to the stopping time $ğ˜´$, the channel tree is pruned,
	or â€œharvestedâ€, before it reaches depth $n$.
	This implies that the leafs $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ are not as polarized as before.
	We therefore are liable to rebound the block error probability and code rate.
	
	Let $ğ’¥$ be some set of indices $(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚â€¦ğ˜‘_ğ˜´)$ that point to
	the synthetic channels we are to use to send plain messages.
	Lemmas follow.
	
	\begin{lem}[$R$ in terms of $ğ’¥$]\label{lem:prune-R}
		The code rate
		\[R=ğ˜—\{(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚â€¦ğ˜‘_ğ˜´)âˆˆğ’¥\}\]
		is the probability that the prefix $ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´$ is selected in $ğ’¥$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		I claim without a proof that the code rate is the density
		of the naked pins that correspond to channels in $ğ’¥$.
		
		Assuming that, we see that each $ğ˜_ğ˜´$
		corresponds to $2^{n-ğ˜´}$ pairs of naked pins.
		That is to say, each $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ assumes $2^{n-ğ˜´}$ copies in the EU--DU circuit.
		Since there are always $2^n$ pairs of naked pins
		(adding ED/DU does not alter the number of naked pins),
		a pair of naked pins possesses probability measure $2^{-n}$.
		Thus each $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ possesses probability measure $2^{-ğ˜´}$,
		the same probability measure possessed by $(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚â€¦ğ˜‘_ğ˜´)$.
		This finishes the proof.
	\end{proof}

	\begin{lem}[$ï¼°$ in terms of $ğ’¥$]\label{lem:prune-P}
		The block error probability
		\[ï¼°â‰¤Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡_ğ˜´Â·ğ˜\{(ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚â€¦ğ˜‘_ğ˜´)âˆˆğ’¥\}]\]
		is bounded by the sum of $ğ˜¡_ğ˜´$ that are
		selected in $ğ’¥$, weighted by multiplicity.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		I claim without a proof that the block error probability
		is bounded from above by the sum of $Z$'s of
		the synthetic channels selected in $ğ’¥$, multiplicity included.
		
		Assuming that, and knowing that each $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ assumes $2^{n-ğ˜´}$ copies
		and possesses probability measure $2^{-ğ˜´}$, we infer that
		each $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ contributes $Nğ˜—\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´=jâ‚^ğ˜´\}Â·Z(ğ˜_ğ˜´)$ to the upper bound
		if it is selected, otherwise it would have contributed $0$.
		Each $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ contributing $Nğ˜—\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´=jâ‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}Â·Z(ğ˜_ğ˜´)$,
		their sum is clearly $Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡_ğ˜´Â·ğ˜\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}]$.
		This is the upper bound we want to prove.
	\end{proof}

	It is time to declare an $ğ˜´$ and compute the induced $R$, $ï¼°$, and complexity.
	The basic strategy, like what we did in \cref{cha:origin}, is to set a threshold $Î¸$
	and hope that $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ is â€œat least $Î¸$-goodâ€, or $ğ˜¡_ğ˜´â‰¤Î¸$ to be rigorous.
	In contrast to \cref{cha:origin}, we can now control $ğ˜´$,
	so it is actually more efficient if we incorporate $Î¸$ into the declaration of $ğ˜´$.

\section{Actual Code Construction}

	Let $Î¸$ be $4^{-n}$.
	Define the stopping time $ğ˜´$ as below
	\[ğ˜´â‰”nâˆ§\min\{m:ğ˜›_m<Î¸â€  or â€ ğ˜¡_m<Î¸\}.\label{for:stop}\]
	That is, we look for the first $m$ such that either $ğ˜›_m$ or $ğ˜¡_m$ are small enough;
	but if we cannot find such $m$ before reaching depth $n$, let $ğ˜´$ default to $n$.
	Let $ğ’¥$ be the indices with $ğ˜¡_ğ˜´<Î¸$.
	That is, if $ğ˜´$ is set to be $m$ because $ğ˜¡_m<Î¸$,
	then the corresponding $ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´$ are included in $ğ’¥$.
	If $ğ˜´$ is set to be $m$ because $ğ˜›_m<Î¸$ or because we reach $m=n$,
	then the corresponding $ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´$ are not included in $ğ’¥$.
	
	The block error probability incurred by this choice of $ğ˜´$ and $ğ’¥$
	is bounded by the weighted sum of $Z$'s that are all smaller than $Î¸â‰”4^{-n}$.
	Hence $ï¼°<NÎ¸=1/N$ by \cref{lem:prune-P}.
	
	The complexity and code rate are more complicated.
	See the next two theorems.
	
	\begin{thm}\label{thm:actual-C}
		Given that $ğ˜´$ is defined as in the first paragraph of this section,
		an upper bound on the complexity is
		\[O(Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜´])=O(Nã’(ã’N)).\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		By how $ğ˜´$ is defined, we have
		\[ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]=âˆ‘_{m=0}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{ğ˜´>m\}=âˆ‘_{m=0}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_mâ‰¥4^{-n}â€  and â€ ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}\}.
			\label{ine:fubini}\]
		It remains to understand what is going on in $\{ğ˜›_mâ‰¥4^{-n}$ and $ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}\}$.
		For that, recall the last two chapters:
		\begin{gather*}
			ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_m<\exp(-2^{m/40})\}>I(W)+2^{-m/5+o(m)},	\\
			ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_m<\exp(-2^{m/40})\}>H(W)+2^{-m/5+o(m)}.	
		\end{gather*}
		Here I choose $(Ï€,Ï)=(1/40,1/5)âˆˆğ’ª$.
		In words, I showed that either $ğ˜¡_m$ or $ğ˜›_m$,
		exclusively, becomes small with high probability.
		Hence it is unlikely that $ğ˜›_mâ‰¥4^{-n}$ and $ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}$ at once
		unless $m$ is small, in which case the proved threshold
		$\exp(-2^{m/40})$ is less than the demanded $4^{-n}$.
		
		We now classify $m$ into two classes:
		Those such that $\exp(-2^{m/40})â‰¥4^{-n}$ are called small $m$.
		Those such that $\exp(-2^{m/40})<4^{-n}$ are called large $m$.
		For small $m$, we have nothing but $ğ˜—\{$both $ğ˜›_m,ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}\}â‰¤1$.
		For large $m$,
		\[ğ˜—\{â€ both â€ ğ˜›_m,ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}\}
			â‰¤ğ˜—\{â€ both â€ ğ˜›_m,ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥\exp(-2^{m/40})\}<2^{-m/5+o(m)}.\]
		Continue bounding \cref{ine:fubini}:
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			âˆ‘_{m=0}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{â€ both â€ ğ˜›_m,ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}\}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{â€ small â€ m}ğ˜—\{â€ both â€ ğ˜›_m,ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}\}
				+âˆ‘_{â€ large â€ m}ğ˜—\{â€ both â€ ğ˜›_m,ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥4^{-n}\}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{â€ small â€ m}1+âˆ‘_{â€ large â€ m}2^{-m/5+o(m)}=\#\{â€ small â€ mâ€ 'sâ€ \}+O(1).
		\end{align*}
		The number of small $m$'s is the root of the euqation $\exp(-2^{m/40})=4^{-n}$.
		The root is $m=O(ã’n)$.
		Hence the complexity $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]<O(ã’n)=O(ã’(ã’N))$, as desired.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{thm}\label{thm:actual-R}
		Given that $ğ˜´$ and $ğ’¥$ are defined as in the first paragraph of this section,
		a lower bound on the code rate $R$ is
		\[Râ‰¥I(W)-2^{-n/5+o(n)}.\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Just to clarify the whole picture, let me claim the following trichotomy:
		\begin{itemize}
			\item	The frequency that $ğ˜´$ is set to $m$
					due to $ğ˜›_m<Î¸$ is $H(W)-2^{-n/5+o(n)}$.
			\item	The frequency that $ğ˜´$ is set to $m$
					due to reaching $n$ is $2^{-n/5+o(n)}$.
			\item	The frequency that $ğ˜´$ is set to $m$
					due to $ğ˜¡_m<Î¸$ is $I(W)-2^{-n/5+o(n)}$.
		\end{itemize}
		$ğ’¥$ collects those $ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´$ when $ğ˜¡_m<Î¸$ is the case.
		So the theorem statement is implied by the last bullet point.
		I will show the last bullet point;
		the proof thereof applies to the first bullet point,
		so I am effectively showing all three bullet points at once.
		
		In order to show the last bullet point,
		it suffices to understand the bad event $ğ˜‰â‰”\{ğ˜¡_ğ˜´â‰¥Î¸$ but $ğ˜¡_mâ†’0\}$.
		Once we know how to bound $ğ˜—(ğ˜‰)$, we will conclude that,
		with \cref{lem:prune-R}, the code rate is $R=ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_ğ˜´<Î¸\}â‰¥ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_mâ†’0\}-ğ˜—(ğ˜‰)$,
		which will be $I(W)-2^{-n/5+o(n)}$.
		To bound $ğ˜—(ğ˜‰)$, two cases will be discussed---$ğ˜¡_ğ˜´â‰¥Î¸$ because
		$ğ˜›_m<Î¸$ happens first, and $ğ˜¡_ğ˜´â‰¥Î¸$ because we reach $m=n$.
		
		Here goes the rigorous bound on $ğ˜—(ğ˜‰)$:
		If $ğ˜¡_ğ˜´â‰¥Î¸$, then $ğ˜´$ is not set to the current value because $ğ˜¡_m<Î¸$;
		it must be the case that the other criterion $ğ˜›_ğ˜´<Î¸$ holds,
		or that we reach $ğ˜´=n$.
		For the former case, $ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_ğ˜´<Î¸$ but $ğ˜¡_mâ†’0\}=ğ˜—\{ğ˜›_ğ˜´<Î¸$ but $ğ˜›_mâ†’1\}â‰¤Î¸=2^{-n}$
		by the common-fate property and $\{ğ˜›_n\}$ being a martingale.
		For the latter case,
		$ğ˜—\{ğ˜´=n$ and $ğ˜¡_mâ†’0\}<ğ˜—\{ğ˜´=n\}=ğ˜—\{$both $ğ˜›_m,ğ˜¡_mâ‰¥Î¸$ for all $m<n\}
			<ğ˜—\{$both $ğ˜›_{n-1},ğ˜¡_{n-1}â‰¥Î¸\}=2^{-(n-1)/5+o(n-1)}$.
		Sum the upper bounds of the two cases;
		it is $2^{-n/5+o(n)}$.
		
		Now that we get $ğ˜—(ğ˜‰)<2^{-n/5+o(n)}$, deduce the code rate
		$R=ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_ğ˜´<Î¸\}=ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_mâ†’0\}-ğ˜—(ğ˜‰)=I(W)-2^{-n/5+o(n)}$;
		that is what we want to prove.
	\end{proof}
	
	Recap:
	So far we had seen that, when a code is defined by my choice of $ğ˜´$ and $ğ’¥$,
	(a)	the block error probability is $1/N$,
	(b)	the complexity is $O(Nã’(ã’N))$ per code block
		or $O(ã’(ã’N))$ per channel usage, and
	(c)	the code rate is $I(W)-2^{-n/5+o(n)}$.
	The minor term $o(n)$ within $R$ can be eliminated by a topological argument.
	We summarize the chapter with the following corollary.
	
	\begin{cor}[Log-log code for SBDMC]
		Over any SBDMC, there exist capacity-achieving codes with encoding and decoding
		complexity $O(Nã’(ã’N))$ per code block or $O(ã’(ã’N))$ per channel usage.
		Moreover, the said codes have gaps to capacity $I(W)-R<N^{-1/5}$.
	\end{cor}
	
	In one sentence, \emph{log-logarithmic complexity achieves capacity}.

\section{A Note on Better Pace}

	The only place we used the pair $(1/40,1/5)âˆˆğ’ª$ explicitly is when
	we were solving $\exp(-2^{m/40})=4^{-n}$ for $m$ and obtain that $m=O(ã’n)$.
	We could, instead, choose another pair such as $(1/150,1/4.8)âˆˆğ’ª$.
	Then, we still get to keep $m=O(ã’n)$
	but the gap to capacity is $I(W)-R<N^{-1/4.8}$.
	By a topological argument, any $Ï<1/4.714$ works.
	This is why I claimed that the pruned polar code
	has the same gap to capacity as the original version.
	
	Concerning the equation $\exp(-2^{m/40})=4^{-n}$,
	we can also replace $4^{-n}$ with $\exp(-n^Ï„)$ for arbitrarily large $Ï„$.
	The root is then $m=O(Ï„ã’n)$, which is $O(ã’n)$ if $Ï„$ is fixed.
	That means the block error probability can be as low as $\exp(-n^Ï„)$
	while keeping the log-logarithmic complexity.
	This asymptote lies below $1/\poly(N)$ and belongs to $\exp(-1\poly(ã’N))$.
	This is why I claimed that the pruned polar code
	has quasi-polynomial block error probability.

\section{A Note on Asymmetric Case}

	For if the concerned channel $W$ is an asymmetric BDMC, we will need to apply
	a similar argument to $\{ğ˜˜_n\}$, the channel process grown from
	the non-uniform input distribution $Q$ treated as a channel with constant output.
	
	In the asymmetric case, the stopping time will be defined as
	\[ğ˜´â‰”nâˆ§\min\{m:\min(Z(ğ˜_m),T(ğ˜_m))<4^{-n}â€  and â€ \min(Z(ğ˜˜_m),T(ğ˜˜_m))<4^{-n}\}.\]
	To put in another way, we are looking for the minimal $m$
	that satisfies any of the following:
	\begin{itemize}
		\item	Both $Z(ğ˜_m),Z(ğ˜˜_m)$ are small.
		\item	Both $T(ğ˜_m),T(ğ˜˜_m)$ are small.
		\item	Or, both $Z(ğ˜_m),T(ğ˜˜_m)$ are small.
		\item	(Both $T(ğ˜_m),Z(ğ˜˜_m)$ being small is not possible.)
	\end{itemize}
	And if no $m$ meets the requirement before depth $n$,
	the stopping time defaults to $n$.
	Furthermore, $ğ’¥$ will be the indices $ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´$ with small $Z(ğ˜_m)$ and small $T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)$:
	that is, the indices where $ğ˜´$ is set to $m$ due to the third bullet point.
	
	The block error probability is upper bounded similarly to \cref{ine:P<sumsum}:
	\[ï¼°â‰¤Nğ”¼[Z(W_ğ˜´)ğ˜(ğ’¥)]+Nğ”¼[T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)ğ˜(ğ’¥)]â‰¤2NÂ·4^{-n}=2^{-n+1}.\]
	The complexity is upper bounded similarly to the symmetric case
	\begin{align*}
		ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]
		&	=âˆ‘_{m=0}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{ğ˜´>m\}	\\
		&	=âˆ‘_{m=0}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{\max(Z(ğ˜_m),Z(ğ˜_m))â‰¥4^{-n}
			â€  or â€ \max(Z(ğ˜˜_m),Z(ğ˜˜_m))â‰¥4^{-n}\}	\\
		&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{m=0}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{\max(Z(ğ˜_m),Z(ğ˜_m))â‰¥4^{-n}\}
			+ğ˜—\{\max(Z(ğ˜˜_m),Z(ğ˜˜_m))â‰¥4^{-n}\}	\\
		&	=O(ã’n)+O(1)+O(ã’n)+O(1)=O(ã’n).
	\end{align*}
	The code rate is lower bounded similarly to the symmetric case
	\begin{align*}
		R
		&	=ğ˜—\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}=ğ˜—\{â€ both â€ Z(ğ˜_ğ˜´),T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)<4^{-n}\}	\\
		&	â‰¥ğ˜—\{Z(ğ˜_m),T(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0\}-ğ˜—\{Z(ğ˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ Z(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¥4^{-n}\}	\\*
		&\kern12em	-ğ˜—\{T(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)â‰¥4^{-n}\}	\\
		&	â‰¥ğ˜—\{Z(ğ˜_m),T(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0\}-ğ˜—\{Z(ğ˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ T(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¤4^{-n}\}	\\*
		&\kern12em	-ğ˜—\{T(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ Z(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)â‰¤4^{-n}\}-ğ˜—\{ğ˜´=n\}	\\
		&	â‰¥I(W)-4^{-n}-4^{-n}-2^{-Ïn+o(n)}=I(W)-2^{-Ïn+o(n)}.
	\end{align*}
	Hence the following corollary.
	
	\begin{cor}[Log-log code for BDMC]
		Over any BDMC, there exist capacity-achieving codes with encoding and decoding
		complexity $O(Nã’(ã’N))$ per code block or $O(ã’(ã’N))$ per channel usage.
		Moreover, the said codes have gaps to capacity $I(W)-R<N^{-1/5}$.
	\end{cor}
	
	The same statement can be made to lossless and lossy compression.
	
	\begin{thm}[Log-log code for lossless compression]
		If $X$ is a binary source and $Y$ is the side information,
		then lossless compression can be done with encoding and decoding complexity
		$O(Nã’(ã’N))$ per code block or $O(ã’(ã’N))$ per source observation,
		block error probability $ï¼°<1/N$, and gap to entropy $H(Xï½œY)-R<N^{-1/5}$.
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{cor}[Log-log code for lossy compression]
		If $Yâˆˆğ’´$ is a random source and $\distï¼šğ”½â‚‚Ã—ğ’´â†’[0,1]$ is the distortion function,
		then lossy compression can be performed with encoding and decoding complexity
		$O(Nã’(ã’N))$ per code block or $O(ã’(ã’N))$ per source observation.
		excess of distortion $D-Î”<1/NÂ²$, and gap to capacity $R-I(W)<N^{-1/5}$.
	\end{cor}
	
	The discussion in the previous section applies.
	That is to say, the gap to capacity can be made as low as $N^{-1/4.8}$,
	the block error probability (or the excess of distortion)
	as low as $\exp(-n^Ï„)$, while retaining the same log-logarithmic complexity.

\section{Prospective Pruning}

	The pruning technique generalizes to arbitrary input (non-binary)
	and arbitrary matrix (no longer $\loll$).
	The next two chapters establish the theory of $\{ğ˜_n\}$ for the general scenario.
	After that we can prune, yielding $ğ˜_ğ˜´$, etc.



\chapter{General Alphabet and Kernel}\label{cha:general}

	\dropcap
	Binary channel models, be it BEC, BSC, BDMC, or BI-AWGN, are favored
	in theory for their simplicity and for real world communications.
	But the real world is not always binary;
	we might want to compress losslessly a non-binary source (yes, no, or unanswered);
	or we might want to approximate colors with a $256$-color palette.
	This is one of the two generalizations I want to make
	in this chapter---to enable all input alphabets of finite sizes \cite{STA09}.
	
	Enabling more input alphabets creates new challenges.
	The root of all challenges is that, to synthesize $Wï¼·1$ and $Wï¼·2$,
	we need to talk about the random variables $Uâ‚Â²â‰”Xâ‚Â²G^{-1}$ (c.f.\ \cref{for:U=X/G}).
	This does not a priori make sense as the addition structure
	on the input alphabet $ğ’³$ does not uniquely exist.
	Plus, even if we equip $ğ’³$ with some group structure (abelian or not),
	there are cases where the descendants $ğ˜_n$ are not polarized.
	This chapter addresses this challenge---how to equip $ğ’³$ with
	a proper algebraic structure to facilitate polarization,
	even if it means adding dummy symbols into $ğ’³$.
	
	Specking of the root of the definition $Uâ‚Â²â‰”Xâ‚Â²G^{-1}$,
	it is not hard to imagine that, if we substitute $G$ with a general $â„“Ã—â„“$ matrix,
	the overall performance of polar coding may differ \cite{KSU10}.
	To tell if it becomes better, worse, or not working at all,
	we need a machinery that predicts the performance of each matrix.
	This is the second generalization I want to make
	in this chapter---to judge all matrices as the polarizing kernel.
	
	The judgement of a matrix will be
	very similar to those in \cref{cha:origin,cha:dual}.
	We will need to prove (or assume) an eigen behavior.
	From that we can derive the en23 behavior (named after $\exp(-n^{2/3})$),
	the een13 behavior (named after $\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)$),
	and finally the elpin behavior (named after $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$).
	Along the way, I will explain how these behaviors relate to
	LLN, LDP, CLT, and MDP in probability theory.
	
	This is a long chapter.
	Here goes the actual plan for this chapter.

\section{Chapter Organization}

	I will define the most general channel (\cref{sec:setup}).
	Following that is a reduction of input alphabet
	to prime size or prime-power size (\cref{sec:six}).
	Even more definitions are then made, including channel parameters
	$ï¼°$, $Z$, $ï¼º$, $T$, $S$, and $ï¼³$ (\cref{sec:param}) and how to use
	an invertible $â„“Ã—â„“$ matrix to synthesize $Wï¼·1,Wï¼·2â€¦Wï¼·{â„“}$ (\cref{sec:matrix}).
	Inserted here is a briff review of LLN, LDP, CLT, and MDP in probability theory
	and how they relate to coding theory (\cref{sec:regime}).
	I will then clarify that only a subset of matrices are
	qualified to polarize channels (\cref{sec:ergodic}).
	After that is a compactness argument that shows
	$Ï±>0$ for those qualified matrices (\cref{sec:en23}).
	Finally, the region of $(Ï€,Ï)$ where $ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_n<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})\}>1-H(W)+â„“^{-Ïn}$
	will be pictured in two steps (\cref{sec:een13,sec:elpin}).

\section{General Problem Setup}\label{sec:setup}

	The following channel is what was considered by Shannon in the eternal paper,
	followed by other big scholars who generalized Shannon.
	It is of historical interest to prove theorems over the same class of channels.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		A \emph{discrete memoryless channel} (DMC) is a Markov chain $Wï¼šğ’³â†’ğ’´$ such that
		\begin{itemize}
			\item	the input alphabet $ğ’³$ is a finite set,
			\item	the output alphabet $ğ’´$ is a finite set, and
			\item	$W(yï½œx)$ is the array of transition probabilities satisfying	\\
					$âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}W(yï½œx)=1$ for all $xâˆˆğ’³$.
		\end{itemize}
	\end{dfn}
	
	Denote by $Q(x)$ an input distribution, by $W(x,y)$ the joint distribution,
	by $W(xï½œy)$ the posterior distribution, and by $W(y)$ the output distribution.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{conditional entropy} of $W$ is
		\[H(W)â‰”-âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}W(x,y)ã’_{\abs{ğ’³}}W(xï½œy),\]
		which is the amount of noise/equivocation/ambiguity/fuzziness caused by $W$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{mutual information} of $W$ is
		\[I(W)â‰”H(Q)-H(W)=âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}W(x,y)ã’_{\abs{ğ’³}}Ã·{W(xï½œy)}{Q(x)},\]
		Fix a $Q$ that maximizes $I(W)$.
		Call this $Q$ a \emph{capacity-achieving input distribution}.
		Call this maximal $I(W)$ the \emph{channel capacity} of $W$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	Let $ğ’°$ be a finite set called the user alphabet.
	Messages to be transmitted over $W$ will be
	pre-encoded into $ğ’°$ and distributed uniformly.
	The overall goal is to construct, for some large $N$, an encoder $â„°ï¼šğ’°^{RN}â†’ğ’³^N$
	and a decoder $ğ’Ÿï¼šğ’´^Nâ†’ğ’°^{RN}$ such that the composition
	\cd[every arrow/.append style=mapsto]{
		Uâ‚^{RN}\rar{â„°}	&	Xâ‚^N\rar{W^N}	&	Yâ‚^N\rar{ğ’Ÿ}	&	Ë†Uâ‚^{RN}
	}
	is the identity map as frequently as possible,
	and $R$ as close to the channel capacity $I(W)$ as possible.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		Call $N$ the block length.
		Call $R$ the code rate.
		Denote by $ï¼°$, called the block error probability,
		the probability that $Ë†Uâ‚^{RN}â‰ Uâ‚^{RN}$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	In the next section, I will argue that we can reduce the problem of noisy-channel
	coding over arbitrary DMCs to $ğ’°$ and $ğ’³$ having finite field structure.

\section{Pay Asymmetry to Buy Non-Field}\label{sec:six}

	Reducing the size of user alphabet is easier and
	will be handled before the reduction of input alphabet:
	Consider integer factorization $|ğ’°|=pâ‚^{kâ‚}pâ‚‚^{kâ‚‚}\dotsm p_Ï‰^{k_Ï‰}$.
	We can then choose a bijection
	\[ğ’°â‰…ğ”½_{pâ‚^{kâ‚}}Ã—ğ”½_{pâ‚‚^{kâ‚‚}}Ã—\dotsbÃ—ğ”½_{p_Ï‰^{k_Ï‰}}.\]
	To transmit a symbol $uâˆˆğ’°$, it suffices to transmit its projections
	onto each of the constituent fields $ğ”½_{pâ‚^{kâ‚}}â€¦ğ”½_{p_Ï‰^{k_Ï‰}}$.
	It remains to show, for each finite filed $ğ”½_{p^k}$,
	how to design encoder/decoder for the user alphabet $ğ’°=ğ”½_{p^k}$.
	
	Moreover, each finite field admits a vector space structure
	\[ğ”½_{p^k}â‰…ğ”½_p^k.\]
	Therefore, when necessary, each $uâˆˆğ”½_{p^k}$ is considered as a vector
	in $ğ”½_p^k$, with each of its coordinates treated as a standalone symbol in $ğ”½_p$.
	That is to say, when necessary, we can even assume $ğ’°=ğ”½_p$ is of prime order.
	
	Reducing the size of user alphabet takes effort and will be handled in the sequel:
	Recall that $\abs{ğ’°}$ is either a prime or a prime power.
	Let $q$ be any power of $\abs{ğ’°}$ greater than or equal to $\abs{ğ’³}$.
	Consider an embedding $ğ’³âŠ†ğ”½_q$;
	call an $xâˆˆğ’³$ an actual symbol;
	call a $vâˆˆğ”½_qã€ğ’³$ a virtual symbol.
	Define a preprocessor channel $â™®ï¼šğ”½_qâ†’ğ’³$ that sends an actual symbol to itself,
	and a virtual symbol to a fixed actual symbol $xâ‚âˆˆğ’³$.
	In terms of transition probabilities:
	\[â™®(yï½œx)=\cas{
		1	&	if $y=xâˆˆğ’³$,	\\
		1	&	if $y=xâ‚$ and $xâˆ‰ğ’³$,	\\
		0	&	otherwise.
	}\]
	That is to say, all virtual symbols are semantic copies of $xâ‚$;
	they make no difference to the decoder whatsoever.
	
	Now build the following degraded channel
	\cd{
		ğ”½_q\rar{â™®}	&	ğ’³\rar{W}	&	ğ’´.
	}
	By the data processing inequality, the channel capacity of $Wâˆ˜â™®$
	is at most the channel capacity of $W$.
	Hence using $ğ”½_q$ as the input alphabet does no harm at best,
	and handicaps ourselves at worst.
	The former is the case:
	If we take any capacity-achieving input distribution $Q$ of $W$ and
	apply it directly to $Wâˆ˜â™®$, then we can transmit information at the same rate.
	In conclusion, $W$ and $Wâˆ˜â™®$ share the same channel capacity.
	It suffices to consider $Wâˆ˜â™®$---channels with prime-power sized
	input alphabet---for the remainder of this chapter.
	
	Remark:
	$â™®âˆ˜W$ is not a symmetric channel;
	in particular, the uniform distribution does not achieve the best transmission rate.
	(Because that means $xâ‚$ is input more frequently than it should have been.)
	As a consequence, this technique of allowing dummy symbols, albeit trivial,
	does not apply until \cite{HY13} taught us how to deal with asymmetric channels.
	Hence the section title \emph{pay asymmetry to buy non-field}.
	
	We hereby assume that $ğ’³=ğ”½_q$ is a finite field.
	If necessary, $p$ is the characteristic of $ğ”½_q$
	and $ğ”½_p$ denotes the ground field of $ğ”½_q$.
	I am going to define more channel parameters in the next section;
	some of them require the finite field structure on $ğ’³$
	in a way that is not required/obvious when $q=2$.

\section{Parameters and HÃ¶lder Tolls}\label{sec:param}

	Let $W$ be any DMC with input alphabet $ğ’³=ğ”½_q$ for some prime power $q$.
	To emphasize that the input alphabet was reduced to $q$,
	we call $W$ a \emph{$q$-ary channel}.
	The parameters below generalize $H$, $I$, and $Z$ defined in \cref{cha:origin}
	and $T$ defined in \cref{cha:dual}.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{bit error probability} of a $q$-ary channel $W$ is
		\[ï¼°(W)â‰”âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}W(y)ï¼ˆ1-\max_{xâˆˆğ’³}W(xï½œy)ï¼‰,\]
		which is how often the maximum a posteriori estimator
		$yâ†¦\argmax_{xâˆˆğ’³}W(xï½œy)$ makes a mistake.
	\end{dfn}
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{Bhattacharyya parameter} of a $q$-ary channel $W$ is
		\[Z(W)â‰”Ã·1{q-1}âˆ‘_{\substack{x,x'âˆˆğ”½_q\\xâ‰ x'}}
			âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}âˆš{W(x,y)W(x',y)}.\]
		In addition, define
		\[ï¼º(W)â‰”\max_{0â‰ dâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}âˆš{W(x,y)W(x+d,y)}.\]
		The parameter below is not used directly in this work,
		but plays a central role in a theorem I cite.
		I include it for completeness:
		\[Z_d(W)â‰”âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}âˆš{W(x,y)W(x+d,y)}.\]
	\end{dfn}
	
	Remarks:
	$Z$ is the average of $Z_d$ over $dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—$ and $ï¼º$ is the maximum thereof.
	The rescaling is such that $0â‰¤Zâ‰¤ï¼ºâ‰¤(q-1)Zâ‰¤q-1$ and that $0â‰¤Z_dâ‰¤1$.
	When $q=2$, i.e., for the binary case, $Z=ï¼º=Zâ‚=$
	the $Z$-parameter defined in \cref{dfn:bin-Z}.
	Notice how the summation over distinct $x,x'âˆˆğ”½â‚‚$
	turns into twice the case of $(x,x')=(0,1)$.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{total variation norm} of a $q$-ary channel $W$ is
		\[T(W)â‰”âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}W(y)âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}\abs[\Big]{W(xï½œy)-Ã·1q}.\]
		which is the total variation distance from $W(â€¢ï½œy)$ to
		the uniform distribution, weighted by the frequency each $yâˆˆğ’´$ appears.
	\end{dfn}
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{Fourier coefficients} of a $q$-ary channel $W$ is
		\[M(wï½œy)â‰”âˆ‘_{zâˆˆğ”½_q}W(zï½œy)Ï‡(wz),\]
		where $Ï‡ï¼šğ”½_qâ†’â„‚$ is an additive character defined as $Ï‡(x)â‰”\exp(2Ï€i\tr(x)/p)$,
		and $\trï¼šğ”½_qâ†’ğ”½_p$ is the field trace onto the ground field.
	\end{dfn}
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{Fourier $â„“Â¹$ norm} of a $q$-ary channel $W$ are
		\[S(W)â‰”Ã·1{q-1}âˆ‘_{0â‰ wâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}W(y)Â·\abs[\Big]{M(wï½œy)}\]
		In addition, define
		\[ï¼³(W)â‰”\max_{0â‰ wâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}W(y)Â·\abs[\Big]{M(wï½œy)}.\]
	\end{dfn}
	
	Remarks:
	The rescaling is such that $0â‰¤Sâ‰¤ï¼³â‰¤(q-1)Sâ‰¤q-1$.
	When $q=2$, the parameters collapse---$S=ï¼³=T=1-2ï¼°=$
	the $T$-parameter defined in \cref{dfn:bin-T}.
	
	I borrow some lemmas to relate these parameters.
	
	\begin{lem}[$P$ vs $Z$]\label{lem:PvsZ}
		\cite[Lemma~22 with $k=1$]{MT14}
		For any $q$-ary channel $W$,
		\[Ã·{q-1}{qÂ²}ï¼ˆâˆš{1+(q-1)Z(W)}-âˆš{1-Z(W)}ï¼‰Â²â‰¤ï¼°(W)â‰¤Ã·{q-1}2Z(W).\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{lem}[$P$ vs $T$]\label{lem:PvsT}
		\cite[Lemma~23 with $k=q-1$]{MT14}
		For any $q$-ary channel $W$,
		\[Ã·{q-1}q-ï¼°(W)â‰¤Ã·{T(W)}2â‰¤Ã·{q-1}q-Ã·1qï¼ˆ(q-1)qï¼°(W)-(q-1)(q-2)ï¼‰.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{lem}[$P$ vs $S$]\label{lem:PvsS}
		\cite[Lemma~26 with $k=q-1$]{MT14}
		For any $q$-ary channel $W$,
		\[1-Ã·q{q-1}ï¼°(W)â‰¤S(W)â‰¤(q-1)qï¼ˆÃ·{q-1}q-ï¼°(W)ï¼‰âˆš{1-Ã·q{q-1}Ã·{q-2}{q-1}}.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{lem}[$P$ vs $H$]\label{lem:PvsH}
		\cite[Theorem~1]{FM94}
		For any $q$-ary channel $W$,
		\begin{gather*}
			hâ‚‚(ï¼°(W))+ï¼°(W)ã’â‚‚(q-1)â‰¥H(W)ã’â‚‚qâ‰¥2ï¼°(W),	\\
			H(W)ã’â‚‚qâ‰¥(q-1)qã’â‚‚Ã·q{q-1}ï¼ˆï¼°(W)-Ã·{q-2}{q-1}ï¼‰+ã’â‚‚(q-1).
		\end{gather*}
		Here, $hâ‚‚$ is the binary entropy function.
		The upper bound is Fano's inequality.
		The first lower bound is useful when $H(W)$ and $ï¼°(W)$ are small;
		the second lower bound is useful when $H(W)$ and $ï¼°(W)$ are close to $1$.
	\end{lem}
	
	The phenomenon described by the cited lemmas
	is distilled to form the following definition.
	It is the quantified version of the common-fate property.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		Fix a $q$.
		Let $A$ and $B$ be two channel parameters.
		Say $A$ and $B$ are \emph{bi-HÃ¶lder at $(a,b)$}
		if there exist constants $c,d>0$ such that, for all $q$-ary channels,
		$\abs{A(W)-a}â‰¤cÂ·\abs{B(W)-b}^d$ and $\abs{B(W)-b}â‰¤cÂ·\abs{A(W)-a}^d$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	Bi-HÃ¶lder-ness is clearly an equivalence relation.
	In particular, we will make use of its transitivity property---%
	if $A$ and $B$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(a,b)$ and $B$ and $C$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(b,c)$,
	then $A$ and $C$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(a,c)$.
	In this case, we say $A$, $B$, and $C$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(a,b,c)$.
	This notion generalizes to arbitrarily many parameters.
	What \cref{lem:PvsZ,lem:PvsT,lem:PvsS,lem:PvsH} tell us
	is where the parameters are bi-HÃ¶lder at.
	
	\begin{pro}[Implicit bi-HÃ¶lder tolls]\label{pro:im-toll}
		Fix a prime power $q$.
		Then channel parameters $H$, $ï¼°$, $Z$, and $ï¼º$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(0,0,0,0)$.
		Channel parameters $H$, $ï¼°$, $T$, $S$, and $ï¼³$
		are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(1,1-1/q,0,0,0)$.
	\end{pro}
	
	\begin{proof}
		As for the first statement:
		$Z,ï¼º$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(0,0)$ since $Zâ‰¤ï¼ºâ‰¤(q-1)Z$.
		\Cref{lem:PvsZ} implies that $ï¼°$ and $Z$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(0,0)$.
		\Cref{lem:PvsH} (with the first lower bound)
		implies that $ï¼°$ and $H$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(0,0)$.
		Now apply the transitivity to conclude the first statement.
		
		As for the second statement:
		$S$ and $ï¼³$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(0,0)$ since $Sâ‰¤ï¼³â‰¤(q-1)S$.
		\Cref{lem:PvsS} implies that $ï¼°$ and $S$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(1-1/q,0)$.
		\Cref{lem:PvsT} implies that $ï¼°$ and $T$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(1-1/q,0)$.
		\Cref{lem:PvsH} (with the second lower bound)
		implies that $ï¼°$ and $H$ are bi-HÃ¶lder at $(1-1/q,1)$.
		Now apply the transitivity to conclude the second statement.
	\end{proof}
	
	In short, when a channel is reliable, $H,ï¼°,Z,ï¼º$ are small and $T,S,ï¼³$ are large.
	When a channel is noisy, $H,ï¼°,Z,ï¼º$ are large and $T,S,ï¼³$ are small.
	See \cref{fig:holder} for an example of the possible region.
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=6]{
			\draw
				(0,1)pic{y-tick=$1$}(0,0)pic{y-tick=$0$}
				(0,0)pic{x-tick=$0$}(1,0)pic{x-tick=$1$};
			\draw[fill=gray]
				plot[domain=0:1,samples=180](\x/2*\x/2,\x)--(1,1)--
				plot[domain=1:0,samples=180](\x,\x/2*\x/2)--cycle;
			\draw[->](0,0)--(0,1.1)node[left]{$ï¼º$ or $ï¼³$};
			\draw[->](0,0)--(1.1,0)node[right]{$H$ or $1-H$};
		}
		\caption{
			A figurative example of bi-HÃ¶lder relation.
			This plot assumes $c=2$ and $d=1/2$.
			But $c$ is in general greater, which makes the dark region larger.
		}\label{fig:holder}
	\end{figure}
	
	Sometimes, explicit bounds are desired as it saves some epsilon--delta notations.
	The bounds do not have to be tight,
	but they should be as easy to manipulate as possible.
	The next proposition serves exactly that purpose.
	
	\begin{pro}[Explicit HÃ¶lder tolls]\label{pro:ex-toll}
		For all $q$-ary channels $W$, it holds that
		\begin{gather*}
			ï¼º(W)â‰¤qÂ³âˆš{H(W)},		\label{ine:Z<H}\\
			H(W)â‰¤qÂ³âˆš{ï¼º(W)},		\label{ine:H<Z}\\
			ï¼³(W)â‰¤qÂ³âˆš{1-H(W)},	\label{ine:S<1-H}\\
			1-H(W)â‰¤qÂ³âˆš{ï¼³(W)}.	\label{ine:1-H<S}
		\end{gather*}
	\end{pro}
	
	\begin{proof}
		The proof is nothing but working out
		\cref{lem:PvsZ,lem:PvsT,lem:PvsS,lem:PvsH} very carefully.
		By â€œworking outâ€, I mean to Taylor expand every inequality at a proper point
		and keep track of how the constants $c,d$ accumulate as transitivity applies.
		
		In the upcoming arguments, $H$, $ï¼°$, $Z$, $ï¼º$, $S$, and $ï¼³$ mean
		$H(W)$, $ï¼°(W)$, $Z(W)$, $ï¼º(W)$, $S(W)$, and $ï¼³(W)$, respectively.
		Also $q'$ means $q-1$, and $q''$ means $q-2$.
		Furthermore, $\lg$ means the base-$2$ logarithm;
		this is handy when we jump back and forth between nats, bits, and $q$-bits.
		
		First we show \cref{ine:Z<H}.
		Start from $ï¼º$:
		By definition $ï¼ºâ‰¤q'Z$.
		Move on to $Z$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsZ}, $q'q^{-2}(âˆš{1+q'Z}-âˆš{1-Z})Â²â‰¤ï¼°$,
		hence $âˆš{1+q'Z}-âˆš{1-Z}â‰¤qâˆš{ï¼°/q'}$.
		Multiplying both sides by the conjugate yields
		$(1+q'Z)-(1-Z)â‰¤qâˆš{ï¼°/q'}(âˆš{1+q'Z}+âˆš{1-Z})$.
		The left-hand side is $qZ$;
		in the right-hand side $âˆš{1+q'z}+âˆš{1-z}$ assumes
		the maximum $q/âˆš{q'}$ at $z=q''/q'$ by taking derivative.
		So $Zâ‰¤âˆš{ï¼°/q'}(q/âˆš{q'})=qâˆš{ï¼°}/q'$.
		Move on to $ï¼°$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsH} (the first lower bound),
		$2ï¼°â‰¤H\lg q$ or equivalently $ï¼°â‰¤Hã’â‚„q$.
		Now we chain the inequalities $ï¼ºâ‰¤q'Zâ‰¤qâˆš{ï¼°}â‰¤qâˆš{Hã’â‚„q}$.
		This completes \cref{ine:Z<H} as $qâˆš{ã’â‚„q}<qÂ³$.
		
		Second we show \cref{ine:H<Z}.
		Start from $H$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsH} (the upper bound, Fano's inequality),
		$H\lg qâ‰¤hâ‚‚(ï¼°)+ï¼°\lg q'$.
		By \cref{fig:rootep}, $hâ‚‚(ï¼°)+ï¼°\lg q'â‰¤âˆš{eï¼°}+ï¼°\lg q'=âˆš{ï¼°}(âˆše+âˆš{ï¼°}\lg q')$.
		What is inside parentheses is less than $âˆše+âˆš{q'/q}\lg q'$.
		Hence $Hâ‰¤âˆš{ï¼°}(âˆše+âˆš{q'/q}\lg q')/\lg q$.
		Focus on the scalar---$(âˆše+âˆš{q'/q}\lg q')/\lg q$
		has maximum $âˆše$ at $q=2$ (remember that $qâ‰¥2$).
		So $Hâ‰¤âˆš{eï¼°}$.
		Move on to $ï¼°$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsZ}, $ï¼°â‰¤q'Z/2$.
		Move on to $Z$:
		By definition $Zâ‰¤ï¼º$.
		Now we chain the inequalities $Hâ‰¤âˆš{eï¼°}â‰¤âˆš{eq'Z/2}â‰¤âˆš{eq'ï¼º/2}$.
		This completes \cref{ine:H<Z} as $âˆš{eq'/2}<qÂ³$.
		
		Third we show \cref{ine:S<1-H}.
		Start from $ï¼³$:
		By definition $ï¼³â‰¤q'S$.
		Move on to $S$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsS}, $Sâ‰¤q'q(q'/q-ï¼°)âˆš{1-Ã·q{q'}Ã·{q''}{q'}}$.
		The square root simplifies to $âˆš{1/(q')Â²}=1/q'$ as $qq''=(q')Â²-1$.
		So $Sâ‰¤q'-qï¼°$.
		Move on to $q'-qï¼°$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsH} (the upper bound, Fano's inequality),
		$H\lg qâ‰¤hâ‚‚(ï¼°)+ï¼°\lg q'$.
		We claim that $hâ‚‚(ï¼°)+ï¼°\lg q'â‰¤\lg q-2(q'/q-ï¼°)Â²/ã‘2$.
		To prove the claim, Taylor expand both sides at $ï¼°=q'/q$.
		Verify that both evaluate to $\lg q$ at $ï¼°=q'/q$;
		verify that both have derivative $0$ at $ï¼°=q'/q$; and
		verify that the acceleration of the left-hand side, $-1/(ï¼°(1-ï¼°)ã‘2)$,
		is more negative than the acceleration of the right-hand side, $-4/ã‘2$.
		By Taylor's theorem, mean value theorem, or the Euler method,
		the function with greater acceleration is greater;
		hence the claim.
		See also \cite[Fig.~1]{FM94};
		the $Î¦$-curve seems parabolic at the upper right corner.
		Now we have $H\lg qâ‰¤\lg q-2(q'/q-ï¼°)Â²/ã‘2$, which is equivalent to
		$2(q'/q-ï¼°)Â²/ã‘qâ‰¤1-H$ and to $q'-qï¼°â‰¤qâˆš{(1-H)ã‘(q)/2}$.
		Now we chain the inequalities $ï¼³â‰¤q'Sâ‰¤q'(q'-qï¼°)â‰¤q'qâˆš{(1-H)\ln(q)/2}$.
		This completes \cref{ine:S<1-H} as $q'qâˆš{\ln(q)/2}<qÂ³$.
		
		Fourth we show \cref{ine:1-H<S}.
		Start from $1-H$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsH} (the second lower bound),
		$H\lg qâ‰¥q'q\lg(q/q')(ï¼°-q''/q')+\lg q'$.
		The right-hand side is $\lg q-q'\lg(q/q')(q'-qï¼°)$
		by matching the (rational) coefficients of
		$ï¼°\lg q$, $ï¼°\lg q'$, $\lg q$, and $\lg q'$, respectively.
		As $H\lg qâ‰¥\lg q-q'\lg(q/q')(q'-qï¼°)$ we bound
		$\lg(q/q')=\lg(1+1/q')â‰¤1/q'$ by the tangent line at $1/q'=0$.
		So $H\lg qâ‰¥\lg q-(q'-qï¼°)$ and hence $1-Hâ‰¤(q'-qï¼°)/\lg q$.
		Move on to $q'-qï¼°$:
		By \cref{lem:PvsH}, $1-qï¼°/q'â‰¤S$ so $q'-qï¼°â‰¤q'S$.
		Move on to $S$:
		By definition $Sâ‰¤ï¼³$.
		Now we chain the inequalities
		$1-Hâ‰¤(q'-qï¼°)/\lg qâ‰¤q'S/\lg qâ‰¤q'ï¼³/\lg q$.
		This completes \cref{ine:1-H<S} as $q'/\lg q<qÂ³$.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=8]{
			\draw
				(0,1)pic{y-tick=$1$}(0,0)pic{y-tick=$0$}
				(0,0)pic{x-tick=$0$}(1,0)pic{x-tick=$1$};
			\draw
				plot[domain=0:90,samples=360]({sin(\x)^2},{h2o(\x)})
				({sin(60)^2},{h2o(60)})node[above right]{$hâ‚‚(p)$}
				plot[domain=0:1.1,samples=20](\x^2/e,\x)node[right]{$âˆš{ep}$};
			\draw[->](0,0)--(0,1.1);
			\draw[->](0,0)--(1.1,0)node[right]{$p$};
		}
		\caption{
			The binary entropy function and an upper bound of $âˆš{ep}$.
		}\label{fig:rootep}
	\end{figure}
	
	HÃ¶lder-ness is a â€œtollâ€ because, while \cref{pro:im-toll,pro:ex-toll}
	are the bridges that connect channel parameters,
	it feels like we pay fees, or taxes, to translate bounds on channels;
	and the fee/tax is charged regardless we go one way or another.
	For example, say we start with $H(ğ˜_n)<\exp(-n^{2/3})$,
	then $ï¼º(ğ˜_n)<qÂ³\exp(-n^{2/3}/2)$,
	which contains annoying minor terms that harden the proof.
	Once we succeed in proving $ï¼º(ğ˜_n)<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)$, we pay the price again
	when translating it back to $H(ğ˜_n)<qÂ³\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}/2\)$.
	
	The next section synthesizes channels on the basis of general matrices,
	and shows how synthetic channels relate to parameters defined here.

\section{Kernel and Fundamental Theorems}\label{sec:matrix}

	Fix a prime power $q$.
	Let $â„“â‰¥2$.
	Let $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ be an invertible $â„“Ã—â„“$ matrix over $ğ”½_q$.
	This matrix is to replace $\loll$ that was used to polarize channels
	in \cref{cha:origin,cha:dual,cha:prune}, and is called a \emph{kernel}.
	See cref{fig:nine} for an illustration of the EU--DU pairs that corresponds to
	a $3Ã—3$ kernel and how to wrap them around (synthetic) channels.
	
	\tikzset{
		E/.pic={
			\pgftransformscale{1/6}\def~{coordinate}
			\draw[very thick](-6,6)~(=o0)--(6,6)~(=i0)

				(-6,0)~(=i1)--(6,0)~(=o1)(-6,-6)~(=i2)--(6,-6)~(=o2);
			\draw[fill=white](-5,-8)rectangle(5,8)(0,0)node[align=center]{$3Ã—3$\\DU};
		}
	}
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=1/6]{
			\def\pi{pic}
			\draw
											  (0,54)Ï€(c00){C}
			(-31,48)Ï€(2e0){E}(-11,48)Ï€(1e0){E}(0,48)Ï€(c01){C}(11,48)Ï€(1d0){D}(31,48)Ï€(2d0){D}

			The design of encoder and decoder when $â„“=3$.
		}\label{fig:nine}
	\end{figure}
	
	Let $Xâ‚^â„“$ be $â„“$ i.i.d.\ random variables that follow $Q$,
	the capacity-achieving input distribution of $W$.
	Define $Uâ‚^â„“â‰”Xâ‚^â„“G^{-1}$.
	Let $Yâ‚^â„“$ be the outputs of $â„“$ i.i.d.\ copies of $W$ given the inputs $Xâ‚^â„“$.
	That is to say, each $(X_j,Y_j)$ is governed by $Q(x)$ and $W(yï½œx)$.
	Now consider the following guessing job:
	\begin{itemize}
		\item	Guess $Uâ‚$ given $Yâ‚^â„“$.
		\item	Guess $Uâ‚‚$ given $Yâ‚^â„“$,
				assuming that the guess $Ë†Uâ‚$ of $Uâ‚$ is correct.
		\item	Guess $Uâ‚ƒ$ given $Yâ‚^â„“$,
				assuming that the guesses $Ë†Uâ‚Â²$ of $Uâ‚Â²$ are correct.
				\[â‹®\]
		\item	Guess $U_â„“$ given $Yâ‚^â„“$,
				assuming that the guesses $Ë†Uâ‚^{â„“-1}$ of $Uâ‚^{â„“-1}$ are correct.
	\end{itemize}
	For each $j=1,2â€¦â„“$, pretend that $Wï¼·j$ is a channel
	whose input is $U_j$ and output is $Yâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1}$.
	This channel captures the difficulty of the $j$th guessing job.
	The precise definition follows.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		For each $j=1,2â€¦â„“$, define a synthetic channel
		\[Wï¼·j(yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1}ï½œu_j)â‰”Ã·{P\{Yâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1}=yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1}\}}{P\{U_j=u_j\}}
			=Ã·{âˆ‘\limits_{u_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}W(uâ‚^â„“G,y_k)}
				{âˆ‘\limits_{vâ‚^{j-1}u_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“-1}}Q^â„“(vâ‚^{j-1}u_j^â„“G)},\]
		where $Q^â„“$ is the product measure and $W^â„“$ is the product channel, i.e.,
		\[Q^â„“(xâ‚^â„“)=âˆ_{j=1}^â„“Q(x_j),\qquad W^â„“(xâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)â‰”âˆ_{j=1}^â„“W(x_j,y_j),\]
		and $vâ‚^{j-1}u_j^â„“G$ is vector concatenation
		before vector-matrix multiplication.
	\end{dfn}
	
	There are three results that control $Wï¼·j$ via
	the parameters $H,ï¼º,ï¼³$ defined in the last section.
	These results play central roles in the theory of polar coding
	and is bestowed the title of fundamental theorems.
	
	\begin{thm}[Fundamental theorem of polar coding---$H$ version (FTPC$H$)]
		\label{thm:ftpcH}
		For any $q$-ary channel $W$ and any invertible matrix kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$,
		\[âˆ‘_{j=1}^â„“H(Wï¼·j)=â„“H(W).\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		We derive that
		\[âˆ‘_{j=1}^â„“H(Wï¼·j)=âˆ‘_{j=1}^â„“H(U_jï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1})
			=H(Uâ‚^â„“ï½œYâ‚^â„“)=H(Xâ‚^â„“ï½œYâ‚^â„“)=â„“H(Xï½œY).\]
		The first equality is by definition.
		The next equality is by the chain rule of conditional entropy.
		The next equality is by $G$ being invertible.
		The last equality is because $(Xâ‚^â„“,Yâ‚^â„“)$ are i.i.d.\ copies of $(X,Y)$.
	\end{proof}
	
	Fundamental theorem stated and proved,
	the other two fundamental theorems require more details about $G$ to be stated.
	
	Let $0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“$ be a vector of
	$0$ repeated $j-1$ times followed by a $1$ and $â„“-j$ arbitrary symbols.
	A \emph{coset code} is a subset of codewords of the form
	$\{0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“G:u_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“-j}\}âŠ†ğ”½_q^â„“$.
	The coset codes have weight distributions just like every other code does.
	Let $\hwt(xâ‚^â„“)$ be the Hamming weight of $xâ‚^â„“$.
	The weight enumerator of the $j$th coset code is
	this one-variable polynomial over the integers
	\[f_Zï¼·j(z)â‰”âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}z^{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“G)}âˆˆâ„¤[z].\]
	Note that this coincides with the distance enumerator
	from the $j$th row of $G$ to the span of the rows beneath.
	
	\begin{thm}[Fundamental theorem of polar coding---$Z$ end (FTPC$Z$)]
		\label{thm:ftpcZ}
		For any $q$-ary channel $W$ and any invertible matrix kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$,
		\[ï¼º(Wï¼·j)â‰¤f_Zï¼·j(ï¼º(W)).\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		By the definition of the synthetic channel $Wï¼·j$ and
		that of the Bhattacharyya parameter, $ï¼º(Wï¼·j)$ is
		\[\max_{0â‰ d_jâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{u_jâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{uâ‚^{j-1}yâ‚^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^jÃ—ğ’´^â„“}
			âˆš{Wï¼·j(u_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})Wï¼·j(u_j+d_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})}.\]
		By the nature of $\max_{0â‰ d_jâˆˆğ”½_q}$, it suffices to show that
		the double sum within is at most $f_Zï¼·j(ï¼º(W))$ for arbitrary nonzero $d_j$.
		
		In the upcoming argument, vector concatenation takes precedence
		over vector-matrix multiplication and vector addition.
		Fix a $d_jâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—$, we argue that
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			âˆ‘_{u_jâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1}âˆˆğ’´^â„“Ã—ğ”½_q^j}
				âˆš{Wï¼·j(u_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})Wï¼·j(u_j+d_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{uâ‚^jyâ‚^â„“}âˆš{Wï¼·j(u_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})Wï¼·j(u_j+d_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{uâ‚^jyâ‚^â„“}âˆš{\vphantom{âˆ‘}\smash{
					âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“-j}}W^â„“(uâ‚^ju_{j+1}^â„“G,yâ‚^â„“)
					âˆ‘_{v_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“-j}}W^â„“(uâ‚^{j-1}(u_j+d_j)v_{j+1}^â„“G,yâ‚^â„“)
				}}	\\
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{uâ‚^jyâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{v_{j+1}^â„“}âˆš{W^â„“(uâ‚^ju_{j+1}^â„“G,yâ‚^â„“)
					W^â„“(uâ‚^{j-1}(u_j+d_j)v_{j+1}^â„“G,yâ‚^â„“)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{uâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“-j}}
				âˆš{W^â„“(uâ‚^â„“G,yâ‚^â„“)W^â„“(uâ‚^{j-1}(u_j^â„“+d_j^â„“)G,yâ‚^â„“)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}
				âˆš{W^â„“(xâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)W^â„“(xâ‚^â„“+0â‚^{j-1}d_j^â„“G,yâ‚^â„“)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}
				âˆš{W^â„“(xâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)W^â„“(xâ‚^â„“+eâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}
				âˆ_{kâˆˆ[â„“]}âˆš{W(x_k,y_k)W(x_k+e_k,y_k)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}
				âˆš{W(x_k,y_k)W(x_k+e_k,y_k)}âˆ_{kâˆ‰K}W(x_k,y_k)	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_ky_k}
				âˆš{W(x_k,y_k)W(x_k+e_k,y_k)}ï¼‰âˆ_{kâˆ‰K}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_ky_k}W(x_k,y_k)ï¼‰	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_ky_k}âˆš{W(x_k,y_k)W(x_k+e_k,y_k)}ï¼‰	\\
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}\max_{0â‰ e_kâˆˆğ”½_q}
				ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_ky_k}âˆš{W(x_k,y_k)W(x_k+e_k,y_k)}ï¼‰	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆk}ï¼º(W)=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}ï¼º(W)^{\abs K}
				=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}ï¼º(W)^{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}d_jd_{j+1}^â„“G)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{d_{j+1}^â„“}ï¼º(W)^{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_jd_{j+1}^â„“G)}=f_Zï¼·j(ï¼º(W)).
		\end{align*}
		The first equality abbreviates the summation.
		The next equality expands $Wï¼·j$ by the very definition,
		where $u_{j+1}^â„“$ and $v_{j+1}^â„“$ are free variables in $ğ”½_q$.
		The next inequality is by sub-additivity of square root.
		In the next equality we define $d_{j+1}^â„“â‰”v_{j+1}^â„“-u_{j+1}^â„“$;
		so summing over $v_{j+1}^â„“$ is equivalent to summing over $d_{j+1}^â„“$.
		In the next equality we define $xâ‚^â„“â‰”uâ‚^â„“G$;
		so summing over $uâ‚^â„“$ is equivalent to summing over $xâ‚^â„“$
		as $G$ is invertible.
		In the next equality we substitute $eâ‚^â„“â‰”0â‚^{j-1}d_j^â„“G$
		and reorder the summation.
		The next equality expands the product of the memoryless channels.
		The next equality classifies the indices into two classes---%
		$kâˆˆK$ are those such that $e_kâ‰ 0$ and $kâˆ‰K$ are those such that $e_k=0$.
		The next equality is the distributive law $ax+ay+bx+by=(a+b)(x+y)$.
		The next equality uses the fact that $W(x,y)$ sum to $1$.
		In the next inequality we replace $e_k$ by a nonzero element
		that maximizes the sum in the parentheses.
		In the next equality we realize that
		the maximum is the Bhattacharyya parameter (surprisingly).
		The second last equality uses the fact that
		multiplying a vector by a scalar preserves its Hamming weight.
		And quod erat demonstrandum.
	\end{proof}
	
	The last fundamental theorem lives in the dual picture.
	Let $uâ‚^{j-1}1_j0_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“$ be a vector of
	$j-1$ arbitrary symbols followed by a $1$ and $0$ repeated $â„“-j$ times.
	Let $\Git$ be the inverse transpose of $G$.
	The $j$th dual coset code of $G$ is a subset of codewords of the form
	$\{uâ‚^{j-1}1_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git:uâ‚^{j-1}âˆˆğ”½_q^{j-1}\}âŠ†ğ”½_q^â„“$.
	The weight enumerator of the $j$th dual coset code is
	defined to be this one-variable polynomial over the integers
	\[f_Sï¼·j(s)â‰”âˆ‘_{uâ‚^{j-1}}s^{\hwt(uâ‚^{j-1}1_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git)}âˆˆâ„¤[s].\]
	We can now state the dual of the second fundamental theorem.
	
	\begin{thm}[Fundamental theorem of polar coding---$S$-end (FTPC$S$)]
		\label{thm:ftpcS}
		For any $q$-ary channel $W$ and any invertible matrix kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$,
		\[ï¼³(Wï¼·j)â‰¤f_Zï¼·j(ï¼³(W)).\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		(Reminder: 
		For those who attempt to skip the proof, the proof spans about $2.2$ pages.)
		
		Recall the character $Ï‡(x)â‰”\exp(2Ï€i\tr(x)/p)$.
		We need these properties of $Ï‡$:
		(xa)	$Ï‡(0)=1$;
		(xb)	$\abs{Ï‡(x)}=1$ for all $xâˆˆğ”½_q$;
		(xc)	$Ï‡(x)Ï‡(z)=Ï‡(x+z)$ for all $x,zâˆˆğ”½_q$; and
		(xd)	$âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ”½_q}Ï‡(x)=0$.
		See also \cite[Definition~24]{MT14} or a dedicated book \cite{Terras99}.
		To prove the theorem,
		we first verify that the Fourier coefficients recover the origin:
		Let $M(w,y)â‰”W(y)M(wï½œy)=âˆ‘_{zâˆˆğ”½_q}W(z,y)Ï‡(wz)$, then
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			âˆ‘_{wâˆˆğ”½_q}M(w,y)Ï‡(-xw) = âˆ‘_{wâˆˆğ”½_q}âˆ‘_{zâˆˆğ”½_q}W(z,y)Ï‡(wz)Ï‡(-xw)	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{zâˆˆğ”½_q}W(z,y)âˆ‘_{wâˆˆğ”½_q}Ï‡(w(z-x))=âˆ‘_{zâˆˆğ”½_q}W(z,y)qğ•€\{z-x=0\}=qW(x,y).
		\end{align*}
		The first equality expands $M(w,y)$ by the definition.
		The next equality uses that $Ï‡$ is an additive character (xc),
		and reorders the summation.
		The next equality uses $âˆ‘_{wâˆˆğ”½_q}Ï‡(w)=0$ (xd) and $âˆ‘_{wâˆˆğ”½_q}Ï‡(0)=q$ (xa);
		and $ğ•€$ is the indicator function.
		
		Knowing $W(x_j,y_j)=q^{-1}âˆ‘_{w_jâˆˆğ”½_q}M(w_j,y_j)Ï‡(-x_jw_j)$,
		we proceed to
		\begin{align*}
			&
			Wï¼·j(u_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})
				=âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}W^â„“(uâ‚^â„“G,yâ‚^â„“)=âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“-j}}W^â„“(xâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)
				=âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆ[â„“]}W(x_k,y_k)	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆ[â„“]}ï¼ˆÃ·1qâˆ‘_{w_kâˆˆğ”½_q}M(w_k,y_k)Ï‡(-x_kw_k)ï¼‰
				=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{wâ‚^â„“}âˆ_{kâˆˆ[â„“]}M(w_k,y_k)Ï‡(-x_kw_k)	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{wâ‚^â„“}Ï‡(-xâ‚^â„“(wâ‚^â„“)^âŠ¤)âˆ_{kâˆˆ[â„“]}M(w_k,y_k)
				=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{wâ‚^â„“}Ï‡(-xâ‚^â„“(wâ‚^â„“)^âŠ¤)M^â„“(wâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{wâ‚^â„“}Ï‡(-uâ‚^â„“G(wâ‚^â„“)^âŠ¤)M^â„“(wâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)
				=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{wâ‚^â„“}Ï‡(-uâ‚^â„“(wâ‚^â„“G^âŠ¤)^âŠ¤)M^â„“(wâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^â„“}Ï‡(-uâ‚^â„“(vâ‚^â„“)^âŠ¤)
				M^â„“(vâ‚^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^â„“}Ï‡(-uâ‚^j(vâ‚^j)^âŠ¤)
				M^â„“(vâ‚^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}Ï‡(-u_{j+1}^â„“(v_{j+1}^â„“)^âŠ¤)	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^â„“}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^â„“}Ï‡(-uâ‚^j(vâ‚^j)^âŠ¤)
				M^â„“(vâ‚^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)q^{â„“-j}ğ•€\{v_{j+1}^â„“=0\}	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^j}Ï‡(-uâ‚^j(vâ‚^j)^âŠ¤)M^â„“(vâ‚^j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“).
		\end{align*}
		The first equality expands the definition of $Wï¼·j$.
		In the next equality, we substitute $xâ‚^â„“â‰”uâ‚^â„“G$.
		The next equality expands the definition of $W^â„“$ down to $W$.
		The next two equalities Fourier expand $W$ and reorder the operators.
		The next equality merges all $Ï‡(-x_kw_k)$
		into one inner product by additivity (xc).
		In the next equality we define $M^â„“(wâ‚^â„“,yâ‚^â„“)$
		to be the product of all $M(w_k,y_k)$.
		The next two equalities use
		$xâ‚^â„“(wâ‚^â„“)^âŠ¤=uâ‚^â„“G(wâ‚^â„“)^âŠ¤=uâ‚^â„“(wâ‚^â„“G^âŠ¤)^âŠ¤$.
		In the next equality we define $vâ‚^â„“â‰”wâ‚^â„“G^âŠ¤$;
		so summing over $wâ‚^â„“$ is equivalent to summing over $vâ‚^â„“$.
		The last three equalities sum over $u_{j+1}^â„“$ to force $v_{j+1}^â„“=0$.
		
		Having that
		$Wï¼·j(u_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})=q^{-j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^j}
			Ï‡(-uâ‚^j(vâ‚^j)^âŠ¤)M^â„“(vâ‚^j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)$
		in mind, we move on to
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			Mï¼·j(Ï‰_j,uâ‚^{j-1}yâ‚^â„“)â‰”âˆ‘_{z_jâˆˆğ”½_q}Wï¼·j(z_j,yâ‚^â„“uâ‚^{j-1})Ï‡(Ï‰_jz_j)	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{z_jâˆˆğ”½_q}Ã·1{q^j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^j}Ï‡(-uâ‚^{j-1}z_j(vâ‚^j)^âŠ¤)
				M^â„“(vâ‚^j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)Ï‡(Ï‰_jz_j)	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^j}Ï‡(-uâ‚^{j-1}(vâ‚^{j-1})^{âŠ¤})
				M^â„“(vâ‚^j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)âˆ‘_{z_jâˆˆğ”½_q}Ï‡(z_j(Ï‰_j-v_j))	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^j}Ï‡(-uâ‚^{j-1}(vâ‚^{j-1})^âŠ¤)
				M^â„“(vâ‚^j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)qğ•€\{Ï‰_j=v_j\}	\\
			&	=Ã·q{q^j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}Ï‡(-uâ‚^{j-1}(vâ‚^{j-1})^âŠ¤)
				M^â„“(vâ‚^{j-1}Ï‰_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“).
		\end{align*}
		In the first line we let $Mï¼·j$ be the Fourier coefficients of $Wï¼·j$.
		The next equality plugs in what we have in mind about $Wï¼·j$.
		The next three equalities sum over $z_j$ to force $v_j=Ï‰_j$.
		
		With the fact that $Mï¼·j(Ï‰_j,uâ‚^{j-1}yâ‚^â„“)$ is equal to
		$q^{1-j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}Ï‡(-uâ‚^{j-1}(vâ‚^{j-1})^âŠ¤)\*
			M^â„“(vâ‚^{j-1}Ï‰_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)$,
		we obtain that with arbitrary $0â‰ Ï‰_jâˆˆğ”½_q$,
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			âˆ‘_{uâ‚^{j-1}yâ‚^â„“âˆˆğ”½^{j-1}Ã—ğ’´^â„“}\abs{Mï¼·j(Ï‰_j,uâ‚^{j-1}yâ‚^â„“)}	\label{for:sum-Mj}\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{uâ‚^{j-1}yâ‚^â„“}\abs[\Big]{Ã·q{q^j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}
				Ï‡(-uâ‚^{j-1}(vâ‚^{j-1})^âŠ¤)M^â„“(vâ‚^{j-1}Ï‰_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)}	\\
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{uâ‚^{j-1}yâ‚^â„“}Ã·q{q^j}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}
				\abs{M^â„“(vâ‚^{j-1}Ï‰_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}\abs{M^â„“(vâ‚^{j-1}Ï‰_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git,yâ‚^â„“)}
				=âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}âˆ_{kâˆˆ[â„“]}\abs{M(w_k,y_k)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}\abs{M(w_k,y_k)}âˆ_{kâˆ‰K}\abs{M(w_k,y_k)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{y_k}\abs{M(w_k,y_k)}ï¼‰
				âˆ_{kâˆ‰K}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{y_k}\abs{M(w_k,y_k)}ï¼‰	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{y_k}\abs{M(w_k,y_k)}ï¼‰
				â‰¤âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}âˆ_{kâˆˆK}ï¼³(W)	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}ï¼³(W)^{\abs K}
				=âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}ï¼³(W)^{\hwt(vâ‚^{j-1}Ï‰_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{vâ‚^{j-1}}ï¼³(W)^{\hwt(vâ‚^{j-1}1_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git)}=f_Sï¼·j(ï¼³(W)).
		\end{align*}
		The first equality expands the Fourier coefficients.
		The next inequality is triangle inequality plus (xb).
		The next equality cancels the summation over $uâ‚^{j-1}$ with $q^{1-j}$.
		In the next equality we substitute $wâ‚^â„“â‰”vâ‚^{j-1}Ï‰_j0_{j+1}^â„“\Git$;
		slightly different from the free $wâ‚^â„“$ before,
		they are now restricted to a proper subspace.
		The next equality classifies the indices into two classes---%
		$jâˆˆK$ are those such that $w_jâ‰ 0$ and $kâˆ‰K$ are such that $w_k=0$.
		The next two equalities reorder the operators and
		simplify $âˆ‘_{y_k}\abs{M(0,y_k)}=âˆ‘_{y_k}W(y_k)=1$.
		The next inequality replaces $w_j$ by
		one that maximizes $âˆ‘_{y_k}\abs{M(w_j,y_k)}$.
		The rest is trivial.
		
		\Cref{thm:ftpcS} claims that $ï¼³(Wï¼·j)â‰¤f_Sï¼·j(ï¼³(W))$.
		Since $ï¼³(Wï¼·j)$ is merely the maximum of \cref{for:sum-Mj}
		over $0â‰ Ï‰_jâˆˆğ”½_q$, we arrive at $ï¼³(Wï¼·j)â‰¤f_Sï¼·j(ï¼³(W))$.
		And quod erat demonstrandum.
	\end{proof}
	
	Sometimes, a simpler bound is enough for the analysis---instead of the exact
	weight enumerator, we use $(â€ \#codewordsâ€ )z^â€ minimum distanceâ€ $ as an upper bound.
	The number of codewords is easy to predict (it is some power of $2$).
	So one only needs to record the minimum distances.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		For any $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$, define \emph{coset distance}
		\[D_Zï¼·jâ‰”\hdis(r_j,R_j),\]
		where $\hdis$ is the Hamming distance, $r_j$ is the $j$th row of $G$,
		and $R_j$ is the subspace spanned by the rows beneath $r_j$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	Then FTPC$Z$ reads $ï¼º(Wï¼·j)â‰¤q^{â„“-j}ï¼º(W)^{D_Zï¼·j}$.
	The dual picture obeys the same logic.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		For any $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$, define \emph{dual coset distance}
		\[D_Sï¼·jâ‰”\hdis(c_j,C_j),\]
		where $\hdis$ is the Hamming distance, $c_j$ is the $j$th column of $G^{-1}$,
		and $C_j$ is the subspace spanned by the columns before $c_j$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	Then FTPC$S$ reads $ï¼³(Wï¼·j)â‰¤q^{j-1}ï¼³(W)^{D_Zï¼·j}$.
	
	The fundamental theorems introduced here are the â€œlocal relationsâ€ that
	control how one iteration of the channel transformation manipulates $W$.
	But in the end, we want to talk about all of its descendants at once,
	hence the introduction of the stochastic processes.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		Let $ğ˜‘â‚,ğ˜‘â‚‚,\dotsc$ be i.i.d.\ uniform
		random variables taking values in $\{1,2â€¦â„“\}$.
		Fix a $q$-ary channel $W$ and an invertible matrix $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$.
		Let $ğ˜â‚‰,ğ˜â‚,ğ˜â‚‚,\dotsc$, or $\{ğ˜_n\}$ in short,
		be a stochastic process of DMCs defined as follows:
		\begin{itemize}
			\item	$ğ˜â‚€â‰”W$; and
			\item	$ğ˜_{n+1}â‰”ğ˜_nï¼·{ğ˜‘_{n+1}}$.
		\end{itemize}
		This is called the \emph{channel process}.
	\end{dfn}
	
	Imagine the following infinite array:
	\[\bma{
		ï¼³(ğ˜â‚€)	&	ï¼³(ğ˜â‚)	&	ï¼³(ğ˜â‚‚)	&	ï¼³(ğ˜â‚ƒ)	&	â‹¯\quad{}	\\
		T(ğ˜â‚€)	&	T(ğ˜â‚)	&	T(ğ˜â‚‚)	&	T(ğ˜â‚ƒ)	&	â‹¯\quad{}	\\
		H(ğ˜â‚€)	&	H(ğ˜â‚)	&	H(ğ˜â‚‚)	&	H(ğ˜â‚ƒ)	&	â‹¯\quad{}	\\
		ï¼°(ğ˜â‚€)	&	ï¼°(ğ˜â‚)	&	ï¼°(ğ˜â‚‚)	&	ï¼°(ğ˜â‚ƒ)	&	â‹¯\quad{}	\\
		ï¼º(ğ˜â‚€)	&	ï¼º(ğ˜â‚)	&	ï¼º(ğ˜â‚‚)	&	ï¼º(ğ˜â‚ƒ)	&	â‹¯\quad{}	
	}\]
	The HÃ¶lder tolls, \cref{lem:PvsZ,lem:PvsT,lem:PvsS,lem:PvsH,%
	pro:im-toll,pro:ex-toll}, are vertical relations.
	The fundamental theorems, \cref{thm:ftpcH,thm:ftpcZ,thm:ftpcS},
	are horizontal relations.
	The top two rows are related to the distortion of
	lossy compression and shaping the input distributions for asymmetric channels.
	The bottom two rows are related to the block error probability of
	lossless compression and the anti-error part of noisy-channel coding.
	The middle row controls the code rate.
	In particular, \cref{thm:ftpcH} implies
	the following generalization of \cref{pro:martin}.
	
	\begin{pro}
		$\{H(ğ˜_n)\}$ is a martingale.
	\end{pro}
	
	\Cref{pro:im-toll,pro:ex-toll,thm:ftpcH,thm:ftpcZ,thm:ftpcS}
	are all we need to control the behavior of $\{ğ˜_n\}$.
	But before we make use of these tools to examine the performance of polar coding,
	let us walk through some terminologies to see the big picture and what to expect.

\section{Probability Theory Regimes}\label{sec:regime}

	There is an analogy between coding theory and probability theory
	that connects the results from both sides and the proofs thereof.
	This analogy constituents the picture of the expected performance of coding.
	This section is a brief introduction to that and is inspired by \cite{AW14}.
	
	Consider i.i.d.\ copies of some bounded random variable
	$Xâ‚,Xâ‚‚,\dotsc,X_N$ and their average $Â¯X_N$.
	We want to understand the distribution of $Â¯X_N$,
	i.e., we want to understand $P\{Â¯X_Nâ‰¤x\}$ for various $x$.
	The key is to identify the following
	\begin{itemize}
		\item	the mean $Î¼â‰”E[Xâ‚]$ with channel capacity $C$,
		\item	the number of copies $N$ with the block length $N$,
		\item	the cutoff $x$ with the code rate $R$
		\item	the cumulative probability $P\{Â¯X_Nâ‰¤x\}$
				with the block error probability $ï¼°$, and
		\item	the variance $ÏƒÂ²$ with another channel parameter called $V$.
	\end{itemize}
	
	For example, when Shannon said there exist error correcting codes
	with code rate $R<C$ close to channel capacity and block error probability $ï¼°â†’0$,
	this translates into when the cutoff $x<Î¼$ is close to
	the mean, the cumulative probability $P\{Â¯X_Nâ‰¤x\}$ converges to $0$ as $Nâ†’âˆ$.
	The latter is the law of large numbers (LLN).
	That establishes the first analogue.
	
	Later, Gallager said that the block error probability
	scales like $\exp(-\Er(R)N)$ for a fixed $R<C$.
	It translates into that the cumulative probability scales like
	$P\{Â¯X_Nâ‰¤x\}â‰ˆ\exp(-L(x)N)$ for a fixed cutoff $z<Î¼$ \cite{Gallager68}.
	The former is called the error exponent regime;
	the latter is called the \emph{large deviation principle} (LDP).
	Moreover, Gallager's error exponent function $\Er$
	is analogous to CramÃ©r's rate function $L(x)$.
	(To avoid abusing the word rate, I will referred to this as the CramÃ©r function.)
	The former is the convex conjugate of Gallager's $\Eo$ function;
	the latter is the convex conjugate of
	the cumulant generating function $tâ†¦ã‘E[\exp(tXâ‚)]$.
	That establishes the second analogue.
	
	On a parallel track, Strassen said that the code rate
	scales like $C+Î¦^{-1}(ï¼°)\*âˆš{V/N}$, where $Î¦$ is the cdf of
	the standard normal distribution, and $V$ is another intrinsic parameter
	called channel dispersion or varentropy \cite{Strassen62}.
	It translates into that the cutoff scales like $xâ‰ˆÎ¼+Î¦^{-1}(p)âˆš{ÏƒÂ²/N}$
	in order to attain a certain cumulative probability $p$.
	The former is called the finite block length regime
	(although, in fact, both this and the error exponent regime has finite $N$);
	the latter is called the \emph{central limit theorem} (CLT).
	That establishes the third analogue.
	Moreover $V$ is the analog of variance in coding theory.
	
	It is clear that LDP and CLT generalize LLN in ways that
	fix a variable and inspect the asymptote of the other variable.
	A third regime varies both.
	By \cite{AW14,PV10},
	\[Ã·{-ã‘ï¼°}{N(C-R)Â²}âŸ¶Ã·1{2V}.\]
	And it specializes to $ï¼°â‰ˆ\exp(-Î©(N))$ for a fixed $R<C$
	and $Râ‰ˆC-O(1)/âˆšN$ for a fixed $ï¼°$.
	Similarly, in probability theory, there is
	\[Ã·{ã‘P\{Â¯X-Î¼>Î³(N)x\}}{NÎ³(N)Â²}âŸ¶L(x),\]
	where $Î³(N)$ is some appropriate re-scalars,
	and $L(x)=1/ÏƒÂ²xÂ²$ is another CramÃ©r function .
	This is called the \emph{moderate deviation principle} (MDP).
	That establishes the fourth analogue.

\begin{table}
	\caption{

		random coding theory, and polar coding theory.
		All $Î´>0$ can be made arbitrarily close to $0$.
	}\label{tab:analog}
	\pgfplotstableread{

		LDP	â„™\{ï¼¸>x\}â‰ˆe^{-NL(x)}				ï¼°â‰ˆe^{-\Er(R)N}			ï¼°â‰ˆe^{-N^{1-Î´}}		
		CLT	ï¼¸âˆ¼ğ’©(0,Ã·{Ïƒ}{âˆšN})				ï¼²â‰ˆQ^{-1}(ï¼°)âˆš{Ã·VN}		ï¼²â‰ˆN^{-1/2+Î´}		
		MDP	\Æ’{P\{ï¼¸>Î³(N)x\}}{Î³(N)Â²}â‰ˆNL(x)	\Æ’{ï¼°}{(ï¼²)Â²}â‰ˆÃ·N{2V}		\Æ’{ï¼°}{(ï¼²)Â²}â‰ˆN^{1-Î´}
		%(\P,\R)â‰ˆ(e^{-N^Ï€},N^{-Ï})
	}\tableTrinity
	\def\arraystretch{1.44}
	\def\Æ’#1{Ã·{-ã‘#1}}
	\defÃ·#1#2{\frac{#1}{#2\rule[-1ex]{0pt}{0ex}}}
	\pgfplotstabletypeset[
		columns/par/.style={column name=,string type},
