	For their achievability bounds, the aforementioned results use
	random coding whose complexity is out of control.
	Beside random coding, polar coding is the only low-complexity code
	that is strong enough to approach the LDP, CLT, and MDP regimes.
	Some history is briefed below.
	See also \cref{tab:analog} for a comparison among
	probability theory and random and polar coding.
	
	ArÄ±kan's original works on channel polarization \cite{Arikan09} established
	the foundation of polar coding, placing polar coding in the LLN paradigm.
	ArÄ±kan--Telatar \cite{AT09} characterized the LDP behavior of polar coding,
	showing that $ï¼°$ scales like $\exp(-âˆšN)$ when an $R<I$ is fixed.
	Later, Korada--ÅaÅŸoÄŸlu--Urbanke \cite{KSU10} generalized polar codes
	from ArÄ±kan's kernel $\loll$ to any invertible $â„“Ã—â„“$ matrix $G$ over $ğ”½â‚‚$,
	granted that $â„“â‰¥2$ and $G$ is not column-equivalent to a lower triangular matrix.
	And then they showed that the LDP behavior is $ï¼°â‰ˆ\exp(-N^{\Ec(G)})$
	where $\Ec(G)$ is a constant depending on the kernel matrix $G$.
	In fact, $\Ec(G)$ is the expectation of $-ã’_â„“ğ˜‹â‚$.
	The notation $\Ec(G)$ is meant to resemble Gallager's error exponent $\Er(R)$;
	but be aware of that the former is inserted at $\exp(-N^â€ thisâ€ )$ place
	while the latter is inserted at $\exp(-â€ thisâ€ N)$ place.
	The LDP behavior of polar codes is then refined in \cite{HMTU13}.
	Therein, $ï¼°$ is approximated by $\exp(-â„“^ğ”ˆ)$ where
	$ğ”ˆâ‰”\Ec(G)n-âˆš{\Vc(G)n}Q^{-1}(R/I)+o(âˆšn)$ is a more accurate exponent,
	$â„“$ is the matrix dimension, $n$ is the depth of the code,
	and $\Vc(G)$ is another constant depending on $G$.
	The notation $\Vc(G)$ is meant to resemble the channel dispersion $V$.
	Appearing to be a CLT behavior, this result lies in
	the corner of the LDP paradigm that touches the MDP paradigm.
	Finally, Mori--Tanaka \cite{MT14} generalized everything above
	from binary input to channels with prime-power input.
	Over arbitrary input alphabets, \cite{STA09,Sasoglu11} %%%Check Nasser
	showed the equivalence of \cite{Arikan09,AT09}.
	Over binary but asymmetric channels, \cite{SRDR12,HY13}
	showed the counterpart of \cite{Arikan09,AT09}
	with the channel capacity in place of the symmetric capacity $I$.
	No further result on the LDP side,
	e.g.\ over non-binary asymmetric channels, is known.
	The technique in \cite{HY13} (\cref{ine:P<sumsum}) and \cref{sec:six} fill the gap.
	
	The CLT behavior of polar codes turns out to be difficult to characterize.
	It was Korada--Montanari--Telatar--Urbanke \cite{KMTU10} who came up with the idea
	that approximating an eigenfunction tightly bounds the eigenvalue $â„“^{-Ï±}$.
	Here $Ï±>0$ will become a number such that
	$R$ scales like $I-N^{-Ï±}$ with a fixed $ï¼°$.
	It is called the scaling exponent because it controls
	the scaling of $N$ as a function of the gap to capacity $I-R$.
	(Although the LDP regime can also be rephrased as the scaling of $N$
	as a function of $ã‘ï¼°$, it was named error exponent regime beforehand.
	Thus the name scaling exponent [regime] is dedicated to the CLT regime).
	They had $0.2669â‰¤Ï±â‰¤0.2841$ over binary erasure channels (BECs).
	The upper bound was brought down to $3.553Ï±â‰¤1$ over
	binary-input discrete-output memoryless channels (BDMCs) \cite{GHU12}.
	Hassini--Alishahi--Urbanke \cite{HAU14} moved down the upper bound
	to $3.627Ï±â‰¤1$ over BECs and $3.579Ï±â‰¤1$ over BDMCs.
	They also proved a lower bound $1â‰¤6Ï±$ over BDMCs.
	The latter is suboptimal and \cite{GB14,MHU16}
	improved the bound to $1â‰¤5.702Ï±$ and to $1â‰¤4.714Ï±$.
	Additive white Gaussian noise channles (AWGNCs) have continuous
	output alphabet, but \cite{FT17} show that they have $1â‰¤4.714Ï±$ too.
	Over BECs particularly, \cite{FV14,YFV19} examined a series of larger kernels;
	the current record is a $64Ã—64$ kernel believed to have $1â‰¤2.9Ï±$.
	Near the end of the road to $2Ï±<1$, \cite{PU16} showed that by allowing $qâ†’âˆ$,
	Reed--Solomon kernels achieve $2Ï±<1$ over $q$-ary channels.
	This does not really prove that polar codes achieve $2Ï±<1$
	over any specific channel, but gave hopes.
	Fazeli--Hassani--Mondelli--Vardy \cite{FHMV17,FHMV18,FHMV20}, eventually,
	showed that large random kernels achieve $2Ï±<1$ over BECs, breaking the barrier.
	Guruswami--Riazanov--Ye \cite{GRY19,GRY20} extended their result
	to all BDMCs utilizing the dynamic kernel technique.
	Our paper \cite{Hypotenuse21} fills the gap.
	
	Between LDP and CLT is polar coding's MDP behavior.
	First, Guruswami--Xia \cite{GX13,GX15} showed that there exists $Ï>0$ such that
	$ï¼°$ scales like $\exp(-N^{0.49})$ while $R$ scales like $I-N^{-Ï}$ over BDMCs.
	This raised a question about what are the possible pairs $(Ï€,Ï)$
	such that $(ï¼°,R)$ scales like $(\exp(-N^Ï€),I-N^{-Ï})$.
	Mondelli--Hassani--Urbanke \cite{MHU16} answered this,
	partially, in the same paper they bounded $Ï$.
	They showed that under a certain curve connecting $(0,1/5.714)$ and
	$(1/2,0)$ all $(Ï€,Ï)$ are achievable over BDMCs.
	For BECs the upper left corner is $(0,1/4.627)$.
	A straightforward generalization to AWGNCs was also given in \cite{FT17}.
	We in \cite{ModerateDeviations18,LargeDeviations18} improved their result,
	suggesting that via a combinatorial trick the upper left corner
	of the curve is $(0,Ï±)$ for any $Ï±$ that is valid in the CLT regime.
	The same trick also implicated that over BECs all $(Ï€,Ï)$
	such that $Ï€+2Ï<1$ are achievable, which is mainly owing to
	\cite{FHMV17}'s result that $2Ï<1$ over BECs is achievable.
	Meanwhile, \cite{BGNRS18} made the first step to investigate
	the general kernel matrices over general prime-ary channels.
	They showed that it is possible to achieve $Ï>0$ with $ï¼°â‰ˆN^{-Î©(1)}$.
	This is, strictly speaking, ``only'' a CLT behavior as
	the desired block error probability in the MDP world is $\exp(-N^Ï€)$.
	Later, BÅ‚asiok--Guruswami--Sudan \cite{BGS18} were able to show that
	for all $Ï€<\Ec(G)$ there exists $Ï>0$ such that $(Ï€,Ï)$ is achievable.
	This makes it a direct generalization of \cite{GX13}
	to all polarizing kernel matrices $G$ over all prime-ary channels.
	The preprint \cite{GRY20} contains a section that pushes the conference
	abstract \cite{GRY19} to positive $Ï€$ while maintaining $Ïâ‰ˆ1/2$.
	Over the general DMCs, our \cite{Hypotenuse21} fills the gap.
	
	See \cref{fig:triangle} for a comprehensive plot of all these results.
	
	\begin{figure}
		\def\cp#1:#2:{coordinate[pin=#1:{#2}](X)}
		\tikz[scale=8,nodes={black,align=center}]{
			\draw[->](0,0)--(1.1,0)node[right]{$Ï€$};

				plot[domain=30:55,samples=75]({sin(\x)^2},{1-h2o(\x)})
			;

				(.45,.55)edge[dashed](.4,.6)--(1,0)

		}
		\caption{
			Recent works on polar coding arranged on a $Ï$--$Ï€$ plot.
			Note that results utilizing different kernels
			over various channels are mixed.
			The higher $Ï,Ï€$, the better performance.
		}\label{fig:triangle}
	\end{figure}
	
	In \Cref{cha:origin}, I presented the main contribution of
	\cite{ModerateDeviations18} which, to be more precise,
	is an interpolating result $(ï¼°,R)â‰ˆ(\exp(-N^Ï€),C-N^{-Ï})$ for pairs $(Ï€,Ï)$
	lying in the region $ğ’ª$ that touches $(0,1/4.714)$ and $(1/2,0)$.
	This is the result I want to generalize in this chapter.
	Id est, I want to characterize the region of $Ï€,Ï$
	for any invertible matrix $G$ over any finite field $ğ”½_q$.
	And this region will be determined by the best $Ï±>0$ one can find (or believe in)
	plus the coset distance profile $D_Zï¼·j$ and $D_Sï¼·j$.
	
	I will do this step-by-step.
	First, I will show that most kernels enjoy a (very weak) CLT behavior.
	More precisely, kernels that satisfy a certain ergodic precondition
	will enjoy an eigen behavior with positive $Ï±$.
	After that, we either stick to the weak but provable $Ï±$ or assume
	a higher $Ï±$ based on experiments, simulations, and/or heuristics.
	And then we go through an ergodic--eigen--en23--een13--elpin that resembles
	the eigen--en23--een13--elpin chain in \cref{cha:origin,cha:dual}.

\section{An Ergodicity Precondition}\label{sec:ergodic}

	\def\nabla{\tikz\draw(2em/3,0)|-(0,2em/3)--cycle;}
	
	Before we board the long proof train eigen--en23--een13--elpin,
	I want to recall the classification of matrix kernels into two groups.
	The bad group consists of matrices that do nothing to the channels, and hence get
	no chance to polarize channels, let alone enjoying any LDP, CLT, or MDP behavior.
	The good group consists of matrices that can polarize channels in a calculus regard.
	And then in the next section I will show that all of them
	enjoy some LDP, CLT, and MDP behaviors.
	This strengthens the dichotomy even further---a matrix is either not altering
	the channels at all, or polarizing the channels exponentially fast.
	
	To motivate the classification, recall that channels $Wï¼·j$
	are synthesized based on the matrix-multiplication $Xâ‚^â„“=Uâ‚^â„“G$.
	The kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ is said to be ergodic if it mixes/blends the content $Uâ‚^â„“$
	such that there are nontrivial relations between each $U_j$ and all of $Yâ‚^â„“$.
	The following two counterexamples demonstrate the necessity of this condition.
	
	\begin{exa}
		Consider any prime power $q$ and any $â„“â‰¥2$.
		If $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ is an upper triangular matrix with $1$'s on the diagonal,
		then $Xâ‚^â„“=Uâ‚^â„“G$ is such that $X_j-U_j$ is a linear combination of $Uâ‚^{j-1}$.
		Therefore, when we want to guess $U_j$ given $Yâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1}$,
		it suffices to guess $X_j$ based on $Y_j$ and then
		subtract the correction term $X_j-U_j=Uâ‚^â„“G=Uâ‚^{j-1}0_j^â„“G$.
		That is to say, we are facing essentially
		the same guessing job as if we were facing $W$;
		the channel transformation does no benefit to us.
		In this case, $H(ğ˜_n)$ stays where $H(W)$ is
		and does not polarize to $\{0,1\}$.
		Lesson:
		$G$ must not be upper triangular.
		In fact, it can be shown that if two matrices $G$ and $ËœG$
		differ by some upper triangular row-operations, i.e., $ËœG=âˆ‡G$,
		then they share the same polarizing ability.
	\end{exa}
	
	\begin{exa}
		Consider $q=4$ and $G=\loll$.
		Let $W$ be the independent product of BEC$(1/3)$ and BEC$(2/3)$.
		That is, $W$ takes a pair $(x',x'')âˆˆğ”½â‚‚Â²$ as an input and then outputs
		\[\cas{
			(x',x'')	&	w.p.\ $2/9$,	\\
			(?,x'')		&	w.p.\ $1/9$,	\\
			(x',?)		&	w.p.\ $4/9$,	\\
			(?,?)		&	w.p.\ $2/9$.	
		}\]
		Let $câˆˆğ”½â‚„ã€ğ”½â‚‚$ be a non-binary element,
		and identify each $(x',x'')âˆˆğ”½â‚‚Â²$ with $xâ‰”x'+cx''âˆˆğ”½â‚„$, then
		$W$ behaves like a channel with input alphabet $ğ”½â‚„$, and
		\[\bma{xâ‚'&xâ‚‚'}+c\bma{xâ‚''&xâ‚‚''}=\bma{xâ‚&xâ‚‚}
			=\bma{uâ‚&uâ‚‚}G=\bma{uâ‚'&uâ‚‚'}G+c\bma{uâ‚''&uâ‚‚''}G.\]
		In plain English, $G$ multiplies the prime component
		and the double-prime component separately.
		Now we attempt to use $G$ to polarize $W$.
		Doing that is equivalent to polarizing BEC$(1/3)$ and BEC$(2/3)$ separately.
		Then, with probability $1/3$, the entropy process $\{H(ğ˜_n)\}$ converges to
		$1/2$ because the prime component converges to the entirely noisy channel
		but the double-prime component converges to the completely reliable channel.
		Lesson: $G$ needs to bring interaction to
		the vector space substructure within a prime-power finite field.
	\end{exa}
	
	The two examples motivate the following definition and theorem
	for classifying and prejudging matrices.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		For any invertible matrix $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$,
		the \emph{lowered form} of $G$ is the lower triangular matrix $ËœG$
		with $1$'s on the diagonal and such that $ËœGG^{-1}$ is upper triangular.
		A matrix $G$ is said to be \emph{ergodic} if
		the off-diagonal entries of $ËœG$ generate $ğ”½_q$ as an $ğ”½_p$-algebra;
		or $ğ”½_q=ğ”½_p[ËœG]$ for short.
	\end{dfn}
	
	\begin{thm}[Ergodic kernel polarizes]\cite[Theorem 14]{MT14}\label{thm:ergodic}
		Let $W$ be a $q$-ary channel.
		The matrix kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ is ergodic iff
		$\{H(ğ˜_n)\}$ converges to $0$ or $1$ almost surely.
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}[Sketch of the proof in \cite{MT14}]
		The stated theorem is strong and general, handling all edge cases.
		The majority of its proof involves reducing some general situation
		(e.g., $â„“â‰¥2$) to a special case (e.g., $â„“=2$) to ease notational burdens.
		I pick out what I think is the key part of the proof.
		
		First, we know that a bounded martingale will almost always converge,
		which implies $H(ğ˜_n)-H(ğ˜_{n+1})â†’0$.
		So all we need to show is that when $H(ğ˜_n)$ starts slowing down,
		i.e., when $\abs{H(ğ˜_n)-H(ğ˜_{n+1})}$ is small,
		$ğ˜_n$ will be either very reliable or very noisy.
		To put in another way, our goal is to prove
		$H(ğ˜_n)$ only slows down when it is reaching $0$ or $1$.
		Then we can conclude that the limit of $H(ğ˜_n)$ is either $0$ or $1$.
		
		To show that $ğ˜_n$ is not mediocre when $\abs{H(ğ˜_n)-H(ğ˜_{n+1})}$ is small,
		the subscript Bhattacharyya parameter $Z_d(W)$ is introduced to inspect
		the relation between $W(xï½œy)$ and $W(x+dï½œy)$, for any $dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—$.
		Now follow the recipe below to show $ğ˜_n$ is extreme:
		\begin{itemize}
			\item	Show that if $\abs{H(W)-H(Wï¼·j)}$ is small, then
					$\abs{H(W)-H(W^{[c]})}$ is small for some
					simpler channel transformation $â€¢^{[c]}$,
					where $c$ is any nonzero entry of $ËœG$.
			\item	Show that if $\abs{H(W)-H(W^{[c]})}$ is small, then
					$Z_{cd}(W)(1-Z_d(W))$ is small for all $dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—$.
			\item	Show that if $Z_d(W)$ is small, then
					$Z_{cd}(W)$ is small for any nonzero entry $c$ of $ËœG$.
					Or, if $Z_d(W)$ is close to $1$, then
					$Z_{cd}(W)$ is close to $1$ for any nonzero entry $c$ of $ËœG$.
					This step is the scalar-multiplication part
					of $ğ”½_p[ËœG]$ as an $ğ”½_p$-algebra.
			\item	Show that if both $Z_c(W)$ and $Z_d(W)$ are small, then
					$Z_{c+d}(W)$ is small for any entries $c$ and $d$ of $ËœG$.
					Similarly, if both $Z_c(W)$ and $Z_d(W)$ are close to $1$, then
					$Z_{c+d}(W)$ is close to $1$ for any $c,dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—$.
					This step is the addition part of $ğ”½_p[ËœG]$ as an $ğ”½_p$-algebra.
			\item	Show that if $\{Z_d(W):dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—\}$ are all small,
					then $W$ is very reliable.
					Otherwise, if $\{Z_d(W):dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—\}$ are all close to $1$,
					then $W$ is very noisy.
		\end{itemize}
		
		The key to the first $â€¢$ is to simplify the given premise
		$\abs{H(W)-H(Wï¼·j)}<Îµ$ concerning $ËœG$ to a condition
		$\abs{H(W)-H(W^{[c]})}<â„“Îµ$ concerning some $2Ã—2$ matrix, where
		\[W^{[c]}(yâ‚yâ‚‚uâ‚ï½œuâ‚‚)â‰”Ã·12W(yâ‚ï½œuâ‚+cuâ‚‚)W(yâ‚‚ï½œuâ‚‚),\]
		for any nonzero entry $c$ of $ËœG$.
		Note that $W^{[c]}$ represents the guessing job
		of $Uâ‚‚$ given $Yâ‚Yâ‚‚Uâ‚$ and the matrix kernel $\locl$.
		To simplify the premise, let $(j,i)$ points to a non-zero entry $c$ of $ËœG$.
		By how $U_i,U_j,X_i,X_j$ are related by $c$, one can prove
		\[0â‰¤H(W)-H(W^{[c]})â‰¤(â„“-j+1)H(W)-âˆ‘_{k=j}^â„“H(Wï¼·k).\]
		In layman's terms, the extra amount of information $W^{[c]}$ can steal from $W$
		is at most the amount that was stolen by $Wï¼·j,Wï¼·{j+1}â€¦Wï¼·{â„“}$ from $W^{â„“-j+1}$.
		Therefore, the difference $\abs{H(ğ˜_n)-H(ğ˜_n^{[c]})}$ converges to $0$.
		
		The key to the second and third $â€¢$ is to show
		$f(Z_{cd}(W)(1-Z_d(W)))â‰¤H(W)-H(W^{[c]})$ for all $dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—$,
		where $f$ is some monotonically increasing function that passes $(0,0)$.
		Once this is done, we have that
		either $Z_{cd}(ğ˜_n)$ is small or $Z_d(ğ˜_n)$ is close to $1$.
		If small $Z_{cd}(ğ˜_n)$ is the case,
		then we obtain small $Z_{cÂ²d}(ğ˜_n)$ when plugging in $d=cd$;
		we further obtain small $Z_{cÂ³d}(ğ˜_n)$ when plugging in $d=cÂ²d$;
		and so on.
		A similar argument applies if we choose close-to-$1$ $Z_d(ğ˜_n)$---%
		we will obtain close-to-$1$ $Z_{d/c}(ğ˜_n)$, $Z_{d/cÂ²}(ğ˜_n)$, etc.
		
		The fourth $â€¢$ is by an independent inequality.
		And it implies that â€œbeing smallâ€ and â€œbeing close to $1$â€
		are properties that can propagate among $Z_d(ğ˜_n)$ for distinct $d$'s.
		The first four $â€¢$'s together imply that either
		all of $\{Z_d(W):dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—\}$ are small or all of them are close to $1$.
		The last $â€¢$ is just some HÃ¶lder tolls that connect the fact that
		all $Z_d(W)$ are small with the fact that $H(W)$ is small, and vice versa.
		And the HÃ¶lder tolls imply that $ğ˜_n$ is extreme.
		This finishes the sketch of the proof.
	\end{proof}
	
	Remark on the theorem:
	Knowing that $\{H(ğ˜_n)\}$ converges to $0$ or $1$ says
	nothing about the pace of convergence.
	In particular, we do not even know if at least one of $H(Wï¼·j)$
	is not equal to $H(W)$---it could be that $H(ğ˜_n)$ stays unchanged
	for many $n$'s and then moves a little bit before another long relaxing.
	For instance, $1/âŒŠã‘nâŒ‹$ eventually converges to $0$ but it moves occasionally.
	
	Our next goal in this section is to extract, from the proof of \cref{thm:ergodic},
	a lemma that some $H(Wï¼·j)$ is different from $H(W)$.
	This lemma will evince that the pace of convergence
	is exponential in $n$ in the next section.
	
	\begin{lem}[Ergodic kernel perturbs]\label{lem:moving}
		Let $W$ be a $q$-ary channel.
		Let $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ be an ergodic kernel.
		Then $H(Wï¼·j)â‰ H(W)$ for some $1â‰¤jâ‰¤â„“$ unless $H(W)âˆˆ\{0,1\}$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		First and foremost, assume that $W$ is a symmetric channel with uniform input
		and that $G=ËœG$ is a lower triangular matrix.
		The former is due to a symmetrization technique that identifies
		the conditional entropy of a $q$-ary channel $W$ and its symmetric sibling $ËœW$.
		The latter is by that upper triangular row-operations do not alter
		the synthetic channels up to some equivalence relation.
		See \cite{MT14} for more details about these two reductions.
		
		Now we assume the opposite of the conclusion,
		that $H(Wï¼·j)=H(W)$ for all $1â‰¤jâ‰¤â„“$.
		Then, for any $1â‰¤jâ‰¤â„“$,
		\begin{align*}
			(â„“-j+1)H(W)
			&	=âˆ‘_{k=j}^â„“H(Wï¼·k)=âˆ‘_{k=j}^â„“H(U_kï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{k-1})
				=H(U_j^â„“ï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1})	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{k=j}^â„“H(U_kï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1}U_{k+1}^â„“)â‰¤âˆ‘_{k=j}^â„“H(W)=(â„“-j+1)H(W).
		\end{align*}
		The equality that starts the second line
		changes the order we guess $U_k$---the new order is $k=1,2â€¦j-1,â„“,â„“-1â€¦j$.
		The inequality in the second line is by that $ËœG$ is lower triangular,
		and hence guessing $U_k$ is no harder than guessing $X_k$ from $Y_k$
		follow by the subtraction of the correction term $X_k-U_k=U_{k+1}^â„“ËœG$.
		Now the inequality squeezes, so all
		$H(U_kï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1}U_{k+1}^â„“)$ are equal to $H(W)$.
		
		Plugging in $k=j$ yields, particularly, $H(U_jï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1}U_{j+1}^â„“)=H(W)$.
		That is, the last guessing job, which is supposedly the easiest,
		turns out to be as difficult as the others.
		Fix any pair $i<j$ such that the $(j,i)$th entry of $ËœG$ is $câ‰ 0$.
		Then
		\[H(W)=H(U_jï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^{j-1}U_{j+1}^â„“)â‰¤H(U_jï½œY_iY_jUâ‚^{j-1}U_{j+1}^â„“)â‰¤H(W).\]
		The first inequality is by monotonicity.
		The second inequality is by that guessing $U_j$ is no harder than
		guessing $X_j$ up to the subtraction of some correction term.
		Now the inequalities squeeze and $H(U_jï½œY_iY_jUâ‚^{j-1}U_{j+1}^â„“)=H(W)$.
		
		Now look closer at the â€œchannelâ€ $U_jï½œY_iY_jUâ‚^{j-1}U_{j+1}^â„“$.
		We get a second chance to learn more about $U_j$---since $X_i$ is a linear
		combination of $U_i^â„“$, wherein the coefficient of $U_j$ is $c$, the output
		$Y_i$ corresponding to the input $X_i$ also carries information about $U_j$.
		The way this information is carried is the same as
		the outputs corresponding to the inputs $U_i$ and $cU_i$.
		This comes down to the second synthetic channel
		of the $2Ã—2$ kernel $\locl$, i.e.
		\[W^{[c]}(yâ‚yâ‚‚uâ‚ï½œuâ‚‚)â‰”Ã·12W(yâ‚ï½œuâ‚+cuâ‚‚)W(yâ‚‚ï½œuâ‚‚).\]
		Hence $H(U_jï½œY_iY_jUâ‚^{j-1}U_{j+1}^â„“)â‰¤H(W^{[c]})â‰¤H(W)$.
		The inequalities squeeze again, $H(W^{[c]})=H(W)$.
		
		Now we cite a difficult inequality in \cite[Appendix~A]{MT14}:
		\[H(W)-H(W^{[c]})â‰¥-ã‘ï¼ˆ1-Ã·1qâˆ‘_{dâˆˆğ”½^Ã—}Z_{cd}(W)Â²(1-Z_d(W))ï¼‰â‰¥0.\]
		Squeeze one more time;
		we end up with $Z_{cd}Â²(1-Z_d)=0$, for all $dâˆˆğ”½_q^Ã—$.
		(We omit the argument â€œ$(W)$â€ from now on.)
		If it is the case that $Z_{cd}=0$, then the next factor $1-Z_{cd}$
		around the corner is $1$, which forces $Z_{cÂ²d}$ to be $0$.
		The latter in turns forces $Z_{cÂ³d}$ to be $0$.
		This argument propagates throughout the multiplicative orbit $âŸ¨câŸ©dâŠ†ğ”½_q^Ã—$.
		Similarly, if it is the case that $Z_d=1$,
		then $Z_{d/c},Z_{d/cÂ²}â€¦Z_d$ are, forcably, all $1$.
		In summary, all $Z_d$ in the orbit $âŸ¨câŸ©d$ share a common fate.
		In fact, since $c$ can be any nonzero off-diagonal entry of $ËœG$,
		we deduce that all $Z_d$ in the big orbit $âŸ¨ËœGâŸ©d$ share a common fate.
		
		The last piece of the jigsaw puzzle is to show that all $Z_d$,
		no matter which orbit they lie in, share the same common fate.
		We hereby cite \cite[Lemma~21]{MT14}:
		If $Z_d=Z_e=1$ and $d+eâ‰ 0$, then $Z_{d+e}=1$.
		Therefore, if $Z_d=1$ for some $dâˆˆğ”½_p$ in the ground field,
		then $Z_d=1$ for all $dâˆˆğ”½_p$.
		By that off-diagonal entries of $ËœG$ generate $ğ”½_q$ as an $ğ”½_p$-algebra and
		that $Z_d$'s lying in the same $ËœG$-orbit share the common fate,
		we conclude that $Z_d=1$ for all $dâˆˆğ”½_q$.
		If, otherwise, no $d$ in the ground field has $Z_d=1$,
		then all $d$ have $Z_d=0$.
		
		Now we have that all $dâˆˆğ”½_q$ share the common fate---either
		all $Z_d(W)$ are $1$ or all $Z_d(W)$ are $0$.
		So $Z(W)$ is either $1$ or $0$.
		Thus $H(W)$ is either $1$ or $0$.
		This completes the proof.
	\end{proof}
	
	We just see that either $G$ does nothing to the channel $W$
	or $G$ will synthesize a $Wï¼·j$ that has a distinct conditional entropy.
	In other words, $H(ğ˜â‚)$ is not a constant but a true random variable.
	In the next section, I will leverage the fact that $H(ğ˜â‚)$ is not constant,
	regardless how small its variance is, to show that
	$H(ğ˜â‚€),H(ğ˜â‚),H(ğ˜â‚‚),\dotsc$ polarizes in an exponential pace.

\section{Eigen Behavior}\label{sec:eigen}

	What constitutes this section is a compactness argument that aims to show that
	every ergodic kernel has a positive $Ï±$ as in the eigenvalue formula
	\[â„“^{-Ï±}â‰”\sup_{Wï¼šqâ€ -aryâ€ }Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))},
		\label{sup:dmc}\]
	where $h$ is an easy-to-handle eigenfunciton.
	From that we can conclude $ğ˜Œ[h(ğ˜_n)]<â„“^{-Ï±n}$
	and then move on to the en23 behavior.
	
	We can almost see how \cref{sup:dmc} can be estimated:
	Take a (strictly) concave $h$.
	By the last section,
	$H(Wï¼·j)$ are not equal to each other whilst their average is $H(W)$.
	By Jensen's inequality, $ğ˜Œ[h(H(ğ˜â‚))]â‰¤h(H(ğ˜â‚€))$, and the equality cannot hold.
	This means that the fraction within the supremum is strictly less than $1$.
	Should the supremum be less than $1$, a positive $Ï±$ exists.
	Now we see why \cref{sup:dmc} is nontrivial:
	The supremum of some less-than-$1$ numbers can be $1$,
	especially when the domain is not compact.
	
	The remainder of this section finds a compact subset of $q$-ary channels,
	on which the supremum is strictly less than $1$,
	and handles the supremum over the complement set separately.
	
	\begin{lem}[Eigen for mediocre channels]\label{lem:eigen-H}
		Fix a $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$.
		Fix an $h(z)â‰”âˆš{\min(z,1-z)}$.
		For any $Î´>0$,
		\[\sup_{Î´â‰¤H(W)â‰¤1-Î´}Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}<1,
			\label{sup:mediocre}\]
		where the supremum is taken over all $q$-ary $(Q,W)$-pairs
		whose conditional entropy lies within $[Î´,1-Î´]$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Approach one:
		All delta--epsilon arguments in \cref{lem:moving}
		(which is inspired by \cref{thm:ergodic}) can be made explicit.
		That will give an upper bound on \cref{sup:mediocre}.
		
		Approach two:
		It suffices to show that the space of $q$-ary channels
		with mediocre conditional entropy is sequentially compact,
		plus $â€¢ï¼·j$ and $H$ are continuous w.r.t.\ the same topology.
		Once this is done, any sequence $Wâ‚,Wâ‚‚,\dotsc$ whose corresponding fractions
		converge to $1$ must converge to the corresponding fraction of some $W_âˆ$,
		which is strictly less than $1$ and leads to a contradiction.
		
		Let the simplex $Î”^ğ’³$ be the closed subset of $[0,1]^ğ’³$
		constrained by that the sum of coordinates is $1$.
		This is the set of all probability distributions on $ğ’³$.
		Let $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$ be the set of probability distributions on $Î”^ğ’³$.
		A pair $(Q,W)$ of a DMC $W$ with an input distribution $Q$
		corresponds to a distribution on $Î”^ğ’³$, i.e. an element of $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$,
		through the posterior probabilities seen by the decoder.
		In details, whenever the decoder sees $Y=y$,
		it looks up the symbol $y$ in the table of
		posterior probabilities and learns a tuple
		\[(W(xâ‚ï½œy),W(xâ‚‚ï½œy)â€¦W(x_qï½œy)),\]
		where $xâ‚,xâ‚‚â€¦x_q$ enumerate the symbols of $ğ’³$.
		This tuple is an element of $Î”^ğ’³$.
		Now that the channel output $Y$ is a random variable,
		the tuple of posterior probabilities
		\[(W(xâ‚ï½œY),W(xâ‚‚ï½œY)â€¦W(x_qï½œY))\]
		is itself random.
		This tuple is a $Î”^ğ’³$-valued random variable
		and obeys some distribution in $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$.
		This distribution of a random tuple is
		the representative of $(Q,W)$ in $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$.
		
		Here comes the measure theory nonsense:
		Since $ğ’³$ is finite, $[0,1]^ğ’³$ and $Î”^ğ’³$
		are compact w.r.t.\ the Euclidean topology.
		So $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$, the set of all distributions on $Î”^ğ’³$, is tight.
		By Prokhorov's theorem, $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$ is sequentially compact
		w.r.t.\ the topology of weak convergence.
		Now notice that $H(W)$ is just the expectation/integral
		\[Eã€Œâˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}-W(xï½œY)ã’_qW(xï½œY)ã€=âˆ«âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}-W(xï½œy)ã’_qW(xï½œy)\,\diff y.\]
		Note that the â€œintegrateeâ€ $âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}-W(xï½œy)ã’_qW(xï½œy)â‰¤q$
		is bounded and continuous in the tuple.
		So $H$ can be extended to a continuous map from $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$ to $[0,1]$.
		By extension I mean that $H$ is now defined over all $q$-ary input channels
		regardless of whether the output alphabet is discrete or continuous.
		Similarly, all $H(Wï¼·j)$ can be written as
		more complicated expectations/integrals of $W(xï½œY)$.
		They are all continuous w.r.t.\ the topology of weak convergence.
		
		Finally, this is what happens if there exists a sequence of channels
		$Wâ‚,Wâ‚‚â€¦$ (notice the font) whose corresponding fractions
		\[Ã·{h(H(â€¢ï¼·1))+h(H(â€¢ï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(â€¢ï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(â€¢))}\]
		converge to $1$:
		Map these channels to $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$.
		A sequence in $ğ’«(Î”^ğ’³)$ must contain a convergent subsequence.
		Let $W_âˆ$ be the limit of any such subsequence.
		Then, as $W_âˆ$ also satisfies \cref{lem:moving},
		\[Ã·{h(H(W_âˆï¼·1))+h(H(W_âˆï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(W_âˆï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W_âˆ))}<1.\]
		(In particular, the denominator is $â‰¥h(Î´)=âˆšÎ´$.)
		A contradiction.
		This means the supremum is strictly less than $1$ and is what I calimed.
		
		This proof is partially inspired by \cite{Nasser18c,Nasser18t}.
	\end{proof}
	
	The case of mediocre channels is done.
	It remains to upper bound the supremum for
	channels in the neighborhoods of $H=0$ and $H=1$.
	First goes the neighborhood of $H=0$.
	
	\begin{lem}[Eigen for reliable channels]\label{lem:eigen-Z}
		Fix a $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ with coset distance $D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥5$ for some $j$,
		that is, the Hamming distance from some row to
		the subspace spanned by the rows below is $5$ or farther.
		Fix an $h(z)â‰”âˆš{\min(z,1-z)}$.
		Then there exists an $Î´$ such that
		\[\sup_{0<H(W)<Î´}Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}<1,
			\label{sup:good}\]
		where the supremum is taken over all $q$-ary $(Q,W)$-pairs
		whose conditional entropy lies within the interval $(0,Î´)$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Let us assume that it is the last row of $G$ that has Hamming weight $5$.
		For if $\hdis(r_j,R_j)â‰¥5$ is satisfied with other index $j<â„“$,
		the following proof works with minor modifications.
		
		Temporarily let $Î´$ be $H(W)$ and assume that this is really small.
		This paragraph bounds $Îµâ‰•H(Wï¼·{â„“})$ from above:
		Pay the explicit HÃ¶lder toll (\cref{pro:ex-toll}),
		we obtain $ï¼º(W)<qÂ³âˆš{H(W)}=qÂ³âˆšÎ´$.
		Apply FTPC$Z$ (\cref{thm:ftpcZ}), we see that
		$ï¼º(Wï¼·{â„“})â‰¤f_Zï¼·{â„“}(ï¼º(W))=ï¼º(W)âµ<q^{15}Î´^{2.5}$.
		Pay the HÃ¶lder toll for the return-trip (\cref{pro:ex-toll}),
		we arrive at $Îµ=H(Wï¼·{â„“})<qÂ³âˆš{ï¼º(Wï¼·{â„“})}<q^{10.5}Î´^{1.25}$,
		or $Îµ<q^{10.5}Î´^{1.25}$ for short.
		This is the only $H(Wï¼·j)$ I know how to estimate.
		
		Look at the fraction in \cref{sup:good}.
		The last term in the numerator is $h(Îµ)â‰¤âˆšÎµ<âˆš{q^{10.5}Î´^{1.25}}$.
		We do not know about the other terms in the numerator;
		but at least we can apply Jenson's inequality
		(i.e., we assume they are all equal to minimize the extent of polarization)
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“-1}))	\\
			&	â‰¤(â„“-1)hï¼ˆÃ·{H(Wï¼·1)+H(Wï¼·2)+\dotsb+H(Wï¼·{â„“-1})}{â„“-1}ï¼‰\\
			&	=(â„“-1)hï¼ˆÃ·{â„“Î´-Îµ}{â„“-1}ï¼‰â‰¤(â„“-1)âˆš{Ã·{â„“Î´-Îµ}{â„“-1}}=âˆš{â„“-1}âˆš{â„“Î´-Îµ}<âˆš{(â„“-1)â„“Î´}.
		\end{align*}
		The first equality is by FTPC$H$ (\cref{thm:ftpcH}).
		
		Now the fraction in \cref{sup:good} has
		its numerator simplified down to two terms:
		\[â€ \cref{sup:good}â€ â‰¤Ã·{âˆš{(â„“-1)â„“Î´}+âˆšÎµ}{â„“âˆšÎ´}
			=âˆš{Ã·{â„“-1}{â„“}}+Ã·1{â„“}âˆš{Ã·{Îµ}{Î´}}\label{ine:1small}\]
		If we let $Î´â†’0$, then $Îµ/Î´â‰¤q^{10.5}Î´^{1.25}/Î´=q^{10.5}Î´^{0.25}â†’0$.
		So there is a positive $Î´$ such that
		the right-hand side of \cref{ine:1small} is strictly less than $1$.
		This completes the proof.
	\end{proof}
	
	Next goes the neighborhood of $H=1$.
	But it is just the dual of the previous lemma.
	
	\begin{lem}[Eigen for noisy channels]\label{lem:eigen-S}
		Fix a $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ with dual coset distance $D_Sï¼·jâ‰¥5$ for some $j$,
		that is, the Hamming distance from some column of $G^{-1}$ to
		the subspace spanned by the column to the left is $5$ or farther.
		Fix $h(z)â‰”âˆš{\min(z,1-z)}$.
		Then there exists an $Î´$ such that
		\[\sup_{1-Î´<H(W)<1}Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}<1,\]
		where the supremum is taken over all $q$-ary $(Q,W)$-pairs
		whose conditional entropy lies within the interval $(1-Î´,1)$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		The proof is merely the dual of the proof of \cref{lem:eigen-Z}.
		We shall not repeat.
	\end{proof}
	
	\Cref{lem:eigen-H,lem:eigen-Z,lem:eigen-S} together imply that,
	if $G$ and $G^{-1}$ have large Hamming distances among their rows and columns,
	respectively, then \cref{sup:dmc} is strictly less than $1$.
	However, this does not imply anything about matrices with shorter distances,
	especially when $G$ is $4Ã—4$ or smaller.
	Thankfully, Kronecker product (tensor product) leverages the Hamming distances.
	
	\begin{lem}[Thress steps as one big step]\label{lem:cube}
		Fix any ergodic matrix $Kâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$.
		Its cubic Kronecker power $Gâ‰”K^{âŠ—3}$
		(a)	is ergodic,
		(b)	has some $D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥8$, and
		(c)	has some $D_Sï¼·jâ‰¥8$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		For (a):
		It suffices to consider the Kronecker power of the lowered form $ËœK$.
		Since the diagonal of $ËœK$ is all $1$, any Kronecker power $ËœK^{âŠ—n}$
		keeps a copy of the original $ËœK$ around the diagonal.
		Use the original copies to generate $ğ”½_q$ as an $ğ”½_p$-algebra.
		
		For (b):
		The proof is the combination of two simple facts:
		\begin{itemize}
			\item	An ergodic matrix must have some coset distance $â‰¥2$.
			\item	Let $(u,U)$ be a vector--subspace pair of some ambient vector space
					$ğ•Œ$ and $(v,V)$ a vector--subspace pair of another ambient
					vector space $ğ•$, then $\hdis(uâŠ—v,uâŠ—V+UâŠ—ğ•)â‰¥\hdis(u,U)\*\hdis(v,V)$.
		\end{itemize}
		The first bullet point is again a consequence of \cref{thm:ergodic}.
		To elaborate, note that $K$ and and its lowered form $ËœK$
		share the same coset distance profile.
		Then note that $ËœK$ has at least one off-diagonal entry that is nonzero.
		Let $j$ be the last row with nonzero off-diagonal entry, then $D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥2$.
		Hence $K$ has some coset distance $â‰¥2$.
		The second bullet point is worth a standalone lemma
		and will be proved as \cref{lem:tensor}.
		The two bullet points imply that
		the $(jâ„“Â²+jâ„“+j)$th coset distance of $Gâ‰”K^{âŠ—3}$ is at least $8$.
		
		For (c):
		It is the dual of (b).
		The proof ends here.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{lem}\label{lem:tensor}
		Let $(u,U)$ be a vector--subspace pair of some ambient vector space $ğ•Œ$
		and $(v,V)$ a vector--subspace pair of another ambient vector space $ğ•$,
		then it holds that $\hdis(uâŠ—v,uâŠ—V+UâŠ—ğ•)â‰¥\hdis(u,U)\hdis(v,V)$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		View $u$, $v$ and $uâŠ—v$ as a
		column vector, a row vector, and a rank-$1$ matrix, respectively.
		Now we want to compute the Hamming distance
		from the matrix $uâŠ—v$ to a subset of matrices $uâŠ—V+UâŠ—ğ•$.
		This is equal to the Hamming distance from $uâŠ—(v+V)$ to $UâŠ—ğ•$.
		Let $v'âˆˆv+V$ be any row vector (whose Hamming weight is at least $\hdis(v,V)$).
		It suffices to prove $\hdis(uâŠ—v',UâŠ—ğ•)â‰¥\hdis(u,U)\hwt(v')$.
		
		Without loss of generality, assume that the first coordinate of $v'$ is nonzero.
		Then the first column of $uâŠ—v'$ is a nonzero multiple of $u$,
		whilst the first column of any matrix in $UâŠ—ğ•$ is a column vector in $U$.
		The number of mismatching entries in the first column is thus $â‰¥\hdis(u,U)$.
		Repeat this argument for all columns where $v'$ is nonzero,
		then the total number of mismatching entries is
		$â‰¥\hdis(u,U)\hwt(v')â‰¥\hdis(u,U)\hdis(v,V)$.
		This is what we want to show.
	\end{proof}
	
	Remark:
	There is no practical reason to use a large matrix
	with poor coset distance profile to polarize channels.
	What \cref{lem:cube} is good for is to prove that
	easy-to-implement $2Ã—2$ matrices such as
	\[\bma{
		1	&	0	\\
		c	&	1	
	}\]
	polarize channels, given that $c$ generates $ğ”½_q$ over $ğ”½_p$.
	
	Recap of this section:
	Up to this point, we have seen that
	an ergodic kernel $G$ will make $H(ğ˜â‚)$ non-constant,
	that the eigenvalue is $<1$ for mediocre channels,
	that the eigenvalue is $<1$ for reliable channels given $D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥5$,
	that the eigenvalue is $<1$ for noisy channels given $D_Sï¼·jâ‰¥5$,
	and that the third Kronecker power $K^{âŠ—3}$ has sufficient coset distances.
	These result in $K^{âŠ—3}$ having eigenvalue (i.e., \cref{sup:dmc}) $<1$.
	The next theorem summarizes the eigen behavior of any ergodic kernel.
	
	\begin{thm}[From ergodic to eigen]\label{thm:dmc-eigen}
		If $Kâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ is an ergodic matrix, then $Gâ‰”K^{âŠ—3}$ is a kernel such that
		\[\sup_{0<H(W)<1}Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“Â³}))}{â„“Â³h(H(W))}<1,\]
		where the supremum is taken over all $q$-ary channels
		that are not completely noisy or entirely reliable.
	\end{thm}
	
	The next section will take advantage of the eigen theorem to show that
	every ergodic kernel enjoys an en23 behavior with positive $Ï±$.


\section{En23 Behavior}\label{sec:en23}

	Fix a $q$-ary $W$ and an ergodic $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$.
	The latter could be a large matrix with sufficient coset distances
	or the cubic power of any ergodic matrix.
	Let the entropy process $\{ğ˜_n\}$ be defined by $ğ˜_nâ‰”H(ğ˜_n)$,
	where $\{ğ˜_n\}$ is the channel process grown from $W$ via $G$.
	Let the Bhattacharyya process $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$ be defined by $ğ˜¡_nâ‰”ï¼º(ğ˜_n)$,
	We want to understand the asymptotic behavior of $\{ğ˜_n\}$ and $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$.
	
	To get the asymptotic behavior, \cref{lem:Z-en23} provides
	a template to translate an eigen behavior into an en23 behavior.
	The only difference is that there, the eigen behavior was given in terms of $Z$,
	and here, the eigen behavior is given in terms of $H$.
	This turns out to be ineffective to the proof;
	in fact, an eigen behavior can be given in terms of
	any parameter that is bi-HÃ¶lder to $H$ and $Z$.
	Beyond this, there is nothing new to comment on.
	Let us go straight toward the lemma.
	
	\begin{lem}[From eigen to en23]\label{lem:dmc-en23}
		If a kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ and a concave function $h$
		are such that $h(0)=h(1)=0$ and
		\[\sup_{0<H(W)<1}Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}
			=â„“^{-Ï±},\]
		then
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜¡_n<e^{-n^{2/3}}ï½>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Telescope
		$ğ˜Œ[h(ğ˜_n)]â‰¤ğ˜Œ[ğ˜Œ[h(ğ˜_n)ï½œğ˜‘â‚ğ˜‘â‚‚\dotsmğ˜‘_{n-1}]]â‰¤ğ˜Œ[h(ğ˜_{n-1})]â„“^{-Ï±}â‰¤â„“^{-Ï±n}$.
		So $ğ˜_n$ refuses to stay around the middle
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			ğ˜—ï½›e^{-n^{3/4}}â‰¤ğ˜_nâ‰¤1-e^{-n^{3/4}}ï½=ğ˜—ï½›h(ğ˜_n)â‰¥h\(e^{-n^{3/4}}\)ï½	\\
			&	â‰¤Ã·{ğ˜Œ[h(ğ˜_n)]}{h(\exp(-n^{3/4}))}â‰¤Ã·{h(ğ˜â‚€)â„“^{-Ï±n}}{h(\exp(-n^{3/4}))}
				<Ã·{â„“^{-Ï±n}}{\exp(-n^{3/4})}<â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}.
		\end{align*}
		Next, recall that $ğ˜_nâ†’ğ˜_âˆâˆˆ\{0,1\}$ and $ğ˜—\{ğ˜_nâ†’0\}=1-ğ˜_0=1-H(W)$.
		So
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜_n<e^{-n^{3/4}}ï½â‰¥ğ˜—\{ğ˜_nâ†’0\}-ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜_mâ†’0â€  but â€ ğ˜_nâ‰¥e^{-n^{3/4}}ï½	\\
			&	=1-H(W)-ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜_mâ€  will visit â€ ï¼»e^{-m^{3/4}},1-e^{-m^{3/4}}ï¼½â€ 
				for some â€ mâ‰¥nï½	\\
			&	>1-H(W)-âˆ‘_{m=n}^âˆâ„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}=1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}.
		\end{align*}
		Now pay the HÃ¶lder toll to translate the inequality into one about $ğ˜¡_n$.
		That is, $ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_n<\exp(-n^{2/3})\}>1-H(W)-â„“^{Ï±n-o(n)}$.
		That is exactly the inequality we want.
	\end{proof}
	
	Before the section ends, I want to comment on
	possible sources of the eigen behavior.
	As one can see in \cref{cha:origin},
	the eigen behavior for BDMC and $\loll$ was given in terms of $Z$
	because it is easier to bound $Z$ back then (cf.\ \cref{lem:squares}).
	Here, the eigen behavior is given in terms of $H$ because it forms a martingale,
	and Jensen's inequality indicates that the eigenvalue is $â‰¤1$.
	In the next chapter, readers will see $H$ again for the same reason.
	In general, eigen behavior can be stated in any parameter such that
	$\textsf{YourParameter}_nâ‰¤\exp(-n^{3/4})$ implies $ğ˜¡_nâ‰¤\exp(-n^{2/3})$;
	because then the proof given above works.
	
	In the next section, we will go one step further to the een13 behavior
	that will be built on top of this section's en23 behavior,
	which was built on top of last section's ergodic behavior.
	The proof in the next section assumes the same format as that in \cref{lem:Z-een13}.

\section{Een13 Behavior}\label{sec:een13}

	The derivation of the een13 behavior from the en23 behavior uses
	the fact that $ï¼º(Wï¼·j)$ is about $ï¼º(W)$ to the power of ${D_Zï¼·j}$.
	By that an ergodic kernel has nontrivial coset distance profile,
	at least one $D_Zï¼·j$ will square the $ï¼º(W)$.
	Now it is a matter of Hoeffding's inequality to control
	the frequency that a trajectory of $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$ undergoes squaring.
	
	To facilitate the reasoning about coset distances,
	define the distance process $\{ğ˜‹_n\}$ via $ğ˜‹_nâ‰”D_Zï¼·{ğ˜‘_n}$.
	Then, by FTPC$Z$,
	$ğ˜¡_{n+1}â‰¤q^{â„“-ğ˜‘_{n+1}}ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}}â‰¤q^â„“ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}}â‰ˆğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}}$.
	And now we can telescope and talk about the product $ğ˜‹â‚ğ˜‹â‚‚\dotsmğ˜‹_n$.
	For instance, the product is $1$ with probability $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}^n$.
	This is the probability that $ğ˜¡â‚€$--$ğ˜¡_n$ do not undergo any squaring
	or higher powering, and we have no control on such $ğ˜¡_n$
	(except the trivial one $ğ˜¡_nâ‰¤q^{(â„“-1)^n}ğ˜¡â‚€$).
	Hence, I hope that $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}^n$ is dominated by
	the desired gap to capacity $â„“^{-Ï±n}$.
	Equivalently, I hope that $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}â‰¤â„“^{-Ï±}$.
	
	\begin{con}[Good $D$ implies good $Ï±$]
		For any kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$,
		\[ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}<â„“^{-Ï±}.\]
	\end{con}
	
	Even if $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}â‰¥â„“^{-Ï±}$,
	we can still re-choose a lower $Ï±>0$ to satisfy $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}<â„“^{-Ï±}$.
	This is one of the two new issues, in contrast to \cref{cha:origin},
	that we need to take care of in this section.
	The other new issue is that $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$ is no longer a supermartingale.
	
	\begin{lem}[Artificial supermartingale]\label{lem:artifact}
		For any $Îµ>0$, there exist a smaller $Îµ>0$ and a small $Î´>0$
		such that $\{ğ˜¡_n^Îµâˆ§Î´\}$ is a supermartingale.
		Here $ğ˜¡_n^Îµâˆ§Î´$ is a shorthand for $\min(ğ˜¡_n^Îµ,Î´)$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		It suffices to pick $Îµ$ and $Î´$ such that $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡â‚^Îµâˆ§Î´]â‰¤ğ˜¡â‚€^Îµâˆ§Î´$ for any $W$.
		If $ğ˜¡â‚€^Îµâ‰¥Î´$, there is nothing to prove;
		thus we may assume that $ğ˜¡â‚€^Îµ<Î´$.
		Start from $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}<â„“^{-Ï±}<1$.
		Then either $q^{â„“Îµ}ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}<1$ or
		we can reselect a smaller $Îµ>0$ to make it true.
		We then choose a small $Î´>0$ such that $q^{â„“Îµ}\*ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}+q^{â„“Îµ}Î´ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚â‰¥2\}â‰¤1$.
		Now upper bound the conditional expectation by handling the two cases:
		\begin{align*}
			ğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡â‚^Îµâˆ§Î´]
			&	â‰¤(q^â„“ğ˜¡â‚€)^Îµğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}+(q^â„“ğ˜¡â‚€Â²)^Îµğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚â‰¥2\}	\\
			&	=ğ˜¡â‚€^Îµ(q^{â„“Îµ}ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}+q^{â„“Îµ}ğ˜¡â‚€^Îµğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚â‰¥2\})â‰¤ğ˜¡â‚€^ÎµÂ·1=ğ˜¡â‚€^Îµâˆ§Î´.
		\end{align*}
		This finishes the proof of $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡â‚^Îµâˆ§Î´]â‰¤ğ˜¡â‚€$.
		By the tree structure of the process,
		$ğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡_{n+1}ï½œğ˜‘â‚ğ˜‘â‚‚\dotsmğ˜‘_n]$ versus $ğ˜¡_n$ is just $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜¡â‚]$ vs $ğ˜¡â‚€$ with $Wâ†ğ˜_n$.
		This completes the proof of the whole lemma.
	\end{proof}
	
	Remark:
	The choice of $Î´$ in the lemma implies that $q^{â„“Îµ}Î´$ is less than
	$(1-q^{â„“Îµ}ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\})/ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚â‰¥2\}<1$, or equivalently $q^{â„“Îµ}â‰¤Î´^{-1}$.
	Thus $ğ˜¡_{n+1}â‰¤q^â„“ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}}â‰¤Î´^{-1/Îµ}ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}}â‰¤ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}-Îµ}$
	whenever $ğ˜¡_n^Îµ<Î´$.
	In other words, when we look at $ğ˜¡_n^Îµ$ in the â€œsafe zoneâ€ $[0,Î´]$,
	not only is $ğ˜¡_n^Îµ$ a supermartingale, but FTPC$Z$ also takes a simpler form
	$ğ˜¡_{n+1}â‰¤ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}-Îµ}â‰¤ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}(1-Îµ)}$ that facilitates telescoping.
	
	The main statement of the een13 behavior follows.
	
	\begin{lem}[From en23 to een13]\label{lem:dmc-een13}
		Given $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}<â„“^{-Ï±}$ and \cref{lem:dmc-en23}, that is, given
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜¡_n<e^{-n^{2/3}}ï½>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)},\]
		we have
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜¡_n<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)ï½>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}.\label{ine:dmc-een13}\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		(Select constants.)
		Since $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚=1\}<â„“^{-Ï±}$,
		there exists a large $Î»>1$ such that $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹â‚^{-Î»}]<â„“^{-Ï±}$.
		Pick a small $Îµ>0$ such that $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹â‚^{-Î»}]8^{ÎµÎ»}<â„“^{-Ï±}$.
		Invoke \cref{lem:artifact};
		pick a smaller $Îµ>0$ and a small $Î´>0$
		such that $\{ğ˜¡_n^Îµâˆ§Î´\}$ is a supermartingale.
		According to the proof of \cref{lem:artifact} (and the remark underneath),
		this $Î´$ is such that $ğ˜¡_{n+1}â‰¤ğ˜¡^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}-Îµ}$ whenever $Z_n^Îµ<Î´$.
		
		(Define events.)
		Consider only perfect square $n$.
		(If it is not the case, follow the workaround in \cref{lem:Z-en23}.)
		Let $ğ˜Œâ‚€â°$ be the empty event.
		For every $m=âˆšn,2âˆšnâ€¦n-âˆšn$, we define five series of events
		$ğ˜ˆ_m$, $ğ˜‰_m$, $ğ˜Š_m$, $ğ˜Œ_m$, and $ğ˜Œâ‚€^m$ inductively as below:
		Let $ğ˜ˆ_m$ be $\{ğ˜¡_m<\exp(-m^{2/3})\}ã€ğ˜Œâ‚€^{m-âˆšn}$.
		Let $ğ˜‰_m$ be a subevent of $ğ˜ˆ_m$ where $ğ˜¡_l^Îµâ‰¥Î´$ for some $lâ‰¥m$.
		Let $ğ˜Š_m$ a subevent of $ğ˜ˆ_m$ where
		\[ğ˜‹_{m+1}ğ˜‹_{m+2}\dotsmğ˜‹_{m+âˆšn}â‰¤8^{Îµâˆšn}.\label{ine:mgf-8en}\]
		Let $ğ˜Œ_m$ be $ğ˜ˆ_mã€(ğ˜‰_mâˆªğ˜Š_m)$.
		Let $ğ˜Œâ‚€^m$ be $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{m-âˆšn}âˆªğ˜Œ_m$.
		Let $ğ˜¢_m$, $ğ˜£_m$, $ğ˜¤_m$, $ğ˜¦_m$, and $ğ˜¦â‚€^m$
		be the probability measures of the corresponding capital letter events.
		Moreover, let $ğ˜¨_m$ be $1-H(W)-ğ˜¦â‚€^m$.
		
		(Bound $ğ˜£_m/ğ˜¢_m$ from above.)
		Conditioning on $ğ˜ˆ_m$, I want to estimate
		the probability that $ğ˜¡_l^Îµâ‰¥Î´$ for some $lâ‰¥m$.
		Recall that $\{ğ˜¡_l^Îµâˆ§Î´\}$ is made a supermartingale.
		By Ville's inequality,
		$ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_l^Îµâ‰¥Î´$ for some $lâ‰¥mï½œğ˜ˆ_m\}â‰¤ğ˜¡_m^Îµ/Î´<\exp(-m^{2/3}Îµ)/Î´$.
		This is an upper bound on $ğ˜£_m/ğ˜¢_m$
		and will be summoned in \cref{ine:core-een13}.
		
		(Bound $ğ˜¤_m/ğ˜¢_m$ from above.)
		I am to estimate how frequently \cref{ine:mgf-8en} happens.
		It is the probability of  $(ğ˜‹_{m+1}ğ˜‹_{m+2}\dotsmğ˜‹_{m+âˆšn})^{-Î»}â‰¥8^{-ÎµÎ»âˆšn}$.
		This probability must not exceed
		$ğ˜Œ[(ğ˜‹_{m+1}ğ˜‹_{m+2}\dotsmğ˜‹_{m+âˆšn})^{-Î»}]\*8^{ÎµÎ»âˆšn}
			=ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹â‚^{-Î»}]^{âˆšn}8^{ÎµÎ»âˆšn}=(ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹â‚^{-Î»}]8^{ÎµÎ»})^{âˆšn}<â„“^{-Ï±âˆšn}$
		by Markov's inequality.
		This is an upper bound on $ğ˜¤_m/ğ˜¢_m$
		and will be summoned in \cref{ine:core-een13}.
		
		(Bound $(ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+$ from above.)
		By definitions, $ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m=1-H(W)-(ğ˜¦â‚€^{m-âˆšn}+ğ˜¢_m)$.
		The definition of $ğ˜ˆ_m$ forces it to be disjoint from $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{m-âˆšn}$,
		thus $ğ˜¦â‚€^{m-âˆšn}+ğ˜¢_m$ is the probability measure of $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{m-âˆšn}âˆªğ˜ˆ_m$.
		This union event must contain the event
		$\{ğ˜¡_m<\exp(-m^{2/3})\}$ by how $ğ˜ˆ_m$ was defined.
		Recall the en23 behavior $ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_m<\exp(-m^{2/3})\}>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}$.
		Chaining all inequalities together, we deduce $ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m<â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}$.
		Let $(ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+$ be $\max(0,ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)$
		so we can write $(ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+<â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}$.
		This upper bound will be summoned in \cref{ine:core-een13}.
		
		(Bound $ğ˜¦â‚€^{n-âˆšn}$ from below.)
		We start rewriting $ğ˜¨_m$ with $ğ˜¨_m^+$ being $\max(0,ğ˜¨_m)$:
		\begin{align*}
			ğ˜¨_m
			&	=1-H(W)-ğ˜¦â‚€^m=1-H(W)-(ğ˜¦â‚€^{m-âˆšn}+ğ˜¦_m)	\\
			&	=1-H(W)-ğ˜¦â‚€^{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¦_m=ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¦_m	\\
			&	=ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}ï¼ˆ1-Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}ï¼‰+Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}(ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}^+ï¼ˆ1-Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}ï¼‰+Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}(ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}^+ï¼ˆ1-Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}ï¼‰+(ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}^+ï¼ˆÃ·{ğ˜£_m}{ğ˜¢_m}+Ã·{ğ˜¤_m}{ğ˜¢_m}ï¼‰+(ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+	\\
			&	<ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}^+ï¼ˆe^{-m^{2/3}Îµ}/Î´+â„“^{-Ï±âˆšn}ï¼‰+â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}.
				\label{ine:core-een13}
		\end{align*}
		The first four equalities are by the definitions of $ğ˜¨_m$ and $ğ˜Œâ‚€^m$.
		The next equality is by elementary algebra.
		The next two inequalities are by $0â‰¤ğ˜¦_m/ğ˜¢_mâ‰¤1$.
		The next inequality is by the definition of $ğ˜Œ_m$.
		The last inequality summons upper bounds derived in the last three paragraphs.
		The last line contains two terms in the big parentheses;
		between them, $â„“^{-Ï±âˆšn}$ dominates $\exp(-m^{2/3}Îµ)/Î´$
		once $m$ is greater than $O(n^{3/4})$.
		Subsequently, we obtain this recurrence relation:
		\[\cas{
			ğ˜¨_{O(n^{3/4})}â‰¤1,	\\
			ğ˜¨_mâ‰¤2ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}^+â„“^{-Ï±âˆšn}+â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}.
		}\]
		Solve it (cf.\ the master theorem);
		we get that $ğ˜¨_{n-âˆšn}<â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}$.
		By the definition of $ğ˜¨_{n-âˆšn}$,
		we immediately get $ğ˜¦â‚€^{n-âˆšn}>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}$.
		
		(Analyze $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn}$.)
		We want to estimate $ğ˜_n$ when $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn}$ happens.
		To be precise, for each $m=âˆšn,2âˆšnâ€¦n-âˆšn$,
		we attempt to bound $ğ˜¡_{m+âˆšn}$ when $ğ˜Œ_m$ happens.
		Fix an $m$.
		When $ğ˜Œ_m$ happens, its superevent $ğ˜ˆ_m$ happens,
		so we know that $ğ˜¡_m<\exp(-m^{2/3})$.
		But $ğ˜‰_m$ does not happen, so $ğ˜¡_l^Îµ<Î´$ for all $lâ‰¥m$.
		This implies that $ğ˜¡_{l+1}â‰¤ğ˜¡_l^{ğ˜‹_{l+1}(1-Îµ)}$ for those $l$.
		Telescope;
		$ğ˜¡_{m+âˆšn}$ is less than or equal to $ğ˜¡_m$ raised to
		the power of $ğ˜‹_{m+1}ğ˜‹_{m+2}\dotsmğ˜‹_{m+âˆšn}(1-Îµ)^{âˆšn}$.
		But $ğ˜Š_m$ does not happen, so the product is greater than
		$8^{Îµâˆšn}(1-Îµ)^{âˆšn}=(8âˆš[Îµ]{1-Îµ})^{Îµâˆšn}$.
		The latter is greater than $2^{Îµâˆšn}$ granted that $Îµ<1/2$.
		Jointly we have $ğ˜¡_{m+âˆšn}â‰¤ğ˜¡_m^{2^{Îµâˆšn}}<\exp(-m^{2/3}2^{Îµâˆšn})$.
		Recall that $ğ˜¡_{l+1}â‰¤q^â„“ğ˜¡_l$ for all $lâ‰¥m+âˆšn$.
		Then telescope again;
		$ğ˜¡_nâ‰¤q^{â„“(n-m-âˆšn)}ğ˜¡_{m+âˆšn}<q^{â„“n}\exp(-m^{2/3}2^{Îµâˆšn})<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)$
		provided that $n$ is sufficiently large.
		In other words, $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn}$ implies $ğ˜¡_n<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)$.
		
		(Summary.)
		Now we may conclude
		$ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜¡_n<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)ï½â‰¥ğ˜—(ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn})=ğ˜¦â‚€^n>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}$.
		And hence the proof of the een13 behavior is sound.
	\end{proof}
	
	In the next section, I will show the elpin behavior of $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$.
	That will imply the MDP behavior of polar coding and
	the latter will specialize to the LDP and CLT behaviors of polar coding.
	In contract to this section, where we used
	$ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹_n=1\}$ and $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹_nâ‰¥2\}$ to determine the behavior of $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$,
	we will have to use the â€œfull powerâ€ of $\{ğ˜‹_n\}$ in what follows.

\section{Elpin Behavior}\label{sec:elpin}

	As the een13 behavior lowers $ğ˜¡_m$ to the order of $\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}\)$,
	we can afford telescoping $ğ˜¡_{l+1}â‰¤ğ˜¡_l^{ğ˜‹_{l+1}-Îµ}$
	for more $l$'s without having to worry about $ğ˜¡_l^Îµâ‰¥Î´$.
	So the trade-off between the block error probability ($â‰ˆâˆ‘ğ˜¡_n$) and
	the code rate ($â‰ˆğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_n<â‹¯\}$) amounts to the behavior of the product
	$ğ˜‹_{m+1}ğ˜‹_{m+2}\dotsmğ˜‹_n$ or, as probability theorists may prefer,
	the behavior of the i.i.d.\ sum $ã’_â„“ğ˜‹_{m+1}+ã’_â„“ğ˜‹_{m+2}+\dotsb+ã’_â„“ğ˜‹_n$.
	
	As \cref{sec:regime} suggests,
	the limiting behavior of i.i.d.\ sums is well known.
	In particular, I will borrow the LDP result that concerns
	the limiting behavior of $ğ˜—\{Â¯X_n<x\}$ when $x$ is fixed and $n$ increases.
	First goes some definitions.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{cumulant generating function} of $ã’_â„“ğ˜‹â‚$ is defined to be
		the logarithm of its moment generating function or, more precisely,
		\[ğ˜’(t)â‰”ã’_â„“(ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹â‚^{t}])=Ã·1{ã‘â„“}ã‘âˆ‘_{j=1}^â„“Ã·{ğ˜‹â‚^{t}}{â„“}.\]
	\end{dfn}
	
	\begin{dfn}
		The \emph{CramÃ©r function} of $ã’_â„“ğ˜‹â‚$ is defined to be
		the convex conjugate of the cumulant generating function or, more precisely,
		\[ğ˜“(s)â‰”\sup_{tâ‰¤0}st-ğ˜’(t).\]
	\end{dfn}
	
	$ğ˜“(s)$ controls the limiting law of
	the sum of $ã’_â„“ğ˜‹_l$ or the product of $ğ˜‹_l$.
	An example (which we have seen) is that, when $ğ˜‹_lâˆˆ\{1,2\}$,
	its logarithm is just a coin toss, and $1-hâ‚‚$ controls the limiting law.
	
	\begin{thm}[CramÃ©r's theorem]\label{thm:cramer}
		For any $n$,
		\[ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚ğ˜‹â‚‚\dotsmğ˜‹_nâ‰¤â„“^{sn}\}â‰¤â„“^{-nğ˜“(s)}.\]
		Furthermore, $ğ˜“(s)$ is the greatest value satisfying that, i.e.,
		\[\lim_{nâ†’âˆ}Ã·1nã’_â„“ğ˜—\{ğ˜‹â‚ğ˜‹â‚‚\dotsmğ˜‹_n<â„“^{sn}\}=-ğ˜“(s).\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		What I actually need in the sequel is the upper bound on $ğ˜—$.
		The fact that $ğ˜“(s)$ gives the best upper bound is irrelevant to
		the validity of the theorems in this chapter,
		but indicates that the theorems are somewhat optimal.
		The proof of the latter is thus omitted;
		see standard textbooks instead (e.g., \cite{DZ10}).
		
		To prove the upper bound, recall that $ğ˜“(s)$ is a supremum.
		This supremum, by some compactness argument, turns out to be a maximum.
		Let $t$ be the argument that maximizes $st-ğ˜’(t)$.
		Then $ğ˜‹â‚ğ˜‹â‚‚\dotsmğ˜‹_nâ‰¤â„“^{sn}$ is equivalent to $(ğ˜‹â‚ğ˜‹â‚‚\dotsmğ˜‹_n)^tâ‰¥â„“^{stn}$.
		Apply Markov's inequality, the probability that
		$ğ˜‹â‚^tğ˜‹â‚‚^t\dotsmğ˜‹_n^tâ‰¥â„“^{stn}$ is at most
		$ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹â‚^tğ˜‹â‚‚^t\dotsmğ˜‹_n^t]â„“^{-stn}=(ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹â‚^t]â„“^{-st})^n=â„“^{(ğ˜’(t)-st)n}=â„“^{-nğ˜“(s)}$
	\end{proof}
	
	Now we define the achievable region in terms of $ğ˜“(s)$;
	it ought to be the set of $(Ï€,Ï)$ pairs such that
	$ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_n<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})\}>1-H(W)+â„“^{-Ïn}$.
	Consider $Ï–$ being the mean of $ã’_â„“ğ˜‹â‚$.
	We know that, most of the times, i.i.d.\ averages of $ã’_â„“ğ˜‹_l$
	concentrate around the mean so we should not expect that $Ï€>Ï–$.
	Similarly, we do not expect that $Ï>Ï±$.
	The remaining characterization of $(Ï€,Ï)$ is in terms of $ğ˜“(s)$.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		Let $ğ’ªâŠ†[0,Ï–]Ã—[0,Ï±]$ be an open region
		defined by the following criterion:
		for any $(Ï€,Ï)âˆˆğ’ª$, the ray shooting from $(Ï€,Ï)$ toward the opposite direction
		of $(0,Ï±)$ does not intersect the function graph of $Ï=ğ˜“(Ï€)$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	Note that the criterion is equivalent to, geometrically,
	that $(Ï€,Ï)$ lies to the left of the convex envelope of $(0,Ï±)$ and $ğ˜“(s)$.
	So is it equivalent to
	\[ğ˜“ï¼ˆÃ·{Ï€n}{n-m}ï¼‰>Ã·{Ïn-Ï±m}{n-m}\label{ine:Lray-raw}\]
	for all $0<n<m$.
	The main theorem of this chapter can now be stated and proved.
	
	\begin{thm}[From een13 to elpin]\label{thm:dmc-elpin}
		Fix a pair $(Ï€,Ï)âˆˆğ’ª$.
		Given \cref{lem:dmc-een13}, that is, given
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<\exp\(-e^{n^{1/3}}\)ï½>H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)},\]
		we have
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜›_n<e^{-â„“^{Ï€n}}ï½>H(W)-â„“^{-Ïn+o(n)}.\]
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		(Select constants.)
		Since \cref{ine:Lray-raw} holds, there exists a small constant $Îµ>0$ such that
		\[ğ˜“ï¼ˆÃ·{Ï€n}{n-m}+2Îµï¼‰>Ã·{Ïn-Ï±m}{n-m}\label{ine:Lray-gap}\]
		by the compactness argument.
		Pass this $Îµ$ to \cref{lem:artifact}:
		There exists a smaller $Îµ>0$ and a small $Î´>0$ such that $ğ˜¡_n^Îµâˆ§Î´$ is
		a supermartingale and $ğ˜¡_{n+1}â‰¤ğ˜¡_n^{ğ˜‹_{n+1}(1-Îµ)}$ whenever $ğ˜¡_n<Î´$.
		
		(Define events.)
		Let $n$ be a perfect square.
		(If it is not the case, see how \cref{thm:Z-e2pin} bypasses.)
		Let $ğ˜ˆâ‚€â°$ and $ğ˜Œâ‚€â°$ be the empty event.
		For every $m=âˆšn,2âˆšnâ€¦n-âˆšn$, we define six series of events
		$ğ˜ˆ_m$, $ğ˜ˆâ‚€^m$, $ğ˜‰_m$, $ğ˜Š_m$, $ğ˜Œ_m$, and $ğ˜Œâ‚€^m$
		inductively as follows:
		Let $ğ˜ˆ_m$ be $ï½›ğ˜¡_m<\exp(-e^{m^{1/3}})ï½ã€ğ˜ˆâ‚€^{m-âˆšn}$.
		Let $ğ˜ˆâ‚€^m$ be $ğ˜ˆâ‚€^{m-âˆšn}âˆªğ˜ˆ_m$.
		Let $ğ˜‰_m$ be a subevent of $ğ˜ˆ_m$ where $ğ˜¡_l^Îµâ‰¥Î´$ for some $lâ‰¥m$.
		Let $ğ˜Š_m$ a subevent of $ğ˜ˆ_m$ where
		\[ğ˜‹_{m+1}ğ˜‹_{m+2}\dotsmğ˜‹_nâ‰¤â„“^{Ï€n+2Îµ(n-m)}.\label{ine:mgf-lpin}\]
		Let $ğ˜Œ_m$ be $ğ˜ˆ_mã€(ğ˜‰_mâˆªğ˜Š_m)$.
		Let $ğ˜Œâ‚€^m$ be $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{m-âˆšn}âˆªğ˜Œ_m$.
		Let $ğ˜¢_m$, $ğ˜¢â‚€^m$, $ğ˜£_m$, $ğ˜¤_m$, $ğ˜¦_m$, and $ğ˜¦â‚€^m$
		be the probability measures of the corresponding capital letter events.
		Moreover, let $ğ˜§_m$ be $1-H(W)-ğ˜¢â‚€^m$ and let $ğ˜¨_m$ be $1-H(W)-ğ˜¦â‚€^m$.
		
		(Bound $ğ˜£_m/ğ˜¢_m$ from above.)
		Conditioning on $ğ˜ˆ_m$, we want to estimate
		the probability that $ğ˜¡_lâ‰¥Î´$ for some $lâ‰¥m$.
		Recall that $ğ˜¡_l$ is a supermartingale.
		Hence by Ville's inequality,
		$ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_l^Îµâ‰¥Î´$ for some $lâ‰¥mï½œğ˜ˆ_m\}â‰¤ğ˜¡_m^Îµ/Î´<\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}Îµ\)/Î´$.
		This is an upper bound on $ğ˜£_m/ğ˜¢_m$
		and will be summoned in \cref{ine:core-elpin}.
		
		(Bound $ğ˜¤_m/ğ˜¢_m$ from above.)
		We want to estimate how often \cref{ine:mgf-lpin} happens.
		This is equivalent to asking how often do $n-m$
		fair coin tosses end up with $Ï€n+2Îµ(n-m)$ heads.
		By \cref{thm:cramer}, this probability is less than $â„“$ to the power of
		\[-(n-m)ğ˜“ï¼ˆÃ·{Ï€n}{n-m}+2Îµï¼‰.\]
		By \cref{ine:Lray-gap}, this exponent is less than $Ï±m-Ïn$.
		Thus, the probability is less than $â„“^{Ï±m-Ïn}$.
		This is an upper bound on $ğ˜¤_m/ğ˜¢_m$
		and will be summoned in \cref{ine:core-elpin}.
		
		(Bound $ğ˜§_m^+$ from above.)
		The definition of $ğ˜§_m$ reads $1-H(W)-ğ˜¢â‚€^m$.
		Here $ğ˜¢â‚€^m$ is the probability measure of $ğ˜ˆâ‚€^m$,
		and $ğ˜ˆâ‚€^m$ is a superevent of $ğ˜ˆ_m$ by how the former is defined.
		Event $ğ˜ˆâ‚€^m$ must contain $ï½›ğ˜¡_m<\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}\)ï½$
		by how $ğ˜ˆ_m$ was defined.
		By the een13 behavior, $ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜¡_m<\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}\)ï½>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}$.
		Chaining all inequalities together, we infer that $ğ˜§_m<â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}$.
		Let $ğ˜§_m^+$ be $\max(0,ğ˜§_m)$ so
		we can write $ğ˜§_m^+<â„“^{-Ï±m+o(m)}$.
		This upper bound will be summoned in \cref{ine:core-elpin}.
		
		(Bound $ğ˜¦â‚€^{n-âˆšn}$ from below.)
		We start rewriting $ğ˜¨_m-ğ˜§_m^+$ with
		$(ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+$ being $\max(0,ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)$:
		\begin{align*}
			ğ˜¨_m-ğ˜§_m^+
			&	=1-H(W)-ğ˜¦â‚€^m-(1-H(W)-ğ˜¢â‚€^m)^+	\\
			&	=1-H(W)-ğ˜¦â‚€^{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¦_m-(1-H(W)-ğ˜¢â‚€^{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+	\\
			&	=ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¦_m-(ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¦_m-Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}(ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¢_m)^+	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜¦_m-Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}(ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}^+-ğ˜¢_m)	\\
			&	=ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}^++ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}^+ï¼ˆ1-Ã·{ğ˜¦_m}{ğ˜¢_m}ï¼‰	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}^++ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}^+ï¼ˆÃ·{ğ˜£_m}{ğ˜¢_m}+Ã·{ğ˜¤_m}{ğ˜¢_m}ï¼‰	\\
			&	<ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}^++â„“^{-Ï±(m-âˆšn)+o(m-âˆšn)}
				ï¼ˆ\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}Îµ\)/Î´+â„“^{Ï±m-Ïn}ï¼‰.\label{ine:core-elpin}
		\end{align*}
		The first three equalities are by the definitions of $ğ˜¨_m$ and $ğ˜§_m$.
		The next inequality is by $0â‰¤ğ˜¦_m/ğ˜¢_mâ‰¤1$.
		The next inequality is by $\max(0,f-a)=\max(a,f)-aâ‰¥\max(0,f)-a$.
		The next equality is by elementary algebra.
		The next inequality is by the definition of $ğ˜Œ_m$.
		The last inequality summons upper bounds derived in the last three paragraphs.
		Now the last line contains two terms in the big parentheses;
		between them, $â„“^{Ï±m-Ïn}$ dominates $\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}Îµ\)/Î´$ once $nâ†’âˆ$.
		Subsequently, we obtain this recurrence relation
		\[\cas{
			ğ˜¨â‚€-ğ˜§â‚€^+=0;	\\
			ğ˜¨_m-ğ˜§_m^+â‰¤ğ˜¨_{m-âˆšn}-ğ˜§_{m-âˆšn}^++2â„“^{-Ïn+o(n)}.
		}\]
		Solve it (cf.\ the CesÃ ro summation);
		we get that $ğ˜¨_{n-âˆšn}-ğ˜§_{n-âˆšn}^+<â„“^{-Ïn+o(n)}$.
		Once again we summon $ğ˜§_{n-âˆšn}^+<â„“^{-Ï±(n-âˆšn)+o(n-âˆšn)}<â„“^{-Ï±n+o(n)}$;
		therefore $ğ˜¨_{n-âˆšn}<â„“^{-Ïn+o(n)}$.
		Based on the definition of $ğ˜¨_{n-âˆšn}$
		we immediately get $ğ˜¦â‚€^{n-âˆšn}>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ïn+o(n)}$.
		
		(Analyze $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn}$.)
		We want to estimate $ğ˜¡_n$ when $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn}$ happens.
		To be precise, for each $m=âˆšn,2âˆšnâ€¦n-âˆšn$,
		we attempt to bound $ğ˜¡_n$ when $ğ˜Œ_m$ happens .
		Fix an $m$.
		When $ğ˜Œ_m$ happens, its superevent $ğ˜ˆ_m$ happens,
		so we know that $ğ˜¡_m<\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}\)$.
		But $ğ˜‰_m$ does not happen, so $ğ˜¡_l<Î´$ for all $lâ‰¥m$.
		This implies $ğ˜¡_{l+1}â‰¤ğ˜¡_l^{ğ˜‹_{l+1}(1-Îµ)}$ for those $l$.
		Telescope;
		$ğ˜¡_n$ is less than or equal to $ğ˜¡_m$ raised to
		the power of $ğ˜‹_{m+1}ğ˜‹_{m+2}\dotsmğ˜‹_n(1-Îµ)^{n-m}$.
		But $ğ˜Š_m$ does not happen, so the product
		is greater than $â„“^{Ï€n+2Îµ(n-m)}(1-Îµ)^{n-m}$,
		which is greater than $â„“^{Ï€n}$ granted that $Îµ<1/2$.
		Jointly we have
		$ğ˜¡_nâ‰¤ğ˜¡_m^{â„“^{Ï€n}}<\exp\(-e^{m^{1/3}}â„“^{Ï€n}\)<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$.
		In other words, $ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn}$ implies $ğ˜¡_n<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$.
		
		(Summary.)
		Now we may conclude
		$ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_n<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})\}â‰¥ğ˜—(ğ˜Œâ‚€^{n-âˆšn})=ğ˜¦â‚€^n>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ïn+o(n)}$.
		And hence the proof of the elpin behavior is sound.
	\end{proof}

\section{Consequences, Dual Picture Included}

	That $ğ˜¡_n$ can be proved low implies that we have good codes over
	symmetric channels of prime-power input alphabet.
	To code over the most general DMCs, we need to talk about $\{ğ˜š_n\}$---it is
	the process of $ï¼³(ğ˜_n)$ that controls the behavior of $ğ˜_n$ when it becomes noisy.
	
	$ï¼³$ (and $\{ğ˜š_n\}$) really is the dual counterpart of $ï¼º$ (and $\{ğ˜¡_n\}$).
	The HÃ¶lder tolls we pay to translate between $H$ and $ï¼º$
	is the same amount as the tolls we pay to translate between $1-H$ and $ï¼³$.
	Plus, FTPC$Z$ (\cref{thm:ftpcZ}) and FTPC$S$ (\cref{thm:ftpcS}) state
	almost the same phenomenon in terms of $ï¼º$ and $ï¼³$, respectively.
	By the process nonsense that was once used to show \cref{thm:T-e2pin}
	out of \cref{thm:Z-e2pin}, \cref{thm:dmc-elpin} assumes an $ğ˜š$-version.
	
	\begin{thm}[From eigen to elpin]
		Fix a pair $(Ï€,Ï)$ lying to the left of the convex envelope of
		$(0,Ï±)$ and the CramÃ©r function of $ã’_â„“D_Sï¼·{ğ˜‘â‚}$.
		Given the premise of \cref{lem:dmc-en23}, that is, given
		\[\sup_{0<H(W)<1}Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}
			=â„“^{-Ï±n},\]
		then
		\[ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜š_n<e^{-â„“^{Ï€n}}ï½>H(W)-â„“^{-Ïn+o(n)}.\]
	\end{thm}
	
	This theorem together with \cref{thm:dmc-elpin} implies good codes over all DMCs.
	This is because the block error probability is bounded from above
	by the sum of small $ï¼°(ğ˜_n)$ and small $T(ğ˜_n)$,
	and they are further bounded by $ğ˜¡_n$ and $ğ˜š_n$.
	
	\begin{cor}[Good code for DMC]
		Fix a $q$-ary DMC.
		Fix an ergodic $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ with a positive $Ï±>0$.
		Fix a pair $(Ï€,Ï)$ lying to the left of
		(a)	the convex envelope of $(0,Ï±)$ and the CramÃ©r function of $ã’_â„“D_Zï¼·{ğ˜‘â‚}$ and
		(b)	the convex envelope of $(0,Ï±)$ and the CramÃ©r function of $ã’_â„“D_Sï¼·{ğ˜‘â‚}$.
		Then polar coding with kernel $G$ enjoys block error probability $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$
		and code rate $N^{-Ï}$ less than the channel capacity.
	\end{cor}
	
	The same can be stated for lossless and lossy compression.
	For lossless compression, if the source alphabet $ğ’³$
	is not a prime power, add dummy symbols till it is.
	Then the block error probability is the sum of small $ï¼°(ğ˜_n)$.
	For lossy compression, if the palette $ğ’³$ in the distortion function
	$\distï¼šğ’³Ã—ğ’´â†’[0,1]$ is not prime power, add dummy symbols and
	penalize dummy symbols with distortion $1$ (i.e., $\dist(x,y)=1$ if $x$ is dummy).
	Then the excess of distortion is the average of small $T(ğ˜_n)$.
	
	\begin{cor}[Good code for lossless compression]
		Fix a $q$-ary source $X$ and side information $Y$.
		Fix an ergodic $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ (whose $Ï±$ is guaranteed to be positive).
		Fix a pair $(Ï€,Ï)$ lying to the left of
		the convex envelope of $(0,Ï±)$ and the CramÃ©r function of $ã’_â„“D_Zï¼·{ğ˜‘â‚}$.
		Then polar coding with kernel $G$ enjoys block error probability $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$
		and code rate $N^{-Ï}$ plus the conditional entropy.
	\end{cor}
	
	\begin{cor}[Good code for lossy compression]
		Fix a random source $Yâˆˆğ’´$ and a distortion function $\distï¼šğ”½_qÃ—ğ’´â†’[0,1]$.
		Fix an ergodic $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ (whose $Ï±$ is guaranteed to be positive).
		Fix a pair $(Ï€,Ï)$ lying to the left of the convex envelope
		of $(0,Ï±)$ and the CramÃ©r function of $ã’_â„“D_Sï¼·{ğ˜‘â‚}$.
		Then polar coding with kernel $G$ enjoys excess of distortion $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$
		and code rate $N^{-Ï}$ plus the test channel capacity.
	\end{cor}
	
	There are questions to be answered.
	One, how can we predict the best $Ï±$ for a specific $G$?
	Two, what is the best $Ï±$ among all $G$?
	Can we achieve the optimal exponent $Ï±=1/2$?
	Three, Can we reduce the complexity?
	The first question is open.
	The next chapter answers the second question.
	The chapter after answers the third question.



\chapter{Random dynamic Kerneling}\label{cha:random}

	\dropcap
	Significant is the fact that random coding achieves capacity.
	Researchers have been using random coding
	to prove the first achievability bounds for various coding scenarios.
	This evinces that random coding is the very reason why capacity,
	or capacity region when there are more than two users, is what it is.
	This is the first moment of coding theory.
	In addition, random coding achieves capacity at an unbeatable pace---the
	block error probability decays exponentially fast in the block length,
	and the block length grows inverse-quadratically in the gap to capacity.
	This sets up a holy grail that is left to code designers to chase after,
	which is the second-moment paradigm of coding theory.
	
	The question is simple, Can polar coding touch the second-moment paradigm,
	i.e., achieve capacity at a pace that is comparable to random coding?
	
	This chapter gives an affirmative answer.
	But it requires one to rethink the polarizing kernel from the bottom up.
	As mentioned in  \cref{cha:general}, a general kernel $G$
	can be implemented as an EU--DU pair, and some copies of EU--DU pairs
	will wrap around the parent channels to synthesize the child channels.
	In doing so, not all channels need to be wrapped.
	As demonstrated in \cref{cha:prune}, A channel (a pair of pins)
	is left naked if an algorithm decides that the channel is
	sufficiently polarized and not worth more EU--DU pairs.
	The contribution made in \cref{cha:prune} is that this reduces complexity.
	Stretching this idea a bit, we see that a channel (a pair of pins) can also be
	wrapped by an EU--DU pair that corresponds to a different kernel \cite{YB15}.
	Once we accept the idea that $G$ can vary on a channel-by-channel basis,
	the problem becomes how to find the best $G$ that fits a given channel.
	
	So the simple question becomes, Can we find a good kernel for each and every channel
	to help polar coding touch the edge of the second-moment paradigm,
	i.e., to achieve capacity at a nearly-optimal pace?
	
	Now we rethink what exactly is needed here.
	Do we need an algorithm to generate, for each and every channel, a kernel 
	plus a certificate that this particular kernel is good at polarizing?
	Or, only do we need to prove that, for each and every channel,
	there exists at least one kernel that polarizes decently.
	The latter is considerably easier than the former because we do not
	specify \emph{which} kernel is good---only that it exists.
	And we know how this could be done: random coding.
	Use a random matrix $ğ”¾$ to polarize a channel and show that,
	on average, the channel is polarized to a satisfactory extent.
	Then we are done;
	the rest is the pigeonhole principle.
	
	I call selecting a kernel for each channel \emph{dynamic kerneling},
	This chapter in its entirety is a quantization of random dynamic kerneling.

\section{The Holy Grail}

	Fix a DMC $W$ and a capacity-achieving input distribution $Q$.
	The following optimality bound is borrowed.
	
	\begin{thm}[Optimal codes]\label{pro:optimal}
		\cite[Theorem~2]{AW14}, \cite[Theorem~6]{PV10}.
		Fix constants $Ï€,Ï>0$ such that $Ï€+2Ï>1$.
		Assume $V>0$, that is, assume
		\[Vâ‰”\operatorname{Var}ã€Œã‘Ã·{W(Xï½œY)}{Q(X)}ã€>0.\]
		(Remark:
		This is an easy assumption to meet;
		it mostly excludes trivial channels such as those with $H=0,1$.)
		Then no block code assumes $ï¼°<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$ and $R>C-N^{-Ï}$
		except for sufficiently small $N$.
	\end{thm}
	
	Fix exponents $Ï€,Ï>0$ such that $Ï€+2Ï<1$.
	I am to construct a series of codes with error probability $\exp(-N^Ï€)$
	and code rate $N^{-Ï}$ less than the channel capacity.
	The construction, based on polar coding,
	will enjoy encoding and decoding complexity $O(Nã’N)$.

\section{Why Dynamic Kernels}

	Readers may prefer numerical evidence for why dynamic kerneling is superior.
	Since there is essentially one matrix of $2Ã—2$ size,
	I will take $3Ã—3$ for demonstration.
	Over $ğ”½â‚‚$, there are essentially two matrices of $3Ã—3$ size:
	\[\GYâ‰”\bma{1& & \\0&1& \\1&1&1}\qquad\GBâ‰”\bma{1& & \\1&1& \\1&0&1}\]
	$\GY$ has coset distances $\{1,1,3\}$ and dual coset distances $\{1,2,2\}$.
	And $\GB$ is its dual:
	$\GB$ has coset distances $\{1,2,2\}$ and dual coset distance $\{1,1,3\}$.
	Both $\GY$ and $\GB$ have $Ï±=1/4.938$ over BECs.
	Their regions of realizable $(Ï€,Ï)$-pairs over BECs are plotted in \cref{fig:rigid}.
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=6]{
			\draw[gray,very thin]
				(0,.3691)pic{dot}node[left]{$(0,1-b)$}--
				plot[domain=.00001:.1672,samples=34](\x,{h3(\x)})--(.1672,.06365)
				(1/3,0)--plot[domain=1/3:1,samples=133](\x,{h3(\x)})--
				(1,1)pic{dot}node[right]{$(1,1)$}
				(0,1)pic{dot}node[left]{$(0,1)$}--
				plot[domain=.00001:.3682,samples=75](\x,{h4(\x)})--(.3682,.01364)
				(.4206,0)--plot[domain=.4206:.6309,samples=43](\x,{h4(\x)})--
				(.6309,.3691)pic{dot}node[above]{$(b,1-b)$};
			\draw
				(0,1/4.938)pic{dot}node[left]{$(0,1/4.938)$}--
				(.1672,.06365)pic{dot}node[anchor=5]{$(0.167,0.064)$}--
				plot[domain=.1672:1/3,samples=133](\x,{h3(\x)})--
				(1/3,0)pic{dot}coordinate[pin=-165:{$(1/3,0)$}];
			\draw
				(0,1/4.938)--(.3682,.01364)pic{dot}coordinate[pin=-80:{$(0.368,0.014)$}]--
				plot[domain=.3682:.4206,samples=15](\x,{h4(\x)})--
				(.4206,0)pic{dot}coordinate[pin=-10:{$(2b/3,0)$}];
		}
		\caption{
			The two regions and CramÃ©r functions of $\GY$ over BECs.
			Here $b=ã’â‚ƒ2$.
			The curve passing $(0,1-b)$--$(0.167,0.064)$--$(1/3,0)$--$(1,1)$
			is the CramÃ©r function of the uniform distribution on $\{0,0,1\}$
			and also the KL divergence of $p+(1-p)$ w.r.t.\ $1/3+2/3$.
			The curve passing $(0,1/4.938)$--$(0,1-b)$--$(0.167,0.064)$--$(1/3,0)$
			is the boundary of realizable $(Ï€,Ï)$-pairs in the estimate of $ğ˜¡_n$.
			The curve passing $(0,1)$--$(0.368,0.014)$--$(2b/3,0)$--$(b,1-b)$
			is the CramÃ©r function of the uniform distribution on $\{b,b,0\}$
			and also the KL divergence of $p/b+(1-p/b)$ w.r.t.\ $2/3+1/3$.
			The curve passing $(0,1/4.938)$--$(0.368,0.014)$--$(2b/3,0)$--$(1,0)$
			is the boundary of realizable $(Ï€,Ï)$-pairs in the estimate of $ğ˜š_n$.
			For $\GB$ over BECs, swap the two curves.
		}\label{fig:rigid}
	\end{figure}
	
	Now consider this variant of polar coding:
	When $H(W)â‰¤1/2$, wrap the channel with $\GB$;
	when $H(W)>1/2$, wrap the channel with $\GY$.
	Let $ğ’¢$ denote an amoebic kernel that becomes $\GB$ when it sees a reliable channel
	and becomes $\GY$ when it sees a noisy one.
	Then two things change.
	Firstly, the estimate of $Ï±$ rises from $1/4.938$ to $1/4.183$.
	This is because $\GB$ is better at polarizing reliable channels
	and $\GY$ is better at polarizing noisy ones.
	This is the division of labor.
	Secondly, the coset distances are now $\{1,2,2\}$ on both reliable and noisy ends.
	Comparing to $\{1,1,3\}$, which reward you more ($3$)
	with a lower probability ($1/3$), distances $\{1,2,2\}$
	reward you less ($2$) but with a higher probability ($2/3$).
	And the random variable that rewards you less, but steadily, is preferred.
	Now $ğ’¢$'s region of $(Ï€,Ï)$-pairs becomes \cref{fig:dynamic}.
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=6]{
			\draw[gray,very thin]
				(0,1)pic{dot}node[left]{$(0,1)$}--
				plot[domain=.00001:.3575,samples=72](\x,{h4(\x)})--(.3575,.01970)
				(.4206,0)--plot[domain=.4206:.6309,samples=43](\x,{h4(\x)})--
				(.6309,.3691)pic{dot}node[right]{$(b,1-b)$};
			\draw[gray,very thin]
				(0,1/4.938)pic{dot}--(.1672,.06365)pic{dot}--
				plot[domain=.1672:1/3,samples=133](\x,{h3(\x)})--(1/3,0)pic{dot}
				(0,1/4.938)--(.3682,.01364)pic{dot};
			\draw
				(0,1/4.183)pic{dot}node[left]{$(0,1/4.183)$}--
				(.3575,.01970)pic{dot}coordinate[pin=-105:{$(0.357,0.020)$}]
				plot[domain=.357:.4206,samples=15](\x,{h4(\x)})--
				(.4206,0)pic{dot}coordinate[pin=-15:{$(2b/3,0)$}];
			\draw[opacity=0]
				(1,1)node[right]{$(1,1)$};
		}
		\caption{
			The region and CramÃ©r function of $ğ’¢$, the compound strategy, over BECs.
			The old regions and CramÃ©r functions are in gray for the ease of comparison.
		}\label{fig:dynamic}
	\end{figure}
	
	For if we allow larger matrices, then it is not hard to imagine that
	the space of DMCs is partitioned into several territories, each is
	â€œruled byâ€ the kernel that is best at polarizing the channels living within.
	These kernels will be collectively called $ğ’¢$,
	and then we can talk about the $(Ï€,Ï)$-pairs of $ğ’¢$.

\section{Why Random Kernels}

	Before the construction begins, let me elaborate on
	why random matrices are preferred.
	As the last chapter paved the way to
	the determination of the MDP region $ğ’ª$ of a kernel,
	we know $ğ’¢$ should meet the following three requirements:
	\begin{itemize}
		\item	LDP requirement:
				The coset distances of $ğ’¢$ should be large.
				Namely, the matrix that $ğ’¢$ becomes when it sees reliable channels
				should have large coset distances.
				In particular, the geometric mean of $D_Zï¼·j$ should be $â„“-o(â„“)$.
		\item	Dual LDP requirement:
				The geometric mean of $ã’_â„“D_Sï¼·j$ should be $â„“-o(â„“)$.
				Namely, the matrix that $ğ’¢$ becomes when it sees noisy channels
				should have large coset distances.
		\item	CLT requirement:
				The performance of $ğ’¢$ measured by
				the eigenvalue $â„“^{-Ï±}$ should be good.
				Namely, $Ï±=1/2-o(1)$ as $â„“â†’âˆ$.
	\end{itemize}
	
	It is not easy to meet any of the requirements, let alone all of them at once.
	As a comparison, classical coding theory usually concerns
	how to construct a rectangular matrix (a code) with high Hamming weights.
	Less frequently it concerns finding nested matrices
	(aka.\ a flag of codes) with high Hamming weights.
	In this viewpoint, the LDP requirement is equivalent to finding a maximal chain
	of nested matrices (a full flag of codes) with high Hamming weight.
	The CLT requirement, on the other hand, does not seem to have classical counterpart.
	
	Polarizing $W$ is just side of the story.
	On the other side, we have to polarize $Q$, the input distribution
	seen as a channel, for asymmetric channels and lossy compression.
	Thus we have four requirements to meet---LDP, dual LDP, $W$'s CLT, and $Q$'s CLT.
	
	Random coding is the perfect remedy for this because it measures
	the density of the â€œgood objectsâ€ that meet various conditions.
	Take the LDP requirement as an example.
	If $ğ”¾$ is a random matrix that is drawn uniformly,
	then any rectangular sub-matrix is uniformly random.
	By classical coding theory, we know how to compute
	the Hamming weights of a random rectangular matrix.
	To be more precise, we know how to compute the probability that the minimum distance
	of a random linear code is at least a certain threshold.
	We just have to repeat the argument for all rectangular sub-matrices,
	and then apply the union bound.
	
	Random matrices have more uses than that.
	If we draw $ğ”¾$ from the uniform ensemble of invertible matrices,
	then its Hamming distances are still under control.
	(In fact, excluding singular matrices avoids low Hamming distances
	in a helpful manner, so we are actually doing a favor
	for the random coding argument.)
	Now that the inverse $ğ”¾^{-1}$ follows the uniform distribution on
	invertible matrices, bounding the dual coset distances of $ğ”¾$ is as easy as
	bounding the primary coset distances of $ğ”¾$---just multiply the union bound by $2$.
	
	Below goes the actual plan to attack the problem:
	In \cref{sec:LDP}, I will show that a random matrix $ğ”¾$ has sufficient distances,
	and meet the LDP requirement (and its dual).
	In \cref{sec:CLT}, I will attempt to bound the eigenvalue of $ğ”¾$;
	this bound then comes down to four bounds:
	\begin{itemize}
		\item	a bound derived from the LDP requirement via HÃ¶lder tolls,
		\item	a bound derived from the dual LDP requirement via HÃ¶lder tolls,
		\item	a bound for noisy-channel coding via random linear codes, and
		\item	a bound for wiretap-channel coding via random linear codes.
	\end{itemize}
	\Cref{sec:prepare} prepares some materials for the last two $â€¢$.
	In \cref{sec:gallager}, the noisy-channel coding bound will be proved.
	In \cref{sec:hayashi}, the wiretap-channel coding bound will be proved.
	
	Notice that the random matrix $ğ”¾$ is typeset in the blackboard bold font.
	So will the related notations (such as $â„™,ğ”¼$)
	be typeset in the blackboard bold font.

\section{LDP Data of Random Matrix}\label{sec:LDP}

	This section is dedicated to showing that a random matrix $ğ”¾âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$
	drawn uniformly from the general linear group $\GL(â„“,q)$
	has good distances $D_Zï¼·j$ anf $D_Sï¼·j$.
	But before that, What is â€œgoodâ€?
	Let us consider $q=2$ as an example.
	The following hand-waving argument is borrowed from \cite{BF02}.
	
	To understand the typical behavior of $D_Zï¼·j$ when $ğ”¾$ varies,
	it is easier if we compute the average of $f_Zï¼·j(z)$ over $ğ”¾$,
	where $f_Zï¼·j(z)$ is the weight enumerator of the coset code
	$\{0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾:u_{j+1}^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“-j}\}âŠ†ğ”½_q^â„“$.
	Since $ğ”¾$ is invertible and distributed uniformly,
	$0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$ is just a random nonzero vector drawn from $ğ”½_q^â„“$.
	So every message $u_{j+1}^â„“$ contributed $((1+z)^â„“-1)/(2^â„“-1)$
	to the average of $f_Zï¼·j$, where $-1$ is to exclude the all-zero vector.
	Overall, the average of $f_Zï¼·j(z)$ over $ğ”¾$ is $((1+z)^â„“-1)/2^j$.
	Forget about the $-1$;
	the $z^k$ coefficient of $(1+z)^â„“/2^j$ is $\binom{â„“}k/2^j$.
	By the large deviations theory, $[z^k]f_Zï¼·j=\binom{â„“}k/2^jâ‰ˆ2^{â„“hâ‚‚(k/â„“)-j}$.
	Roughly speaking, the coefficient would (very likely) be $0$
	for a realization of $ğ”¾$ if the average is less than $1$ (by a lot).
	So we conclude a rule of thumb:
	$D_Zï¼·j>k$ iff $[z^k]f_Zï¼·j<1$ iff $hâ‚‚(k/â„“)<j/â„“$.
	See \cref{fig:coeffi} for an illustration.
