	Let us not forget that we are to bound the distances with high probability.
	Therefore, there is not a clear cut at $hâ‚‚(k/â„“)<j/â„“$;
	we have to leave a gap between $hâ‚‚(k/â„“)$ and $j/â„“$
	to facilitate Markov's inequality.
	Here, I will use the upper bound $âˆš{ep}â‰¥hâ‚‚(p)$ shown in \cref{fig:rootep},
	and will leave a gap by loosening the bound to $âˆš{3p}â‰¥hâ‚‚(p)$.
	The formal statement and proof is below.
	
	\begin{thm}[Typical LDP behavior]\label{thm:typical-LDP}
		Let $zâ‰”ï¼º(W)$.
		Fix an $â„“â‰¥30$.
		Draw a $ğ”¾âˆˆ\GL(â„“,q)$ uniformly at random.
		Fix a $jâˆˆ[â„“]$.
		Then, with probability less than $3q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$, the following fails:
		\[f_Zï¼·j(z)â‰¤â„“(1+q'z)^{â„“-1}(q'z)^{âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰},\label{ine:enum-Z}\]
		where $q'â‰”q-1$.
		In particular, we have $D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰$.
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Divide $j$ into two cases: $1â‰¤jâ‰¤âˆš{3â„“}$ and $âˆš{3â„“}<jâ‰¤â„“$.
		For $j=1,2â€¦âˆš{3â„“}$, the exponent $âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰$ is nothing but $1$,
		thus the inequality to be proved reads
		$f_Zï¼·j(z)â‰¤â„“{(1+q'z)^{â„“-1}}q'z$, coefficient-wisely.
		The right-hand side overcounts all nonzero codewords by
		choosing a position ($â„“$), assigning a nonzero symbol ($q'z$),
		and arbitrarily filling in the rest of $â„“-1$ blanks ($(1+q'z)^{â„“-1}$).
		On the left-hand side, $f_Zï¼·j(z)$ enumerates only codewords of the form
		$0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$, which are all nonzero as $ğ”¾$ is invertible.
		Hence \cref{ine:enum-Z} holds for $jâ‰¤âˆš{3â„“}$ and nonnegative $z$
		regardless of what kernel $ğ”¾$ is in effect.
		
		For $j=âŒŠâˆš{3â„“}âŒ‹+1,âŒŠâˆš{3â„“}âŒ‹+2â€¦â„“$, let $dâ‰”jÂ²/3â„“$.
		To make \cref{ine:enum-Z} hold, we execute a two-phase procedure
		to avoid all codewords of weight less than $d$
		and to eliminate kernels with poor overall score.
		In further detail, we will reject a kernel $ğ”¾$ if there exists $u_{j+1}^â„“$
		such that $\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾)<d$ and call it phase~I\@.
		Afterwards, among surviving kernels with only \emph{heavy}
		(high weight) codewords, we will reject a kernel if its overall score
		$f_Zï¼·j(z)$ is too low and call it phase~II\@.
		The failing probability $3q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$ is the price we pay for rejecting.
		Up to this point, two things remain to be analyzed:
		how much probability we pay for rejecting \emph{light}
		(low weight) codewords in phase~I (answer: $q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$),
		and what is the Markov cutoff that honors \cref{ine:enum-Z}
		in phase~II (answer: $2q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$).
		
		Phase~I analysis is as follows:
		Fix $u_{j+1}^â„“$ and vary $ğ”¾âˆˆ\GL(â„“,q)$;
		the codeword $ğ•â‚^â„“â‰”0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$ is
		a nonzero vector distributed uniformly on $ğ”½_q^â„“ã€\{0â‚^â„“\}$.
		This distribution is almost identical to the uniform distribution on $ğ”½_q^â„“$.
		Assume $ğ•â‚^â„“$ follows the latter;
		this makes $ğ•â‚^â„“$ lighter,
		which is compatible with the direction of the inequalities we want.
		Then the probability that $ğ•â‚^â„“$ has weight less than $d$ is the probability
		that $â„“$ Bernoulli trials---each $ğ•_j$ is ``zero'' with probability $1/q$ and
		``nonzero'' with probability $q'/q$---result in less than $d$ ``nonzero''s.
		By the large deviations theory \cite[Exercise~2.2.23(b)]{DZ10},
		$\hwt(ğ•â‚^â„“)<d$ holds with probability less than
		\[\expï¼ˆ-â„“ğ”»ï¼ˆÃ·d{â„“}â€–Ã·12ï¼‰ï¼‰=2^{-â„“(1-hâ‚‚(d/â„“))}\]
		for the $q=2$ case, where $ğ”»$ is the Kullback--Leibler divergence.
		For general $q$, similarly, $\hwt(ğ•â‚^â„“)<d$ holds with probability less than
		\[\expï¼ˆ-â„“ğ”»ï¼ˆÃ·d{â„“}â€–1-Ã·1qï¼‰ï¼‰â‰¤q^{-â„“(1-hâ‚‚(d/â„“))}.\]
		It is less than $q^{-â„“(1-hâ‚‚(d/â„“))}$ by the comparison made in \cref{fig:kldiv}
		(meaning that $q=2$ is the most difficult case).
		Now we obtain $hâ‚‚(d/â„“)<âˆš{ed/â„“}=âˆš{ejÂ²/3â„“Â²}=(âˆš{e/3})j/â„“<0.952j/â„“$.
		Therefore, the single-word rejecting probability is less than
		$q^{-â„“(1-hâ‚‚(d/â„“))}<q^{-â„“+0.952j}$.
		Take into account that there are $q^{â„“-j}$ codewords, one for each $u_{j+1}^â„“$.
		The union bound yields
		$q^{â„“-j}q^{-â„“+0.952j}=q^{-0.048j}<q^{-0.048âˆš{3â„“}}<q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$.
		Therefore, the total rejecting probability is $q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$.
		Phase~I ends here.
		
		Phase~II analysis is as follows:
		After we reject some $ğ”¾$ in phase~I, some codewords will disappear;
		particularly, this includes all light codewords.
		Therefore,
		the expectation of $f_Zï¼·j(z)$ is bounded by the weight enumerator
		of all heavy codewords rescaled by the number of codewords.
		In detail, start from
		\begin{align*}
			ğ”¼[f_Zï¼·j(z)ï½œğ”¾â€  survives phase Iâ€ ]
			&	=ğ”¼[f_Zï¼·j(z)Â·ğ•€\{ğ”¾â€  survivesâ€ \}]/â„™\{ğ”¾â€  survivesâ€ \}	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ”¼[f_Zï¼·j(z)Â·ğ•€\{ğ”¾â€  survivesâ€ \}]/(1-q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}).\label{ine:phase}
		\end{align*}
		$ğ•€$ is the indicator function.
		In the denominator, $1-q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}>1/4$ as $â„“â‰¥30$.
		Put that aside and redefine $dâ‰”âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰$.
		The expected value part is bounded from above by
		\def\bn#1#2{\hbox{\large$\tbinom{#1}{#2}$}}
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			ğ”¼[f_Zï¼·j(z)Â·ğ•€\{ğ”¾â€  survivesâ€ \}]
				=ğ”¼ã€Œâˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}z^{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾)}Â·ğ•€\{ğ”¾â€  survivesâ€ \}ã€	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ”¼ã€Œâˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}z^{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾)}Â·
				ğ•€\{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾)â‰¥d\}ã€	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{u_{j+1}^â„“}ğ”¼[z^{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾)}Â·
				ğ•€\{\hwt(0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾)â‰¥d\}]	\\
			&	â‰¤q^{â„“-j}ğ”¼[z^{\hwt(ğ•â‚^â„“)}Â·ğ•€\{\hwt(ğ•â‚^â„“)â‰¥d\}]
				=q^{â„“-j}q^{-â„“}âˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}z^{\hwt(xâ‚^â„“)}Â·ğ•€\{\hwt(xâ‚^â„“)â‰¥d\}	\\
			&	=q^{-j}âˆ‘_{wâ‰¥d}\bn{â„“}w(q'z)^w
				â‰¤q^{-j}âˆ‘_{wâ‰¥d}\bn{â„“}d\bn{â„“-d}{w-d}(q'z)^w	\\
			&	=q^{-j}\bn{â„“}dâˆ‘_{wâ‰¥d}\bn{â„“-d}{w-d}(q'z)^{w-d}(q'z)^d
				=q^{-j}\bn{â„“}d(1+q'z)^{â„“-d}(q'z)^d	\\
			\shortintertext{(overestimate the scalar $q^{-j}\bn{â„“}d$)}
			&â‰¤	(q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}â„“/2)(1+q'z)^{â„“-d}(q'z)^d.
		\end{align*}
		The first equality expands the definition.
		The next inequality replaces $ğ”¾$ surviving phase~I by a weaker condition.
		The next equality swaps $ğ”¼$ and $âˆ‘$.
		The next inequality replaces
		the ensemble of $0â‚^{j-1}1_ju_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$ by a uniform $ğ•â‚^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“$.
		The next equality expands the definition of the expectation over $ğ•â‚^â„“$.
		The next equality counts codewords.
		The next inequality selects $w$ positions
		by first selecting $d$ and then selecting $w-d$.
		The next two equalities factor and apply the binomial theorem.
		The rest is by a series of inequalities that overestimate the scalar:
		$q^{-j}\binom{â„“}d=q^{-j}\binom{â„“}{âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰}<q^{-j}\binom{â„“}{jÂ²/3â„“}â„“/2
			<q^{-j}2^{â„“hâ‚‚(jÂ²/3â„“Â²)}â„“/2â‰¤q^{-j+â„“hâ‚‚(jÂ²/3â„“Â²)}â„“/2$.
		Similar to the end of phase~I, the exponent part is
		$-j+â„“hâ‚‚(jÂ²/3â„“Â²)<-j+â„“âˆš{ejÂ²/3â„“Â²}=-j+jâˆš{e/3}<-0.048j<-0.048âˆš{3â„“}<-âˆšâ„“/13$.
		Hence the scalar part is less than $q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}â„“/2$.
		Put $1-q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}>1/4$ back to the denominator as in \cref{ine:phase};
		$ğ”¼[f_Zï¼·j(z)ï½œğ”¾â€  survives phase Iâ€ ]$ has an upper bound of
		\[2q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}â„“(1+q'z)^{â„“-d}(q'z)^d.\]
		By Markov's inequality,
		\cref{ine:enum-Z} holds with probability $1-2q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$,
		i.e., the rejecting probability is $2q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$.
		Phase~II ends here.
		
		The sum of the two rejecting probabilities is $3q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$ as claimed
		in the theorem statement, hence settles the proof of \cref{thm:typical-LDP}.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{figure}
		\tikz[scale=8]{
			\draw
				(0,1)pic{y-tick=$1$}(0,0)pic{y-tick=$0$}
				(0,0)pic{x-tick=$0$}(1,0)pic{x-tick=$1$};
			\draw
				plot[domain=0:90,samples=360]({sin(\x)^2},{h2o(\x)})
				({sin(80)^2},{h2o(80)})node[above right]{$q=2$}
				plot[domain=0:1.1,samples=20](\x^2/e,\x)node[right]{$âˆš{ep}$};
			\foreach\q in{3,4,5,6,7}{
				\PMS\lnqq{ln(\q-1)}\PMS\lnq{ln(\q)}\PMS\invq{1/log2(\q)}
				\PMD{hqo}1{%
					\PMS\sin{sin(##1)}\PMS\cos{cos(##1)}%
					\ifdim\sin pt=0pt%
						\PMP{0}%
					\else\ifdim\cos pt=0pt%
						\PMP{1-\lnqq/\lnq}%
					\else%
						\PMP{(\sin*(ln(\sin)*2+\lnq-\lnqq)*\sin
							+ \cos*(ln(\cos)*2+\lnq)*\cos)/\lnq}
					\fi\fi
				}
				\draw plot[domain=0:90,samples=360]({sin(\x)^2},{1-hqo(\x)});
			}
			\foreach\q in{3,4,5,7}{
				\PMS\lnqq{ln(\q-1)}\PMS\lnq{ln(\q)}\PMS\invq{1/log2(\q)}
				\draw(1,\lnqq/\lnq)node[right,inner sep=1,scale=\invq]{$q=\q$};
			}
			\draw[->](0,0)--(0,1.1);
			\draw[->](0,0)--(1.1,0)node[right]{$p$};
		}
		\caption{
			One minus the Kullback--Leibler divergences $1-ğ”»(p\|1-1/q)/\ln(q)$
			for $q=2,3,5,7$ and an upper bound of $âˆš{ep}$.
		}\label{fig:kldiv}
	\end{figure}
	
	The bound I just proved,
	\[f_Zï¼·j(z)â‰¤â„“(1+q'z)^{â„“-1}(q'z)^{âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰},\tagcopy{ine:enum-Z}\]
	implies its dual siblings because $ğ”¾^{-1}$ is uniform on $\GL(â„“,q)$.
	
	\begin{cor}
		Let $sâ‰”ï¼³(W)$.
		Then, with probability $1-3q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$, the following holds for each $jâˆˆ[â„“]$:
		\[f_Sï¼·{â„“-j+1}(s)â‰¤â„“(1+q'z)^{â„“-1}(q's)^{âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰}.\label{ine:enum-S}\]
	\end{cor}
	
	Together, we have that with failing probability at most $6â„“q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$,
	both \cref{ine:enum-Z,ine:enum-S} hold for all $jâˆˆ[â„“]$.
	In particular, we have that $D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰$ and $D_Zï¼·{â„“-j+1}â‰¥âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰$.
	
	Does the preceding result allow the possibility that $Ï€â†’1$?
	That is to say, Does \cref{ine:enum-Z} imply that
	the average of $ã’_â„“D_Zï¼·j$ is $1-o(1)$ as $â„“â†’âˆ$?
	The answer is
	\[Ã·1{â„“}âˆ‘_{j=1}^â„“ã’_â„“D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥Ã·1{â„“}âˆ‘_{j=âˆš{3â„“}}^â„“ã’_â„“Ã·{jÂ²}{3â„“}
		â‰ˆÃ·2{â„“ã‘â„“}âˆ«_{âˆš{3â„“}}^â„“ã‘j\,\diff j-ã’_â„“3â„“â‰ˆ1-Ã·{3.1}{ã‘â„“}-Ã·{âˆš3}{âˆšâ„“}.\]
	Now readers can see why I insist on loosening $hâ‚‚$ to
	the square root---I do not want to integrate $ã‘(hâ‚‚^{-1}(p))$.
	
	The next section estimates the eigenvalue, $â„“^{-Ï±}$, of random kernels.

\section{CLT Data of Random Matrix}\label{sec:CLT}

	This section is devoted to proving the eigen behavior of random kernels.
	To that end, we first need a concave eigenfunction $hï¼š[0,1]â†’[0,1]$.
	Let $Î±â‰”ã‘(ã‘â„“)/ã‘â„“$ and define
	\[h(z)â‰•\min(z,1-z)^Î±.\]
	From our experience in \cref{lem:eigen-Z}, the eigenvalue for reliable channels
	is a supremum taken over an open condition $0<H(W)<Î´$, and hence
	requires explicit bounds coming from FTPCs and HÃ¶lder tolls.
	The next lemma does that and shows that $h$ has a good eigenvalue if $H(W)<â„“^{-2}$.
	
	\begin{lem}[Typical CLT---reliable case]\label{lem:typical-HZ}
		Let $â„“â‰¥\max(3^q,eâ´,qâµ)$.
		Assume that $0<H(W)<â„“^{-2}$ and that
		$ğ”¾âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ satisfies \cref{ine:enum-Z}, then
		\[Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}<2â„“^{-1/2+3Î±}.
			\label{ine:good}\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		In the proof of \cref{lem:eigen-Z}, we classify $h(H(Wï¼·j))$
		into two cases---those with coset distance treated as $1$
		and those with nontrivial ($â‰¥5$) coset distance.
		Now that we have a spectrum of coset distances ($âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰$),
		there will be as many cases as there are coset distances.
		
		Consider first $jâ‰¤kâ‰”âŒŠâ„“^{1/2+5Î±/2}âŒ‹$.
		This is the case when $ğ˜â‚$ goes up but how far
		it can go is limited by FTPC$H$ (\cref{thm:ftpcH}).
		More precisely,
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·k))â‰¤khï¼ˆÃ·{H(Wï¼·1)+\dotsb+H(Wï¼·k)}kï¼‰	\\
			&	â‰¤khï¼ˆÃ·{â„“H(W)}kï¼‰â‰¤kâ„“^Î±h(H(W))k^{-Î±}â‰¤â„“^Î±(â„“^{1/2+5Î±/2})^{1-Î±}h(H(W))	\\
			&	â‰¤â„“^{Î±+1/2+5Î±/2-Î±/2-5Î±Â²/2}h(H(W))â‰¤â„“^{1/2+3Î±}h(H(W)).
		\end{align*}
		That is to say, the terms $h(H(Wï¼·j))$ with $jâ‰¤kâ‰”âŒŠâ„“^{1/2+5Î±/2}âŒ‹$ contribute
		$â„“^{1/2+3Î±}$ to the right-hand side of \cref{ine:good}.
		We now have another $â„“^{1/2+3Î±}$ to spare for larger $j$.
		
		Consider next $jâ‰¥kâ‰”âŒˆâ„“^{1/2+5Î±/2}âŒ‰$.
		This is the case where we can invoke FTPC$Z$ (\cref{thm:ftpcZ})
		to show that $ï¼º(Wï¼·j)$ is significantly smaller than $ï¼º(W)$.
		So we just sandwich FTPC$Z$ by HÃ¶lder tolls to translate it into control on $H$.
		In greater detail, with $zâ‰”ï¼º(W)$,
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			h(H(Wï¼·k))+H(H(Wï¼·{k+1}))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))â‰¤â„“\max_{jâ‰¥k}H(Wï¼·j)^Î±	\\
			&	â‰¤â„“\max_{jâ‰¥k}q^{3Î±}ï¼º(Wï¼·j)^{Î±/2}
				â‰¤â„“q^{3Î±}\max_{jâ‰¥k}f_Zï¼·j(z)^{Î±/2}\\
			&	â‰¤â„“q^{3Î±}\max_{jâ‰¥k}\(â„“(1+(q-1)z)^{â„“-1}((q-1)z)^{âŒˆjÂ²/3â„“âŒ‰}\)^{Î±/2}	\\
			&	â‰¤â„“q^{3Î±}\max_{jâ‰¥k}â„“^{Î±/2}e^{qzâ„“Î±/2}(qz)^{jÂ²Î±/6â„“}
				â‰¤â„“q^{3Î±}â„“^{Î±/2}e^{qzâ„“Î±/2}(qz)^{kÂ²Î±/6â„“}	\\
			&	â‰¤â„“q^{3Î±}â„“^{Î±/2}e^{qzâ„“Î±/2}(qz)^{ã‘(â„“)âµÎ±/6}
				â‰¤â„“q^{3Î±}â„“^{Î±/2}e^{qâ´Î±/2}(qz)^{ã‘(â„“)âµÎ±/6}	\\
			&	â‰¤â„“q^{3Î±}â„“^{Î±/2}e^{qâ´Î±/2}(qâ¸H(W))^{ã‘(â„“)âµÎ±/12}.	\label{for:raise}
		\end{align*}
		Here, the first inequality uses the largest $H(Wï¼·j)$ to bound the rest.
		The next inequality pays the HÃ¶lder toll.
		The next inequality applies FTPC$Z$.
		The next inequality applies the assumption that \cref{ine:enum-Z} holds.
		The next inequality simplifies $q-1$ and $â„“-1$ and the ceiling function.
		The next inequality knows that the maximum happens at $j=k$.
		The next inequality uses $kÂ²/â„“=â„“^{1-5Î±}/â„“=â„“^{5ã‘(ã‘â„“)/ã‘â„“}=e^{5ã‘(ã‘â„“)}=ã‘(â„“)âµ$.
		The next inequality pays the HÃ¶lder toll for the return trip
		to simplify $zâ‰¤qÂ³âˆš{H(W)}â‰¤qÂ³âˆš{H(W)}â‰¤qÂ³/â„“$ in the exponent.
		The next inequality pays the HÃ¶lder toll for the other $z$ in the base.
		
		We are half way to the goal.
		To show that \cref{for:raise}${}â‰¤â„“^{1/2+3Î±}h(H(W))$,
		raise them to the power of $12/Î±$ and take the quotient:
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			\(â„“q^{3Î±}â„“^{Î±/2}e^{qâ´Î±/2}(qâ¸H(W))^{ã‘(â„“)âµÎ±/12}\)^{12/Î±}
				\div\(â„“^{1/2+3Î±}h(H(W))\)^{12/Î±}	\\
			&	=â„“^{12/Î±}q^{36}â„“â¶e^{6qâ´}(qâ¸H(W))^{ã‘(â„“)âµ}â„“^{-6/Î±-36}H(W)^{-12}	\\
			&	=e^{6qâ´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-30}H(W)^{ã‘(â„“)âµ-12}q^{8ã‘(â„“)âµ+36}
				<e^{6qâ´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-30}â„“^{-2ã‘(â„“)âµ+24}q^{8ã‘(â„“)âµ+36}	\\
			&	<e^{6qâ´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-2ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{8ã‘(â„“)âµ+36}
				=e^{6qâ´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-0.4ã‘(â„“)âµ}â„“^{-1.6ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{8ã‘(â„“)âµ+36}	\\
			&	â‰¤e^{6qâ´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-0.4ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{-8ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{8ã‘(â„“)âµ+36}
				=e^{6qâ´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-0.4ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{36}	\\
			&	=e^{6qâ´}â„“^{-0.3ã‘(â„“)âµ}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-0.1ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{36}
				<e^{6qâ´}e^{-0.3ã‘(41)^2(qã‘3)â´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-0.1ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{36}	\\
			&	<e^{6qâ´}e^{-6.02qâ´}â„“^{6ã‘â„“-0.1ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{36}
				<â„“^{6ã‘â„“-0.1ã‘(â„“)âµ}q^{36}	\\
			&	=â„“^{6ã‘â„“-ã‘(â„“)âµ/15}â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)âµ/30}q^{36}
				=â„“^{6ã‘â„“-ã‘(â„“)âµ/15}q^{-5ã‘(19)âµ/30}q^{36}	\\
			&	<â„“^{6ã‘â„“-ã‘(â„“)âµ/15}q^{-36.8}q^{36}
				<â„“^{6ã‘â„“-ã‘(â„“)âµ/15}â‰¤â„“^{6ã‘â„“-ã‘(22)â´ã‘(â„“)/15}	\\
			&	<â„“^{6ã‘â„“-6.08}<â„“â°â‰¤1.
		\end{align*}
		The inequality involving $1.6$ uses $â„“â‰¥qâµ$.
		The inequality involving $0.3$ uses $â„“â‰¥\max(41,3^q)$.
		The inequality involving $/30$ uses $â„“â‰¥\max(19,qâµ)$.
		The inequality involving $/15$ uses $â„“â‰¥22$.
		I have just showed that \cref{for:raise}$/â„“^{-1/2+3Î±}h(ğ˜_0)$
		is less than $1$, with and hence without the power of $12/Î±$.
		
		To summarize, we saw that $h(H(Wï¼·k))+H(H(Wï¼·{k+1}))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))â‰¤{}
		$\cref{for:raise}${}â‰¤â„“^{-1/2+3Î±}h(ğ˜_0)$.
		Hence the summands $h(H(Wï¼·j))$ with $jâ‰¥kâ‰”âŒˆâ„“^{1/2+5Î±/2}âŒ‰$ contribute
		$â„“^{1/2+3Î±}$ to the right-hand side of \cref{ine:good}.
		Both small $j$ case and large $j$ case together
		contribute $2â„“^{1/2+3Î±}$, as desired.
		This is the end of \cref{lem:typical-HZ}.
	\end{proof}
	
	The following is automatic by duality given \cref{lem:typical-HZ}.
	
	\begin{lem}[Typical CLT---noisy case]\label{lem:typical-HS}
		Assume $1-â„“^{-2}<H(W)<1$ and that $ğ”¾âˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ satisfies \cref{ine:enum-S}.
		Then
		\[Ã·{â„“h(H(Wï¼·1))+â„“h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+â„“h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}<2â„“^{-1/2+3Î±}.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	We are left with the case when $â„“^{-2}<H(W)<1-â„“^{-2}$.
	In this zone, neither of FTPC$Z$ and FTPC$S$ can help because the HÃ¶lder tolls
	do not yield any meaningful estimate on $ï¼º(W)$ or $ï¼³(W)$ to begin with.
	The contribution I make here is to reduce the estimate of $H(Wï¼·j)$, with large $j$,
	to noisy-channel coding and to reduce the large $j$ cases to wiretap-channel coding.
	
	\begin{lem}[Typical CLT---mediocre case]\label{lem:typical-HH}
		Assume $â„“^{-2}â‰¤H(W)â‰¤1-â„“^{-2}$ and that
		$ğ”¾âˆˆ\GL(â„“,q)$ is drawn uniformly at random.
		Then, with exceptional probability $2â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/20}$,
		\[Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}<4â„“^{-1/2+3Î±}.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		The desired inequality is the sum of the following three inequalities:
		\begin{gather*}
			âˆ‘_{j=âŒˆH(W)â„“+â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‰+1}^â„“h(H(Wï¼·j))<â„“^{1/2+Î±},	\label{ine:bob}\\
			âˆ‘_{j=âŒŠH(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‹+1}^{âŒˆH(W)â„“+â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‰}h(H(Wï¼·j))<2â„“^{1/2+Î±},	\\
			âˆ‘_{j=1}^{âŒŠH(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‹}h(H(Wï¼·j))<â„“^{1/2+Î±}.	\label{ine:eve}
		\end{gather*}
		Here $â„“^{-2}â‰¤H(W)â‰¤1-â„“^{-2}$ is used to rewrite the denominator $h(H(W))â‰¥â„“^{2Î±}$.
		The middle one is trivial because $hâ‰¤2^{-Î±}$.
		\Cref{ine:bob} will be proved in \cref{sec:gallager}.
		\Cref{ine:eve} will be proved in \cref{sec:hayashi}.
	\end{proof}
	
	Let me comment on the heuristic behind those inequalities:
	To show that $h(H(Wï¼·j))$ is in general small, we first
	have to identify at which end each $H(Wï¼·j)$ will be.
	We learned from the $\loll$ case that
	smaller indices usually imply noisier synthetic channels.
	So we believe that for $jâ‰ªâ„“H(W)$, the conditional entropy $H(Wï¼·j)$ is high,
	and for $jâ‰«â„“H(W)$, the conditional entropy $H(Wï¼·j)$ is low.
	We also believe that there is a ambiguous zone $jâ‰ˆH(Wï¼·j)$
	where the conditional entropy can be anywhere.
	From the CLT regime of random coding, we believe that the width
	of the ambiguous zone should be on the order of $âˆšâ„“$, hence the partition.
	
	Once we have \cref{lem:typical-HZ,lem:typical-HS} (proved above) and
	\cref{lem:typical-HH} (part of whose proof is in the next two sections
	and no later), we can conclude the eigen behavior of a random kernel $ğ”¾$.
	
	\begin{thm}[Typical CLT behavior]\label{thm:typical-CLT}
		Assume $W$ is a $q$-ary channel.
		Assume $â„“â‰¥\max(3^q,eâ´,qâµ)$.
		Assume $ğ”¾âˆˆ\GL(â„“,q)$ is drawn uniformly at random.
		Then, with failing probability at most $2â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/20}+6â„“q^{-âˆšâ„“/13}$,
		\[Ã·{h(H(Wï¼·1))+h(H(Wï¼·2))+\dotsb+h(H(Wï¼·{â„“}))}{â„“h(H(W))}<4â„“^{-1/2+3Î±}.\]
	\end{thm}
	
	This is equivalent to saying that $4â„“^{-1/2+3Î±}$ is the eigenvalue, or $Ï±â‰ˆ1/2-4Î±$.
	This exponent approaches $1/2$ as $â„“â†’âˆ$.
	Hence we know, at least, that there is a chance to achieve $Ï+2Ï€â†’1$.
	How exactly \cref{thm:typical-LDP,thm:typical-CLT} imply that all $Ï+2Ï€<1$
	are possible will be explained in \cref{sec:signify}.

\section{Symmetrization and Universal Bound} \label{sec:prepare}

	Before the actual proof of \cref{ine:bob,ine:eve},
	There are two more tools to be developed.
	First is a reduction borrowed from \cite{MT14} that says, instead of considering
	general $q$-ary channel $W$, it suffices to consider a symmetric one $ËœW$.
	A convenient consequence is that
	the uniform input distribution will achieve capacity.
	This helps simplify the inequalities further.
	
	\begin{lem}[Symmetrization]
		For any $q$-ary channel, there is a symmetric $q$-ary channel $ËœW$ such that
		$H(ËœW)=H(W)$ and $H(ËœWï¼·j)=H(Wï¼·j)$ for all $jâˆˆ[â„“]$.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		The strategy here is close to what we did in \cref{cha:dual}---one can establish
		an equivalence relation $Wâ‰…ËœW$ on channels and show that channel parameters
		and channel transformations respect the equivalence relation.
		
		Please see \cite[Definition~6 and Lemmas 7 and~8]{MT14}
		plus the arguments in between for the formal treatment.
	\end{proof}
	
	The second tool, built on top of the first one, is an inequality
	concerning Gallager's $\Eo$ function.
	Let us start from the definition.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		Define \emph{Gallager's E-null function}
		\[\Eo(t)â‰”-ã‘âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}Q(x)W(yï½œx)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t}\]
		and its complement
		\[Â¯\Eo(t)â‰”ã‘âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}W(x,y)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t}.\]
	\end{dfn}
	
	$Â¯\Eo$ is said to be the complement of $\Eo$ as
	\begin{align*}
		Â¯\Eo(t)
		&	=ã‘âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}(q^{-1}W(yï½œx))^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t}	\\
		&	=tã‘q+ã‘âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}q^{-1}W(yï½œx)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t}=tã‘q-\Eo(t).
	\end{align*}
	Or $\Eo(t)+Â¯\Eo(t)=tã‘q$ in short.
	That $Q$ is uniform is used, otherwise
	a non-constant cannot penetrate the summations.
	The E-null function and its complement are
	deeply connected to the following family of measures.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		For any $tâˆˆ[-2/5,1]$, define the \emph{$t$-tilted probability mass function}
		$\Wtï¼šğ’³Ã—ğ’´â†’[0,1]$ as in \cite[Definition~1]{CS07}:
		\[\Wt(x,y)â‰”Ã·{\(âˆ‘_{Î¾âˆˆğ’³}W(Î¾,y)^Ã·1{1+t}\)^{1+t}}
			{âˆ‘_{Î·âˆˆğ’´}\(âˆ‘_{Î¾âˆˆğ’³}W(Î¾,Î·)^Ã·1{1+t}\)^{1+t}}
			Ã—Ã·{W(x,y)^Ã·1{1+t}}{âˆ‘_{Î¾âˆˆğ’³}W(Î¾,y)^Ã·1{1+t}}\]
	\end{dfn}
	
	When $t=0$, the tilted $\Wt(x,y)$ falls back to its italic origin $W(x,y)$.
	These measures can be interpreted as follows:
	$\Wt$ behaves like a channel with a dedicated input distribution.
	The first fraction in the definition
	specifies the output distribution $\Wt(y)$.
	The second fraction specifies the posterior distribution
	$\Wt(xï½œy)$ when $y$ is known.
	As $\Wt$ is not an actual channel, it is not meaningful to
	alter the input distribution and ask for the corresponding output.
	Like the symmetrization technique, all that matters is that
	we can compute the conditional entropies as if they were real channels.
	Quantities we are interested in are listed below.
	
	\begin{dfn}
		Let $H_e$ be the base-$e$ entropy.
		Let $H_e(\Wt)$ be $H_e(\Xtï½œ\Yt)$ where
		$(\Xt,\Yt)$ is a random tuple that obeys $\Wt$.
		Let $H_e(\Xtâ†¾y)$ be the entropy of
		the posterior distribution of $\Xt$ given $\Yt=y$;
		to be specific, $H_e(\Xtâ†¾y)=âˆ‘_{xâˆˆX}\Wt(xï½œy)ã‘\Wt(xï½œy)$.
	\end{dfn}
	
	Then $Â¯\Eo$ and $\Wt$ are connected in the following manner---$\Wt$
	is a family of channels that â€œlives along the pathâ€ $Â¯\Eo(t)$.
	
	\begin{lem}[Second derivative]\label{lem:dtdt}
		\cite[Formula (13) and~(19)]{CS07}
		For $tâˆˆ[0,1]$,
		\def\dt{Ã·\diff{\diff t}}
		\def\dtdt{Ã·{\diffÂ²}{\diff tÂ²}}
		\[\dtÂ¯\Eo(t)=Â¯\Eo'(t)=H_e(\Wt)\]
		and
		\begin{align*}
			\dtdtÂ¯\Eo(t)
			&	=Â¯\Eo''(t)=\dt H_e(\Wt)
				=Ã·1{1+t}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}\Wt(y)âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}\Wt(xï½œy)ã‘(\Wt(xï½œy))^2	\\*
			&\kern10em +Ã·t{1+t}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}\Wt(y)H_e(\Xtâ†¾y)^2-H_e(\Wt)^2.
				\label{equ:holomorph}
		\end{align*}
	\end{lem}
	
	Since $Â¯\Eo''(t)$ and every other term in \cref{equ:holomorph}
	is holomorphic in $t$, the equation holds in any region that assumes no poles.
	In particular, $-2/5â‰¤tâ‰¤1$ is such a region.
	In that region, the next lemma helps bounding the terms in $Â¯\Eo''(t)$.
	
	\begin{lem}[Second moment]\label{lem:moment}
		If $wâ‚,wâ‚‚,â€¦w_q$ are positive numbers that totals to $1$, then
		\[âˆ‘_iw_iã‘(w_i)^2â‰¤\begin{Bmatrix}
			ã‘(q)^2	&	â€ for â€ qâ‰¥3 \\
			0.563		&	â€ for â€ q=2
		\end{Bmatrix}â‰¤1.2ã‘(q)^2.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\Cref{lem:dtdt} (with the holomorphic continuation to $t=-2/5$) and
	\cref{lem:moment} jointly imply the following universal quadratic bound.
	
	\begin{lem}[Universal quadratic bound]\label{lem:quadratic}
		\cite[Theorem~2]{CS07}
		Let $W$ be a $q$-ary channel.
		Assume the uniform input distribution.
		Then Gallager's E-null function satisfies
		\begin{gather*}
			\Eo(0)=0,	\\
			\Eo'(0)=I(W)ã‘q,	\\
			\Eo''(t)â‰¥-2ã‘(q)^2
		\end{gather*}
		for all $tâˆˆ[-2/5,1]$.
		In particular, it satisfies
		\[\Eo(t)â‰¥I(W)tã‘q-t^2ã‘(q)^2.\]
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		With \cref{lem:moment},
		$âˆ‘_{xâˆˆğ’³}\Wt(xï½œy)(ã‘\Wt(xï½œy))^2â‰¤1.2ã‘(q)^2$ can be stated.
		Now \cref{equ:holomorph} becomes
		\begin{align*}
			Â¯\Eo''(t)
			&	â‰¤Ã·1{1+t}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}\Wt(y)Â·1.2ã‘(q)^2
				+Ã·{\max(0,t)}{1+t}âˆ‘_{yâˆˆğ’´}\Wt(y)ã‘(q)^2	\\
			&	â‰¤Ã·1{1+t}Â·1.2ã‘(q)^2+Ã·{\max(0,t)}{1+t}ã‘(q)^2â‰¤2ã‘(q)^2
		\end{align*}
		for all $tâˆˆ[-2/5,1]$.
		
		Since $\Eo(t)$ is a linear function $tã‘q$ minus $Â¯\Eo(t)$,
		their first derivatives sum to $ã‘q$ while
		their second derivatives are opposite.
		That means that $\Eo(t)=\Eo(0)+\Eo(0)'t+\Eo''(Ï„)tÂ²/2$, for some $Ï„âˆˆ[-2/5,1]$,
		and therefore $â‰¥0+I(W)tã‘q-tÂ²ã‘(q)Â²$.
	\end{proof}
	
	After seeing that it suffices to consider symmetric channels and that $E(t)$ has
	a universal quadratic bound, we are now ready to prove \cref{ine:bob,ine:eve}.

\section{Noisy-Channel Random Coding}\label{sec:gallager}

	This section and the next take advantage of the universal bound developed
	three lines ago and continues proving \cref{thm:typical-CLT}.
	This section deals with
	\[âˆ‘_{i=âŒˆH(W)â„“+â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‰+1}^â„“h(H(Wï¼·i))<â„“^{-1/2+Î±}\tagcopy{ine:bob}\]
	by passing it to an estimate that captures the performance of noisy-channel coding.
	
	\begin{proof}[Proof of \cref{ine:bob}]
		Owing to $h$'s concavity, the left-hand side
		of \cref{ine:bob} is first simplified into
		\[âˆ‘_{i=j+1}^â„“h(H(Wï¼·k))â‰¤(â„“-j)hï¼ˆÃ·1{â„“-j}âˆ‘_{i=j+1}^â„“H(Wï¼·i)ï¼‰,
			\label{ine:bob-chain}\]
		where $jâ‰”âŒˆH(W)â„“+â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‰$ for short.
		It suffices to prove that the right-hand side is less than $â„“^{-1/2+Î±}$.
		But what lies inside the $h$ on the right-hand side is a sum of $H(Wï¼·i)$,
		which is equal to, by the chain rule, $H(U_{j+1}^â„“ï½œUâ‚^jYâ‚^â„“)$.
		In order to prove \cref{ine:bob-chain}, I will then show
		\[(â„“-j)hï¼ˆÃ·1{â„“-j}H(U_{j+1}^â„“ï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^j)ï¼‰<â„“^{-1/2+Î±}.\label{ine:bob-block}\]
		
		But what is $H(U_{j+1}^â„“ï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^j)$?
		It measures the equivocation at Bob's end when $Uâ‚^j$ is known to Bob.
		In other words, we may as well pretend that
		\begin{itemize}
			\item	there is a random rectangular full-rank matrix $ğ”¾'$ with
					$â„“$ columns and only $kâ‰”â„“-j=âŒŠI(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‹$ rows,
			\item	Alice computes and sends $Xâ‚^â„“â‰”U_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾'$ to Bob, and
			\item	Bob attempts to decode $Ë†U_{j+1}^â„“$ upon receiving $Yâ‚^â„“$ using
					the maximum a posteriori decoder.
		\end{itemize}
		The equivocation is thus, by Fano's inequality,
		bounded in terms of the probability that Bob fails to decode $U_{j+1}^â„“$:
		\begin{align*}
			H(U_{j+1}^â„“ï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^j)
			&	â‰¤-ï¼°ã‘_qï¼°-(1-ï¼°)ã‘_q(1-ï¼°)+ï¼°ã‘_q(q^k-1)	\\
			&	â‰¤-ï¼°ã‘_qï¼°+Ã·{ï¼°}{ã‘q}+ï¼°=ï¼°Â·ï¼ˆÃ·{1-ã‘ï¼°}{ã‘q}+kï¼‰.	\label{ine:Fano}
		\end{align*}
		Here $ï¼°$ is the probability that Bob fails to decode, $Ë†U_{j+1}^â„“â‰ U_{j+1}^â„“$.
		
		In what follows is how to compute Bob's block error probability.
		The generator matrix $ğ”¾'$ used by Alice is selected uniformly
		from the ensemble of full-rank $k$-by-$â„“$ matrices.
		The difference of every pair of codewords
		distributes uniformly on $ğ”½_q^â„“ã€\{0â‚^â„“\}$.
		Over symmetric channels, the difference alone determines the difficulty
		of decoding because $W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œÎ¾â‚^â„“+xâ‚^â„“)=W^â„“(Ïƒâ‚^â„“(yâ‚^â„“)ï½œxâ‚^â„“)$ for
		some component-wise involution $Ïƒâ‚^â„“$ on $ğ’´^â„“$ depending on $Î¾â‚^â„“$.
		Therefore, Gallager's bound applies.
		To elaborate, let $tâˆˆ[0,1]$.
		Then Bob's average error probability satisfies
		\cite[Inequalities (5.6.2) to~(5.6.14)]{Gallager68}
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			ğ”¼ï¼°=ğ”¼EP\{â€ Bob fails to decode â€ U_{j+1}^â„“â€  given â€ ğ”¾',Yâ‚^â„“\} \\
			&=	ğ”¼âˆ‘_{uâ‚^k}Ã·1{q^k}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œuâ‚^kğ”¾')
				ğ•€\{â€ Bob has â€ Ë†U_{j+1}^â„“â‰ uâ‚^kâ€  given â€ ğ”¾',yâ‚^â„“ï½œU_{j+1}^â„“=uâ‚^k\}	\\
			&=	ğ”¼âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)
				ğ•€\{â€ Bob has â€ Ë†U_{j+1}^â„“â‰ 0â‚^kâ€  given â€ ğ”¾',yâ‚^â„“ï½œU_{j+1}^â„“=0â‚^k\}	\\
			&â‰¤	ğ”¼âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)\biggl(âˆ‘_{vâ‚^kâ‰ 0â‚^k}
				ğ•€\{â€ Bob prefers â€ vâ‚^kâ€  over â€ 0â‚^kâ€  given â€ ğ”¾',yâ‚^â„“\}\biggr)^t \\
			&â‰¤	ğ”¼âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{vâ‚^kâ‰ 0â‚^k}
				Ã·{W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œvâ‚^kğ”¾')^Ã·1{1+t}}{W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}}ï¼‰^t \\
			&=	ğ”¼âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}
				ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{vâ‚^kâ‰ 0â‚^k}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œvâ‚^kğ”¾')^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^t \\
			&â‰¤	âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}
				ï¼ˆğ”¼âˆ‘_{vâ‚^kâ‰ 0â‚^k}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œvâ‚^kğ”¾')^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^t \\
			&=	âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“â‰ 0â‚^â„“}
				Ã·{q^k-1}{q^â„“-1}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œxâ‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^t \\
			&â‰¤	q^{kt}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}
				ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“â‰ 0â‚^â„“}Ã·1{q^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œxâ‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^t \\
			&â‰¤	q^{kt}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œ0â‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}
				ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}Ã·1{q^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œxâ‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^t \\
			&=	q^{kt}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}Ã·1{q^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œxâ‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰
				ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}Ã·1{q^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œxâ‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{t} \\
				%% nontrivial math (symmetry)
			&=	q^{kt}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}Ã·1{q^â„“}W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œxâ‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t} \\
			&=	q^{kt}âˆ‘_{yâ‚^â„“}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{xâ‚^â„“}Q^â„“(xâ‚^â„“)W^â„“(yâ‚^â„“ï½œxâ‚^â„“)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t} \\
			&=	\exp(ktã‘q-(\emph{the E-null function of }W^â„“)(t)) \\
			&=	\exp(ktã‘q-â„“\Eo(t)).
		\end{align*}
		
		In summary, the average block error probability
		$ğ”¼ï¼°=ğ”¼EP\{$Bob fails to decode $U_{j+1}^â„“$ given $ğ”¾'\}$
		is no more than $\exp(ktã‘q-â„“\Eo(t))$ whenever $0â‰¤tâ‰¤1$.
		Recall the universal quadratic bound developed in \cref{lem:quadratic}:
		$\Eo(t)â‰¥I(W)tã‘q-t^2ã‘(q)^2$.
		We obtain that the exponent is
		\begin{align*}
			ktã‘q-â„“\Eo(t)
			&	â‰¤(I(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±})tã‘q-â„“\Eo(t) \\
			&	â‰¤(I(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±})tã‘q-â„“(I(W)tã‘q-t^2ã‘(q)^2) \\
			&	=(â„“tã‘q-â„“^{1/2+Î±})tã‘q \\
			\shortintertext{(redeem the inequality at $t=â„“^{-1/2+Î±}/2ã‘q$)}
			&â†¦	(â„“â„“^{-1/2+Î±}/2-â„“^{1/2+Î±})â„“^{-1/2+Î±}/2 \\
			&=	-â„“^{2Î±}/4 = -â„“^{2ã‘(ã‘â„“)/ã‘â„“}/4 = -ã‘(â„“)^2/4.
		\end{align*}
		So far, the average error probability $ğ”¼ï¼°$ is shown to be
		less than $\exp(-ã‘(â„“)Â²/4)=â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/4}$.
		
		Run Markov's inequality with cutoff $â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/20}$.
		To put it another way, we sample a random full-rank matrix $ğ”¾'âˆˆğ”½_q^{kÃ—â„“}$
		and reject it if $P\{$Bob fails to decode $U_{j+1}^â„“$ given $ğ”¾'\}â‰¥â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}$.
		Then the rejecting probability is $â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/20}$ because $1/20+1/5=1/4$.
		An upper bound on Bob's error probability being $ï¼°<â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}$,
		an upper bound on Bob's equivocation is
		\[H(U_{j+1}^â„“ï½œYâ‚^â„“Uâ‚^j)â‰¤â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}ï¼ˆÃ·{1-ã‘â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}}{ã‘q}+kï¼‰
			=â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}ï¼ˆÃ·{1+ã‘(â„“)^2/5}{ã‘q}+kï¼‰\]
		by \cref{ine:Fano}.
		Plugging the right-hand side into $kh(â€ this placeâ€ /k)$,
		we derive that the left-hand side of \cref{ine:bob-block} is less than
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			khï¼ˆÃ·{â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}}kï¼ˆÃ·{1+ã‘(â„“)^2/5}{ã‘q}+kï¼‰ï¼‰
				=kÂ·ï¼ˆâ„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}ï¼ˆÃ·{1+ã‘(â„“)^2/5}{kã‘q}+1ï¼‰ï¼‰^Î±	\\
			&	=â„“^{-Î±ã‘(â„“)/5}kÂ·ï¼ˆÃ·{1+ã‘(â„“)^2/5}{kã‘q}+1ï¼‰^Î±
				<â„“^{-Î±ã‘(â„“)/5}â„“Â·ï¼ˆÃ·{1+ã‘(â„“)^2/5}{â„“ã‘q}+1ï¼‰^Î±	\\
			&	<â„“^{-Î±ã‘(â„“)/5}Â·â„“Â·2^Î± = 2^Î±â„“ã‘(â„“)^{-ã‘(â„“)/5}.
		\end{align*}
		The first inequality uses that the left-hand side increases
		monotonically in $k$ and $k$ is $â„“-j=âŒŠI(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‹<â„“$.
		The second inequality uses the assumption $â„“â‰¥2$.
		The quantity at the end of the chain of inequalities
		decays to $0$ as $â„“â†’âˆ$, so eventually it becomes less than $â„“^{1/2+Î±}$,
		the right-hand side of \cref{ine:bob-block}.
		This proves that \cref{ine:bob,ine:bob-chain} hold with
		failing probability $â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/20}$ as soon as $â„“$ is large enough. % 20
		
		The lower bound on $â„“$ in the statement of
		\cref{lem:typical-HH} is large enough ($â„“>20$).
		Hence \cref{ine:bob}, the first half of \cref{lem:typical-HH}, is settled.
	\end{proof}
	
	That random kernels make $h(H(Wï¼·j))$ small for large $jâ‰«â„“H(W)$ is the first half;
	the next section settles the second half of \cref{lem:typical-HH},
	making $h(H(Wï¼·j))$ small for small $jâ‰ªâ„“H(W)$.

\section{Wiretap-Channel Random Coding}\label{sec:hayashi}

	This subsection contains the very last ingredient
	of the proof of \cref{lem:typical-HH} and \cref{thm:typical-CLT}.
	We dealt with \cref{ine:bob} in the last subsection.
	We now deal with
	\[âˆ‘_{i=1}^{âŒŠH(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‹}h(H(Wï¼·i))<â„“^{1/2+Î±}.\tagcopy{ine:eve}\]
	by passing it to an estimate that captures
	the performance of wiretap-channel coding.
	
	\begin{proof}[Proof of \cref{ine:eve}]
		Similar to how we motivated \cref{ine:bob-block},
		we hereby apply Jensen's inequality and the chain rule of
		conditional entropy to simplify \cref{ine:eve}.
		The left-hand side becomes $jh(H(U_1^jï½œY_1^â„“)/j)$
		where $jâ‰”âŒŠH(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‹$ for short.
		(This is not the same $j$ as in the last subsection.)
		The input being uniform, the argument of $h$ is
		$H(U_1^jï½œY_1^â„“)/j=1-I(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“)/j$, which can be replaced by
		$I(U_1^jï½œY_1^â„“)/j$ thanks to the symmetry $h(1-z)=h(z)$.
		We will show
		\[jhï¼ˆÃ·1jI(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“)ï¼‰<â„“^{1/2+Î±}.\label{ine:eve-block}\]
		
		But what is $I(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“)$?
		It is the amount of information Eve learns from
		wiretapping $Y_1^â„“$ if Eve knows that $U_{j+1}^â„“$ are junk.
		In other words, we may pretend that
		\begin{itemize}
			\item	Alice transmits $X_1^â„“â‰”U_1^jV_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$, wherein $U_1^j$ are
					the confidential bits and $V_{j+1}^â„“$ are the obfuscating bits,
			\item	Bob receives $X_1^â„“$ in full, and
			\item	Eve learns $Y_1^â„“$.
		\end{itemize}
		This context falls back to (a special case of) the traditional setup
		of wiretap channels \cite{Wyner75} where various bounds are studied,
		some in terms of Gallager's E-null function.
		
		Here are some preliminaries
		to control the information leaked to Eve.
		We follow the blueprint of how Hayashi derived
		the secrecy exponent in \cite[Inequality~(21)]{Hayashi06}.
		Consider the communication protocol depicted in \cref{fig:alice}:
		Karl fixes a kernel $ğ”¾âˆˆ\GL(â„“,q)$ and everyone knows $ğ”¾$.
		Alice chooses the confidential message $U_1^â„“$.
		Vincent chooses the obfuscating bits $V_{j+1}^â„“$.
		Charlie generates $Y_1^â„“$ by
		plugging $X_1^â„“â‰”U_1^jV_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$ into a simulator of $W^â„“$.
		Eve learns $Y_1^â„“$ and is interested in knowing $U_1^j$ alone.
		So the channel on topic is the composition of Vincent and Charlie.
		Notation:
		Running out of symbols, we all use $â„™$ with proper subscripts
		to indicate the corresponding probability measures.
		That said, indices in the subscript will be omitted.
		As Eve is interested in the relation between $U_1^j$ and $Y_1^â„“$,
		let $Y_1^â„“â†¾Gu_1^j$ be the r.v.\ that follows
		the posterior distribution of $Y_1^â„“$ given $ğ”¾=G$ and $U_1^j=u_1^j$.
		More formally, $â„™_{Yâ†¾Gu}(y_1^â„“)
			=â„™_{Y|ğ”¾U}(y_1^â„“ï½œG,u_1^â„“)=â„™_{ğ”¾  UY}(G,u_1^j,y_1^â„“)/â„™_{ğ”¾U}(G,u_1^j)$.
		We could have defined $Y_1^â„“â†¾G$ to be the
		posterior distribution of $Y_1^â„“$ given $ğ”¾=G$;
		but it is simply the same distribution as $Y_1^â„“$ since $U_1^jV_{j+1}^â„“G$
		traverses all inputs uniformly regardless of the choice of $G$.
		That is, $â„™_{Y|ğ”¾}(y_1^â„“ï½œG)=â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)$ for all $y_1^â„“âˆˆğ’´^â„“$.
		
		\begin{figure}
			\tikz{
				\draw[nodes={right,draw,align=center}]
					(.7,0)coordinate(X)
					            node(K){Karl\\chooses\\$ğ”¾$}
					(K.east)+(X)node(A){Alice\\chooses\\$U_1^j$}
					(A.east)+(X)node(V){Vincent\\chooses\\$V_{j+1}^â„“$}
					(V.east)+(X)node(N)
								{$X_1^â„“$ let\\to be\\$U_1^jV_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$}
					(N.east)+(X)node(C){Charlie\\generates\\$Y_1^â„“$ by $X_1^â„“$}
					(C.east)+(X)node(E){Eve\\learns\\$ğ”¾,Y_1^â„“$}
				;
				\graph[use existing nodes]{
					K -> A -> V -> N -> C -> E
				};
				\draw[dotted]
					(V.west|-C.north)+(-.3,.3)-|($(C.south east)+(.3,-.8)$)-|
					node[pos=.25,above]{The channel Eve cares about}cycle;
			}
			\caption{
				A finer setup for Hayashi's secrecy exponent.
				Charlie generates $Y_1^â„“$ such that
				$X_1^â„“â‰”U_1^jV_{j+1}^â„“ğ”¾$ and $Y_1^â„“$ follow $W^â„“$.
				Despite of the seemingly sequential structure,
				Karl, Alice, and Vincent work independently.
			}\label{fig:alice}
		\end{figure}
		
		Fix $G$ as an instance of $ğ”¾$.
		Let $I_e$ be the base-$e$ mutual information.
		The channel Eve cares about leaks information of this amount:
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œG)=âˆ‘_{u_1^jy_1^â„“}â„™_{UY|ğ”¾}(u_1^j,y_1^â„“ï½œG)
				ã‘Ã·{â„™_{Y|ğ”¾U}(y_1^â„“ï½œG,u_1^j)}{â„™_{Y|ğ”¾}(y_1^â„“ï½œG)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{u_1^j}â„™_U(u_1^j)âˆ‘_{y_1^â„“}â„™_{Y|ğ”¾U}(y_1^â„“ï½œG,u_1^j)
				ã‘Ã·{â„™_{Y|ğ”¾U}(y_1^â„“ï½œG,u_1^j)}{â„™_{Y|ğ”¾}(y_1^â„“ï½œG)}	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{u_1^j}â„™_U(u_1^j)âˆ‘_{y_1^â„“}â„™_{Yâ†¾Gu}(y_1^â„“)
				ã‘Ã·{â„™_{Yâ†¾Gu}(y_1^â„“)}{â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)}
				=âˆ‘_{u_1^j}â„™_U(u_1^j)ğ”»(Y_1^â„“â†¾Gu_1^j\|Y_1).	\label{for:fix-kld}
		\end{align*}
		$ğ”»(Y_1^â„“â†¾Gu_1^j\|Y_1^â„“)$ is the Kullback--Leibler divergence
		from the posterior distribution of $Y_1^â„“$ given $G,u_1^j$
		to the coarsest distribution $Y_1^â„“$.
		We are to take expectation over $ğ”¾$ to find the average information leak
		since we are interested in Markov's inequality.
		\Cref{for:fix-kld} gives rise to
		\begin{align*}
			ğ”¼I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)
			&	=âˆ‘_Gâ„™_ğ”¾(G)I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œG)	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_Gâ„™_ğ”¾(G)âˆ‘_{u_1^j}â„™_U(u_1^j)ğ”»(Y_1^â„“â†¾Gu_1^j\|Y_1^â„“).
				\label{for:exp-kld}
		\end{align*}
		We now discover that there are redundancies
		in traversing all $G$ and $u_1^â„“$:
		After all, $X_1^j$ is $u_1^jV_{j+1}^â„“G=u_1^j0_{j+1}^â„“G+0_1^jV_{j+1}^â„“G$,
		which is a fixed linear combination of the first $j$ rows
		plus a random vector from the span of the bottom $â„“-j$ rows.
		When $V_1^â„“$ varies, the track of $X_1^â„“$ forms an affine subspace of
		$ğ”½_q^â„“$, a \emph{coset code} as in the context of the fundamental theorems.
		So what matters is the distribution of this coset code.
		
		In the aforementioned manner, we replace the uniform ensemble of $(ğ”¾,U_1^j)$
		by the uniform ensemble of $ğ•‚$, a rank-$(â„“-j)$ affine subspace of $ğ”½_q^â„“$,
		where $jâ‰”âŒŠH(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}âŒ‹$.
		Karl and Alice together choose $ğ•‚$ uniformly.
		Vincent chooses $X_1^â„“âˆˆğ•‚$ uniformly.
		Charlie generates $Y_1^â„“$ by entering $X_1^â„“$ into a simulator of $W^â„“$.
		See \cref{fig:karl} for the depiction of the new scheme.
		Hence \cref{for:exp-kld} induces
		\[ğ”¼I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)=âˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)ğ”»(Y_1^â„“â†¾K\|Y_1^â„“)\]
		where $Y_1^â„“â†¾K$ is the a posteriori distribution of $Y_1^â„“$ given $ğ•‚=K$.
		Suddenly, the quantity $ğ”¼I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)$ we are interested in
		turns into the mutual information $I_e(ğ•‚ï¼›Y_1^â„“)$ between $ğ•‚$ and $Y_1^â„“$
		as $ğ•‚$ replaces the role of $U_1^j$ in \cref{for:fix-kld}.
		Recall that in \cref{lem:quadratic} the mutual information
		is the derivative of Gallager's E-null function.
		We exploit this.
		Define the double-stroke E-null function for $(ğ•‚,Y_1^â„“)$ as follows
		\[ğ”¼_0(t)â‰”-ã‘âˆ‘_{y_1^â„“}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t}.\]
		Then $ğ”¼_0'(0)=I_e(ğ•‚ï¼›Y_1^â„“)=ğ”¼I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)$.
		Owing to the concavity of the E-null function,
		$ğ”¼_0'(0)â‰¤ğ”¼_0(t)/t$ whenever $-2/5â‰¤t<0$.
		Recap:
		To bound the average leaked information $ğ”¼I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)$
		it suffices to bound $I_e(ğ•‚ï¼›Y_1^â„“)$, which is then morphed
		to bound $ğ”¼_0'(0)$ from above and to bound $ğ”¼_0(t)$ from below.
		
		\begin{figure}
			\tikz{
				\draw[nodes={right,draw,align=center}]
					(.7,0)coordinate(X)
					            node(K){Karl and \\Alice choose\\$ğ•‚$}
					(K.east)+(X)node(V){Vincent\\chooses\\$X_1^â„“âˆˆğ•‚$}
					(V.east)+(X)node(C){Charlie\\generates\\$Y_1^â„“$ by $X_1^â„“$}
					(C.east)+(X)node(E){Eve\\learns\\$Y_1^â„“$};
				\graph[use existing nodes]{
					K -> V -> C -> E
				};
				\draw[dotted]
					(V.west|-C.north)+(-.3,.3)-|($(C.south east)+(.3,-.8)$)-|
					node[pos=.25,above]{The modified channel}cycle;
			}
			\caption{
				A simplified setup for Hayashi's secrecy exponent.
				Charlie generates $Y_1^â„“$ such that $X_1^â„“$ and $Y_1^â„“$ follow $W^â„“$.
			}\label{fig:karl}
		\end{figure}
		
		The double-stroke E-null function is bounded as below.
		Assume $-2/5â‰¤t<0$.
		Let $s$ be $-t/(1+t)$; so $0<sâ‰¤2/3$ and $(1+s)(1+t)=1$.
		For any fixed $K$ and fixed $x_1^â„“âˆˆK$, the base of the $(1+t)$-th root
		in the definition of the double-stroke E-null function is
		\begin{align*}
			â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK) 
			&	=âˆ‘_{Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{X|ğ•‚}(Î¾_1^â„“ï½œK)â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œÎ¾_1^â„“)	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆK}q^jâ„™_X(Î¾_1^â„“)â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œÎ¾_1^â„“)
				=âˆ‘_{Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆK}q^jâ„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)	\\
			&	=q^jï¼ˆâ„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)+âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“â‰ Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰	\\
			&	=q^jï¼ˆâ„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)+â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰.
		\end{align*}
		Here $â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)$ is a temporary shorthand for the summation
		of $â„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)$ over $Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆK$ that excludes $x_1^â„“$.
		Raise $â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)$ to the power of $s$;
		it becomes $q^{js}(â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)+â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“))^s
			â‰¤q^{js}(â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s+â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s)$ by sub-additivity.
		Put that aside; raise $â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)$ to the power of $1+s=1/(1+t)$:
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)^{1+s} = â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)^s
				=âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}q^jâ„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)^s	\\
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}q^jâ„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)
				q^{js}ï¼ˆâ„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s+â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^sï¼‰	\\
			&	=q^{j+js}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^{1+s}
				+âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^sï¼‰.
		\end{align*}
		The inequality rewrites the $s$th power of $â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)$.
		Then the inner sum of the E-null function morphs as follows
		\begin{align*}
			âˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)^{1+s}
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)q^{j+js}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^{1+s}	\\*
			&\kern8em	+âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^sï¼‰	\\
			&	=q^{j+js}âˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}
				â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^{1+s}	\tag{diagonal arc}\\
			&	+q^{j+js}âˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}
				â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s.	\tag{off-diagonal arc}
		\end{align*}
		The inequality rewrites the $(s+1)$th power of $â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)$.
		Divide and conquer---the inner sum of
		the double-stroke E-null function is split into two arcs as labeled.
		
		The diagonal arc is exactly
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			q^{j+js}âˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^{1+s}
				=q^{j+js}Ã·1{q^j}âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^{1+s}	\\
			&	=q^{js}\!âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_X(x_1^â„“)^{1+s}â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œx_1^â„“)^{1+s}%%%%LB
				=q^{js-â„“s}\!âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_X(x_1^â„“)â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œx_1^â„“)^{1+s}.%%%%LB
		\end{align*}
		The off-diagonal arc is
		\begin{align*}
			&	q^{j+js}âˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆK}
				â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s	\\
			&\quad	=q^{j+js}âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)
				âˆ‘_{Kâˆ‹x_1^â„“}â„™_ğ•‚(K)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s.
		\end{align*}
		The inner sum is loosened to
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			âˆ‘_{Kâˆ‹x_1^â„“}â„™_ğ•‚(K)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s
				=Ã·1{q^j}âˆ‘_{Kâˆ‹x_1^â„“}â„™_{ğ•‚|X}(Kï½œx_1^â„“)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s	\\
			&	â‰¤Ã·1{q^j}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{Kâˆ‹x_1^â„“}â„™_{ğ•‚|X}(Kï½œx_1^â„“)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰^s	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^j}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{Kâˆ‹x_1^â„“}â„™_{ğ•‚|X}(Kï½œx_1^â„“)
				âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“â‰ Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆK}â„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰^s	\\
			&	=Ã·1{q^j}ï¼ˆÃ·{q^{â„“-j}-1}{q^â„“-1}âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“â‰ Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰^s
				â‰¤Ã·1{q^{j+js}}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_1^â„“â‰ Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰^s
		\end{align*}
		The last equality counts the multiplicity of $Î¾_1^â„“$. %%% GL action 2-tr
		So the off-diagonal arc is loosened to
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			q^{j+js}âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)
				âˆ‘_{Kâˆ‹x_1^â„“}â„™_ğ•‚(K)â„™_{XY}(Kï¼¼x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)^s	\\
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)
				ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_1^â„“â‰ Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰^s	\\
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)
				ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{Î¾_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(Î¾_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)ï¼‰^s	\\
			&	=âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_{XY}(x_1^â„“,y_1^â„“)â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)^s
				=â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)^s = â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)^{1+s}.
		\end{align*}
		
		Both the diagonal and off-diagonal arcs being conquered,
		merge them and raise to the $(1+t)$-th power.
		The summand for any fixed $y_1^â„“$ in
		the definition of the double-stroke E-null function is
		\begin{align*}
			\quad&\kern-2em
			ï¼ˆâˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t}
				=(â€ off-diagonalâ€ +â€ diagonalâ€ )^{1+t}	\\
			&	â‰¤â€ off-diagonalâ€ ^{1+t}+â€ diagonalâ€ ^{1+t}
				â‰¤ï¼ˆâ„™_Y(y_1^â„“)^{1+s}ï¼‰^{1+t}+â€ diagonalâ€ ^{1+t}	\\
			&	=â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)+â€ diagonalâ€ ^{1+t}
				â‰¤â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)+ï¼ˆq^{js-â„“s}âˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_X(x_1^â„“)
				â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œx_1^â„“)^{1+s}ï¼‰^{1+t}	\\
			&	=â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)+q^{â„“t-jt}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}â„™_X(x_1^â„“)
				â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œx_1^â„“)^{1+s}ï¼‰^{1+t}
		\end{align*}
		The first equality divides.
		The next inequality applies the sub-additivity
		of $(1+t)$th power (note that $t<0$).
		We can finally bound the double-stroke E-null function per se:
		\begin{align*}
			\exp(-ğ”¼_0(t))
			&	=âˆ‘_{y_1^â„“}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_Kâ„™_ğ•‚(K)â„™_{Y|ğ•‚}(y_1^â„“ï½œK)^Ã·1{1+t}ï¼‰^{1+t}	\\
			&	â‰¤âˆ‘_{y_1^â„“}â„™_Y(y_1^â„“)+q^{â„“t-jt}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}
				â„™_X(x_1^â„“)â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œx_1^â„“)^{1+s}ï¼‰^{1+t}	\\
			&	=1+q^{â„“t-jt}âˆ‘_{y_1^â„“}ï¼ˆâˆ‘_{x_1^â„“âˆˆğ”½_q^â„“}
				â„™_X(x_1^â„“)â„™_{Y|X}(y_1^â„“ï½œx_1^â„“)^{1+s}ï¼‰^{1+t}	\\
			&	=1+q^{â„“t-jt}\exp(-(\emph{the E-null function of }W^â„“)(t))	\\
			&	=1+q^{â„“t-jt}\exp(-â„“\Eo(t)).
		\end{align*}
		
		All efforts we spent on bounding $I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“)$ are for three creeds:
		First, it demonstrates that Gallager's bounds via E-null functions
		(which behaves like cumulant generating functions)
		is a powerful tool that can be useful to the dual case.
		Second, it fits the paradigm that solving the primary (noisy channel)
		and the dual (wiretap channel) problems as a whole
		is easier than solving the primary problem alone.
		Third, the universal quadratic bound can be used
		to further bound the E-null function.
		
		We infer that
		\begin{align*}
			ğ”¼I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)
			&	=I_e(ğ•‚ï¼›Y_1^â„“)=ğ”¼_0'(0)â‰¤Ã·1tğ”¼_0(t) = Ã·1{-t}ã‘ï¼ˆ\exp(-ğ”¼_0(t))ï¼‰	\\
			&	â‰¤Ã·1{-t}ã‘ï¼ˆ1+q^{â„“t-jt}\exp(-â„“\Eo(t))ï¼‰
				<Ã·1{-t}q^{â„“t-jt}\exp(-â„“\Eo(t))	\\
			&	=\exp(-ã‘(-t)+(â„“-j)tã‘q-â„“\Eo(t)).
		\end{align*}
		Recall the universal quadratic bound $\Eo(t)â‰¥I(W)tã‘q-t^2ã‘(q)^2$
		as stated in \cref{lem:quadratic} and used in the previous subsection.
		But this time $-2/5â‰¤t<0$.
		We obtain that the exponent is
		\begin{align*}
			\qquad&\kern-2em
			-ã‘(-t)+(â„“-j)tã‘q-â„“\Eo(t)	\\
			&	=-ã‘(-t)+(â„“-H(W)â„“+â„“^{1/2+Î±})tã‘q-â„“\Eo(t)	\\
			&	=-ã‘(-t)+(I(W)â„“+â„“^{1/2+Î±})tã‘q-â„“\Eo(t)	\\
			&	â‰¤-ã‘(-t)+(I(W)â„“+â„“^{Ã·12+Î±})tã‘q-â„“(I(W)tã‘q-t^2ã‘(q)^2)	\\
			&	=-ã‘(-t)+(â„“tã‘q+â„“^{1/2+Î±})tã‘q	\\
			\shortintertext{(redeem the inequality at $t=-â„“^{-1/2+Î±}/2ã‘q$)}
			&	â†¦-ã‘ï¼ˆÃ·{â„“^{-1/2+Î±}}{2ã‘q}ï¼‰
				-ï¼ˆ-Ã·{â„“â„“^{-1/2+Î±}}2+â„“^{1/2+Î±}ï¼‰Ã·{â„“^{-1/2+Î±}}2	\\
			&	=Ã·{ã‘â„“}2-Î±ã‘â„“+ã‘2+ã‘(ã‘q)-Ã·{â„“^{2Î±}}4	\\
			&	=Ã·{ã‘â„“}2-ã‘(ã‘â„“)+ã‘2+ã‘(ã‘q)-Ã·{â„“^{2ã‘(ã‘â„“)/ã‘â„“}}4	\\
			&	<Ã·{ã‘â„“}2+ã‘(ã‘q)-Ã·{ã‘(â„“)Â²}4.
		\end{align*}
		The first inequality uses $â„“-j=â„“-H(W)â„“+â„“^{1/2+Î±}$.
		The last inequality uses the assumption $â„“>e^2$.
		With the last line we conclude that $ğ”¼I_e(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)
			<\exp(ã‘(â„“)/2+ã‘ã‘q-ã‘(â„“)^2/4)=â„“^{1/2-ã‘(â„“)/4}ã‘q$.
		Switch back to the base-$q$ mutual information
		$ğ”¼I(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)<â„“^{1/2-ã‘(â„“)/4}$.
		
		We now reject kernels $ğ”¾$ such that $I(U_1^jï¼›Y_1^â„“ï½œğ”¾)â‰¥â„“^{1/2-ã‘(â„“)/5}$.
		By Markov's inequality, the opposite direction ($<$) holds
		with probability $1-â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/20}$ because $1/5+1/20=1/4$.
		Plug this upper bound into $h$.
		The left-hand side of \cref{ine:eve-block} is less than
		\begin{align*}
			jhï¼ˆÃ·1jâ„“^{1/2-ã‘(â„“)/5}ï¼‰
			&	=jj^{-Î±}â„“^{Î±/2-Î±ã‘(â„“)/5}<â„“^{1-Î±}â„“^{Î±/2-Î±ã‘(â„“)/5}	\\
			&	=â„“^{1-Î±/2-Î±ã‘(â„“)/5}=â„“ã‘(â„“)^{-1/2-ã‘(â„“)/5}.
		\end{align*}
		The inequality uses that the left-hand side
		increases monotonically in $j$ and $jâ‰”H(W)â„“-â„“^{1/2+Î±}<â„“$.
		The quantity at the end of the inequalities decays to $0$
		as $â„“â†’âˆ$, so eventually it becomes less than $â„“^{1/2+Î±}$,
		the right-hand side of \cref{ine:eve-block}.
		This proves that \cref{ine:eve} holds with
		failing probability $â„“^{-ã‘(â„“)/20}$ as soon as $â„“$ is large enough. % 20
		
		The lower bound on $â„“$ in the statement of \cref{lem:typical-HH} is large
		enough, hence \cref{ine:eve}, the second half of \cref{lem:typical-HH} settled.
		That means the proof of the whole \cref{thm:typical-CLT} is complete.
	\end{proof}
	
	We just finished the last piece of the proof of \cref{thm:typical-CLT},
	which states that random kernels possesses good $Ï±$ with high probability.
	The next section combines this fact with the coset distance profile $âŒˆjÂ²/â„“âŒ‰$
	to conclude that low-complexity nearly-optimal polar codes exist when $Ï€+2Ïâ†’1$.

\section{Chapter Conclusion}\label{sec:signify}

	\Cref{thm:typical-LDP} shows that, with high probability,
	a random kernel enjoys coset distance profile $D_Zï¼·jâ‰¥âŒˆjÂ²/â„“âŒ‰$.
	And its dual $D_Sï¼·{â„“-j+1}â‰¥âŒˆjÂ²/â„“âŒ‰$ is immediate.
	\Cref{thm:typical-CLT} shows that, with high probability, a random kernel enjoys
	eigenvalue $â„“^{-1/2+4Î±}$, which means $Ï=1/2-4Î±$, where $Î±â‰”ã‘â„“/ã‘(ã‘â„“)$.
	Now, Does any pair $(Ï€,Ï)$ lying under the line $Ï€+2Ï=1$
	lie to the left of the convex envelope of
	$(0,1/2-4Î±)$ and the CramÃ©r function of $ã’_â„“âŒˆğ˜‘â‚Â²/â„“âŒ‰$ for some large $â„“$?
	
	Let us not go all the way down to a derivation
	of the CramÃ©r function of $ã’_â„“âŒˆğ˜‘â‚Â²/â„“âŒ‰$;
	a one-sided bound suffices.
	Consider the moment generating function evaluated at slope $-1/2$
	(that is the slope of $Ï€+2Ï=1$):
	\begin{align*}
		\qquad&\kern-2em
		ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹_1^{-1/2}]
			=Ã·1{â„“}âˆ‘_{j=1}^â„“ï¼ˆ\Bigl\lceilÃ·{jÂ²}{3â„“}\Bigr\rceilï¼‰^{-1/2}
			<Ã·1{â„“}âˆ‘_{j=1}^{âŒŠâˆš{3â„“}âŒ‹}1^{-1/2}
			+Ã·1{â„“}âˆ‘_{j=âŒŠâˆš{3â„“}âŒ‹+1}^â„“ï¼ˆÃ·{jÂ²}{3â„“}ï¼‰^{-1/2}	\\
		&	<Ã·1{â„“}âˆš{3â„“}+Ã·1{â„“}âˆš{3â„“}âˆ«_{âˆš{3â„“}}^â„“Ã·{\diff j}j
			=âˆš{3â„“}+Ã·{âˆš3}{âˆšâ„“}ã‘j\Bigr\rvert_{âˆš{3â„“}}^â„“<âˆš{3â„“}+âˆš{3â„“}ã‘â„“	\\
		&	=â„“^{-1/2}+2â„“^{-1/2+Î±}<4â„“^{-1/2+Î±}<â„“^{-1/2+2Î±}.
	\end{align*}
	So the cumulant generating function is bounded as
	\[ğ˜’(-1/2)=ã’_â„“ğ˜Œ[ğ˜‹_1^{-1/2}]<-1/2+2Î±.\]
	The CramÃ©r function as a supremum is then bounded by
	\[ğ˜“(s)â‰¥sÂ·(-1/2)-ğ˜’(-1/2)â‰¥-s/2+1/2-2Î±.\]
	
	The convex envelope of $(0,1/2-4Î±)$ and the segment $Ï=-Ï€/2+1/2-2Î±$
	(note that only the part with $Ïâ‰¥0$ counts)
	is a straight line connecting $(0,1/2-4Î±)$ and $(1-4Î±,0)$.
	As $â„“$ goes to infinity, $Î±$ goes to $0$, hence the convex envelope
	reveals the right triangle $(0,1/2)$--$(0,0)$--$(1,0)$.
	
	By \cref{cha:general}, any $(Ï€,Ï)$ in this right triangle
	is realizable by some polar codes, with complexity $O(Nã’N)$.
	Let me state the full theorem here.
	
	\begin{thm}[Hypotenuse]
		Fix a $q$-ary channel $W$.
		Fix exponents $Ï€+2Ï<1$.
		Then there exists a large $â„“$ and an amoebic kernel
		(a strategy to assign kernels to synthetic channels) $ğ’¢$ such that
		\begin{gather*}
			ğ˜—\{ğ˜¡_n<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})\}>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ïn},	\\
			ğ˜—\{ğ˜š_n<\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})\}>H(W)-â„“^{-Ïn}		
		\end{gather*}
		for large $n$.
	\end{thm}
	
	Recall that at the beginning of \cref{cha:general}, I have demonstrated
	how to reduce arbitrary input alphabet to prime-power input alphabet.
	Hence the theorem applies to all DMCs, and we have reached
	the holy grail at the beginning of the chapter.
	
	\begin{cor}[Random codes' durability, polar codes' simplicity]\label{cor:holy}
		Over any discrete memoryless channel, for any constants $Ï€,Ï>0$ such that
		$\pi+2\rho<1$, there is a series of error correcting codes with
		block length $N$ approaching infinity,
		block error probability $\exp(-N^Ï€)$,
		code rate $N^{-Ï}$ less than the channel capacity,
		and encoding and decoding complexity $O(Nã’N)$ per code block.
	\end{cor}
	
	This is the second-moment paradigm code that was promised in the abstract.
	\Cref{tab:refarray} compares this corollary to past works.
	The same can be stated for lossless compression and lossy compression.

		requiring $Ï€$ or $Ï$ or both to be positive.
	}\label{tab:refarray}

	\begin{cor}[Very good code for lossless compression]
		For any lossless compression problem, for any constants $Ï€,Ï>0$ such that
		$\pi+2\rho<1$, there is a series of source codes with
		block length $N$ approaching infinity,
		block error probability $\exp(-N^Ï€)$,
		code rate $N^{-Ï}$ plus the conditional entropy,
		and encoding and decoding complexity $O(Nã’N)$ per code block.
	\end{cor}
	
	\begin{cor}[Very good code for lossy compression]
		For any lossy compression problem, for any constants $Ï€,Ï>0$ such that
		$\pi+2\rho<1$, there is a series of source codes with
		block length $N$ approaching infinity,
		block error probability $\exp(-N^Ï€)$,
		code rate $N^{-Ï}$ plus the test channel capacity,
		and encoding and decoding complexity $O(Nã’N)$ per code block.
	\end{cor}
	
	The next chapter prunes.



\chapter{Joint Pruning and Kerneling}\label{cha:joint}

	\dropcap
	Combination of two techniques, when executed properly,
	gives results that inherit advantages from the two individual techniques.
	In this chapter, I want to combine pruning from \cref{cha:prune}
	and random dynamic kerneling from \cref{cha:random}, and will verify that
	the chimera codes have the optimal gap to capacity and log-logarithmic complexity.
	
	Let me elaborate.
	First, we do not expect the resulting codes to have elpin error,
	that is, block error probability $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$.
	This is because we set the threshold $Î¸â‰”N^{-2}$
	and prune the channel tree whenever $ğ˜¡_m$ reaches $Î¸$.
	Should we wait for $ğ˜¡_m$ to become as small as $Î¸â‰”\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$,
	pruning will not take place at $O(ã’n)$ depth,
	and there will be little to no savings on EU--DU pairs.
	In conclusion, there is a conflict between elpin error and log-logarithmic
	complexity, and I will give up the optimal decay of error to favor low complexity.
	
	That being said, theer is no obvious conflict between
	complexity and code rate, and it is very likely that we can retain
	both from \cref{cha:prune} and \cref{cha:random}, respectively.
	Hence this constituents the goal for this section---Construct error correcting codes
	with gap to capacity close to $N^{-1/2}$, encoding and decoding complexity
	$O(Nã’(ã’N))$ per block, and block error probability as small as possible.

\section{Toolbox Checklist}

	From \cref{cha:random}, it is possible to construct a channel process $\{ğ˜_m\}$
	such that, for any $Ï€+2Ï<1$ and large $m$ (depending on $Ï€,Ï$),
	\begin{gather*}
		ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜¡_m<e^{-â„“^{Ï€m}}ï½>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ïm},	\label{ine:Z-elpin}\\
		ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜š_m<e^{-â„“^{Ï€m}}ï½>H(W)-â„“^{-Ïm}.		\label{ine:S-elpin}
	\end{gather*}
	From \cref{cha:general}, we know that every ergodic kernel
	assumes a positive $Ï±$, so every kernel assumes at least
	a pair $Ï€,Ï>0$ such that \cref{ine:Z-elpin,ine:S-elpin} hold.
	
	From \cref{cha:prune}, we can set a threshold $Î¸$, which serves two purposes:
	One, we prune the channel tree at the point where $ğ˜¡_m$ or $ğ˜š_m$ falls below $Î¸$.
	This in turn defines the stopping time
	\[ğ˜´â‰”nâˆ§\min\{m:\min(ï¼º(ğ˜_m),ï¼³(ğ˜_m))<Î¸\}.\]
	Two, we collect in $ğ’¥$ indices that point to synthetic channels $ğ˜_m$ whose
	$ï¼º$-parameter reaches $Î¸$, which is the cause why $ğ˜´$ is set to this depth $m$.
	
	For the asymmetric case, $Î¸$ defines the stopping time
	\[ğ˜´â‰”nâˆ§\min\left\{m:\mat{
		\min(ï¼º(ğ˜_m),ï¼³(ğ˜_m))<Î¸â€  andâ€ 	\\
		\min(ï¼º(ğ˜˜_m),ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m))<Î¸
	}\right\}.\]
	$ğ’¥$ will then collect indices pointing
	to $ğ˜_m$ whose $ï¼º$-value reaches $Î¸$ and to $ğ˜˜_m$ whose $ï¼³$-value reaches $Î¸$.
	To put it differently, $ğ’¥$ is the event where both $ï¼º(ğ˜_ğ˜´),ï¼³(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)<Î¸$.
	
	$Î¸$ and $ğ˜´$ determine a code. 
	We have three lemmas that generalize
	\cref{lem:prune-C,lem:prune-R,lem:prune-P} to arbitrary matrix kernels.
	Their proofs are straightforward (perhaps tautological) and will be sketchy.
	
	\begin{lem}[Complexity in terms of $ğ˜´$]
		The encoding and decoding complexity is
		$O(ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´])$ per channel usage, or $O(Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜´])$ per code block.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Similar to \cref{lem:prune-C}, I claim without a proof that
		the encoding and decoding complexity is proportional to  the number of
		EU--DU devices in the circuit and to the number of synthetic channels
		(multiplicity included) that undergo channel transformation.
		
		Since a trajectory $ğ˜â‚€,ğ˜â‚â€¦ğ˜_ğ˜´$ undergoes the transformation $ğ˜´$ times,
		the average number of transformations is $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]$,
		and hence the total number of transformations is $Nğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]$.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{lem}[$R$ in terms of $ğ’¥$]
		The code rate is $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}$, or $ğ˜—(ğ’¥)$ for short.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Similar to \cref{lem:prune-R}, I claim without a proof that
		the code rate is the density of naked pins that are selected in $ğ’¥$.
		
		Every pair of naked pins possesses probability measure $1/N$
		because there are always $N$ pairs of naked pins.
		Every synthetic channel $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ assumes $N/2^ğ˜´$ copies in the circuit,
		hence possesses probability measure $2^{-ğ˜´}$.
		All indices $ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´$ in $ğ’¥$ possesses probability measure $2^{-m}$,
		which coincides with the measure of pins.
		Thus the density of selected pins is $ğ˜—\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}$.
	\end{proof}
	
	\begin{lem}[$ï¼°$ in terms of $ğ’¥$]
		The block error probability is
		$Nğ˜Œ[ï¼°(ğ˜_ğ˜´)Â·ğ˜\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}]â‰¤qNÎ¸$ for the symmetric case.
		For the asymmetric case, the block error probability is
		$Nğ˜Œ[ï¼°(ğ˜_ğ˜´)Â·ğ˜\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}]â‰¤qNÎ¸+Nğ˜Œ[T(ğ˜_ğ˜´)Â·ğ˜\{ğ˜‘â‚^ğ˜´âˆˆğ’¥\}]$,
		witch is at most $3qNÎ¸$ in total.
	\end{lem}
	
	\begin{proof}
		Similar to \cref{lem:prune-P}, I claim without a proof that,
		for the symmetric case, the block error probability is the sum of
		the $ï¼°$-values of the $ğ˜_ğ˜´$ in $ğ’¥$ (multiplicity included).
		And for the symmetric case, the block error probability is the said sum
		plus the total of the $T$-values of the $ğ˜˜_ğ˜´$ in $ğ’¥$ (multiplicity included).
		
		For the sum of $ï¼°(ğ˜_ğ˜´)$, we learn from \cref{lem:PvsZ}
		that $ï¼°(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¤qZ(ğ˜_ğ˜´)/2â‰¤qï¼º(ğ˜_ğ˜´)/2â‰¤qÎ¸/2$.
		Hence a sum of at most $N$ items, each at most $qÎ¸/2$, is at most $qNÎ¸$.
		
		For the sum of $T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)$, we learn form \cref{lem:PvsT} that
		\begin{align*}
			T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)
			&	â‰¤Ã·{2(q-1)}q-Ã·2qï¼ˆ(q-1)qï¼°(W)-(q-1)(q-2)ï¼‰	\\
			&	=Ã·{2(q-1)}qï¼ˆ1-\(qï¼°(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)-(q-2)\)ï¼‰=Ã·{2(q-1)Â²}qï¼ˆ1-Ã·q{q-1}ï¼°(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)ï¼‰
			\shortintertext{and then from \cref{lem:PvsS} that}
			&	â‰¤Ã·{2(q-1)Â²}qS(ğ˜˜_n)â‰¤2qS(ğ˜˜_n)â‰¤2qï¼³(ğ˜˜_n)â‰¤2qÎ¸.
		\end{align*}
		Hence a sum of at most $N$ items, each at most $2qÎ¸$, is at most $2qNÎ¸$.
		The contributions of $ï¼°(ğ˜_ğ˜´)$ and $T(ğ˜˜_n)$ amount to $3qNÎ¸$;
		that finishes the proof.
	\end{proof}
	
	Now I can state and prove the main theorem in this chapter.
	My contribution is twofold.
	First contribution:
	If you insist on using a certain matrix $G$ to construct polar codes,
	then either $G$ is not ergodic (in which case there is no polarization at all),
	or you can construct log-logarithm codes with some positive $Ï$.
	Second contribution:
	If you allow dynamic kerneling with very large $â„“$, then you can construct
	log-logarithm codes whose $Ï$ is arbitrarily close to $1/2$, the optimal exponent.

\section{Log-logarithmic Codes}

	The first case I want to discuss here is when a fixed kernel $G$ is selected prior.
	In this case, the best $Ï$ we can hope for
	is the $Ï±$ in the eigen/en23/een13 behavior of $G$.
	(After all, pruning should not improve the extent of polarization.)
	
	\begin{thm}[Log-log for asymmetric $q$-ary]\label{thm:ll-R-P}
		Fix a $q$-ary channel $W$.
		Fix a kernel $Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$ and a pair $(Ï€,Ï)$
		that satisfy the two-sided elpin behavior
		\begin{gather*}
			ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜¡_m<e^{-â„“^{Ï€m}}ï½>1-H(W)-â„“^{-Ïm+o(m)},	\\
			ğ˜—ï½›ğ˜š_m<e^{-â„“^{Ï€m}}ï½>H(W)-â„“^{-Ïm+o(m)}.	
		\end{gather*}
		Then pruning the channel tree with threshold $Î¸â‰”1/3qNÂ²$ yields
		\begin{gather*}
			ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]=O(ã’(ã’N)),							\\
			ğ˜—(ğ’¥)=I(W)-N^{-Ï+o(1)},					\\
			Nğ˜Œ[ï¼°(ğ˜_ğ˜´)Â·ğ˜(ğ’¥)]+Nğ˜Œ[T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)Â·ğ˜(ğ’¥)]â‰¤1/N.	
		\end{gather*}
	\end{thm}
	
	\begin{proof}
		To estimate $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]=âˆ‘_{m=1}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{ğ˜´>m\}$, we mimic \cref{thm:actual-C}.
		Consider small $m$ and large $m$.
		Those with $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€m})â‰¥Î¸$ are called small $m$.
		Those with $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€m})<Î¸$ are called large $m$.
		For small $m$, we do not expect decent polarization and assume $ğ˜´â‰¥m$.
		That is, we upper bound $ğ˜—\{s>m\}â‰¤1$ by a pessimistic value.
		For large $m$, we argue that
		\begin{align*}
			\quad&\kern-1em
			ğ˜—\{s>m\}
				â‰¤ğ˜—\{ï¼º(ğ˜_m)âˆ¨ï¼³(ğ˜_m))â‰¥Î¸â€  or â€ ï¼º(ğ˜˜_m)âˆ¨ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)â‰¥Î¸\}	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜—\{\max(ï¼º(ğ˜_m),ï¼³(ğ˜_m))â‰¥Î¸\}+ğ˜—\{\max(ï¼º(ğ˜˜_m),ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m))â‰¥Î¸\}	\\
			&	â‰¤ğ˜—ï½›ï¼º(ğ˜_m)âˆ¨ï¼º(ğ˜_m)â‰¥e^{-â„“^{Ï€m}}ï½
				+ğ˜—ï½›ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)âˆ¨ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)â‰¥e^{-â„“^{Ï€m}}ï½	\\
			&	â‰¤2â„“^{-Ïm+o(m)}+2â„“^{-Ïm+o(m)}=â„“^{-Ïm+o(m)}.
		\end{align*}
		As a result, the complexity is
		\begin{align*}
			ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]
			&	=âˆ‘_{m=0}^{n-1}ğ˜—\{ğ˜´>m\}=\#\{â€ small â€ m\}+âˆ‘_{â€ large mâ€ }â„“^{-Ïm+o(m)}	\\
			&	=O(ã’n)+O(1)=O(ã’(ã’N)).
		\end{align*}
		Here we use the fact that the number of small $m$'s is the root of the equation
		$\exp(-â„“^{Ï€m})=Î¸=1/3qNÂ²=1/3qâ„“^{2n}$, which is $ã’(n)$ (note that $N=â„“^n$).
		
		To estimate $R=ğ˜—(ğ’¥)$, we mimic \cref{thm:actual-R}.
		It is the same as estimating the frequency that
		$ğ˜´$ is set to $m$ due to $ï¼º(ğ˜_m)<Î¸$ and $ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)<Î¸$.
		This frequency is
		\begin{align*}
			R
			&	=ğ˜—(ğ’¥)=ğ˜—\{ï¼º(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¤Î¸â€  and â€ ï¼³(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)â‰¤Î¸\}	\\
			&	â‰¥ğ˜—\{ï¼º(ğ˜_m)â†’0â€  and â€ ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0\}	\\*
			&\kern4em	-ğ˜—\{ï¼º(ğ˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ ï¼º(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¥Î¸\}	\\*
			&\kern4em	-ğ˜—\{ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ ï¼³(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)â‰¥Î¸\}	\\
			&	â‰¥I(W)-ğ˜—\{ï¼º(ğ˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ ï¼³(ğ˜_ğ˜´)<Î¸\}	\\*
			&\kern4em	-ğ˜—\{ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ ï¼º(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)<Î¸\}-ğ˜—\{ğ˜´=n\}.
		\end{align*}
		Here we use the fact that if $ï¼º(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¥Î¸$, then $ğ˜´$ is set to its current value
		due to $ï¼³(ğ˜_ğ˜´)<Î¸$ or, otherwise, due to hitting $n$.
		It remains to estimate the three minus terms on the right-hand side.
		
		To bound $ğ˜—\{ï¼º(ğ˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ ï¼³(ğ˜_ğ˜´)<Î¸\}$, notice that
		$1-H(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¤qÂ³âˆš{ï¼³(ğ˜_ğ˜´)}<qÂ³/Nâˆš{3q}â‰¤N^{-1+o(1)}$.
		Now the probability that $H(ğ˜_m)â†’0$ given $H(ğ˜_ğ˜´)â‰¥1-N^{-1+o(1)}$
		is $N^{-1+o(1)}$ by the martingale property.
		For a similar reason, $ğ˜—\{ï¼³(ğ˜˜_m)â†’0â€  but â€ ï¼º(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)<Î¸\}$ is no greater than
		the probability that $H(ğ˜˜_m)â†’1$ given $H(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)<N^{-1+o(1)}$,
		which is $N^{-1+o(1)}$ by the martingale property again.
		Last is to bound $ğ˜—\{ğ˜´=n\}$, but that is just $ğ˜—\{ğ˜´>n-1\}-ğ˜—\{ğ˜´>n\}$,
		and is thus $â„“^{-Ï(n-1)-o(n-1)}=N^{-Ï+o(1)}$.
		We conclude
		\[Râ‰¥I(W)-N^{-1+o(1)}-N^{-1+o(1)}-N^{-Ï+o(1)}=I(W)-N^{-Ï+o(1)}.\]
		
		The bound $Nğ˜Œ[ï¼°(ğ˜_ğ˜´)Â·ğ˜(ğ’¥)]+Nğ˜Œ[T(ğ˜˜_ğ˜´)Â·ğ˜(ğ’¥)]â‰¤1/N$ is tautological.
		And the proof ends here.
	\end{proof}
	
	When allowing dynamic kerneling, random or not, the proof of the last theorem
	does not change---we still set a threshold $Î¸$ and prune the channel tree by $Î¸$.
	The only difference is that $Ï$ can now be arbitrarily close to $1/2$.
	
	To put it another way, like I once commented under \cref{lem:tensor}, there is
	no point to use a large kernel without knowing that it has a good $(Ï€,Ï)$ pair.
	So \cref{thm:ll-R-P} mainly has three use cases:
	(a)	To estimate the behavior of a minimalist kernel such as $\locl$,
	(b)	to estimate the behavior of a larger kernel with a bound on $Ï$, and
	(c)	to estimate the behavior of random dynamic kerenling.
	That leads to the following corollary.
	
	\begin{cor}[Log-log code for DMC]
		Given any $q$-ary DMC (presumably with virtual symbols) and any ergodic kernel
		$Gâˆˆğ”½_q^{â„“Ã—â„“}$, pruned polar coding achieves
		encoding and decoding complexity $O(ã’(ã’N))$ per channel usage,
		block error probability $1/N$,
		and code rate $1/N^Ï$ less than the channel .
		Here, $Ï$ is a number guaranteed to be positive, lower bounded
		if you know more about the eigen/\AB en23/\AB een13/\AB eplin behavior of $G$,
		and very close to $1/2$ as $â„“â†’âˆ$ if you allow dynamic kerneling.
	\end{cor}
	
	The same can be stated regarding lossless and lossy compression, and is omitted.
	
	The next section discusses a continuous trade-off between $ï¼°$ and complexity.


\section{Error--Complexity Trade-off}

	In the proof of \cref{thm:ll-R-P}, the complexity $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]$ and $Î¸=Î¸(n)$ are linked by
	the equation $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€m})=Î¸$ that determines how many $m$'s are small.
	The root thereof $m=m(Î¸)=m(Î¸(n))$ will be the complexity.
	We may as well alter $Î¸(n)$ and obtain a different error--complexity pair.
	The only restriction is $â„“^{-2n}â‰ªÎ¸(n)â‰ª\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$
	to avoid the necessity to deal with special/edge cases.
	
	\begin{cor}[Continuous error--complexity trade-off]\label{thm:tradeoff}
		Let $Î¸(n)$ be an asymptote lying between $â„“^{-2n}$ and $\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$.
		Then pruned polar coding achieves complexity $O(ã’\abs{ã’Î¸(n)})$
		per channel usage and block error probability $â„“^nÎ¸(n).$
	\end{cor}
	
	In particular, if $Î¸(n)â‰”\exp(-â„“^{Ï€n})$, then the complexity is
	$O(ã’\abs{ã’Î¸(n)})=O(ã’\abs{â„“^{Ï€m}})=O(n)=O(ã’N)$.
	This restores the case where one insists on retaining the $\exp(-N^Ï€)$ error
	while pruning, which only gives you constant-scalar improvement in complexity.
	In fact, we can almost prove this tight:
	The complexity, $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]$, is the (average) number
	of transformations a trajectory $ğ˜_n$ undergoes.
	Since each transformation, at best, raises $ï¼º(ğ˜_n)$ to the power of $â„“$,
	you need $ã’_â„“(ã’_{ï¼º(W)}Î¸)$ transformations to lower $ï¼º(ğ˜_n)$ to $Î¸$.
	That implies that $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]â‰¥ã’_â„“(ã’_{ï¼º(W)}Î¸)$.
	
	On the other hand, if $Î¸(n)â‰”\exp(-n^Ï„)$ for some very large $Ï„>0$,
	then $ğ˜Œ[ğ˜´]=O(ã’\abs{ã’Î¸(n)})=O(ã’\abs{-n^Ï„})=O(Ï„ã’(ã’N))$.
	If $Ï„$ is a constant despite of being very large,
	then the complexity is still $O(ã’(ã’N))$.
	This is the complexity paradigm code that was promised in the abstract.
	\Cref{tab:complex} compare this result and \cref{cor:holy} to past works.

\begin{table}
	\caption{
		A comparison concerning the error--rate--complexity asymptotes
		of some well-known capacity-achieving codes.
	}\label{tab:complex}
	\pgfplotstableread{
		Code			Error		Gap		Complexity	Channel	
		random			e^{-N^Ï€}	N^{-Ï}	\exp(N)		DMC

	]\tableComplex
